THE RED PAGE.
Albert Hanson’s exhibition of p lC tures and
sketches at Bridge-street, Sydney, is full of ex-
nlp^Q 68 de * terous ar t as a colourist. His
pleasant little pieces such as “The Land of
K.vpt and “In the Gulf of Suez” seem to have
acnieved exactly the right quality of light and
atmosphere. Through all Mr. Hanson’s work 1
indeed, runs a vein of natural charm which needs
only a little addition of intensity and power to
make hi a pictures really fine. And- hiHlack?
and-white is quite admirable.
* * * *
Apropos Aubrey Beardsley : There is a sketchv
uncritical notice of him in March Idler &
trated by a good portrait and many more examples
of his work than it was possible to give in Thk
Bulletin. Beardsley is represented as now dying
of consumption—at 23 years of age. He always
works by candlelight—“if I want to work in the
daytime I have to pull the blind down ar|d get
my candles in order before I begin.” (This ac-
cords with George Meredith's idea that the
brain does its best work in a dim light;
and the principle is correct, for glare,
like noise, absorbs cerebral energy and
makes concentration more difficult.) “Of course,
I have but one aim—the grotesque. If lam not
grotesque lam nothing. Apart from the gro-
tesque I suppose I may say that people like my
decorative work, and that I may claim to have
some command of line.” “ How many of our
young English impressionists know the difference
between a palette and a picture—save and except
Walter Sickert ? ” “Tomy mind there is nothing
so depressing as a Gothic cathedral. I hate to
have the sun shut out by the saints.” “ When an
Englishman has expressed his belief in the supre-
macy of Shakespeare amongst poets, he feels him-
self entirely excused from the general study of
literature. He also feels himself entirely excused
from tlm particular study of Shakespeare.”
“ Turner is only a rhetorician in paint. That is
why Buskin understood and liked him.”
♦
Roderic Quinn—born in Sydney, of Irish
parents—is 28 years old, with a weak chest.
Sydney air seems to suit the tubercle bacillus ad-
mirably ; and the local prevalence of phthisis and
phthisic tendencies —just now a current topic—
helps to sharpen wits and accentuate artistic ten-
dencies. For, in some not-understood way, the
consumptive constitution is allied with that con-
dition of the blood or brain most favorable to
imaginative achievement. As A. Mary F. Robin-
son says in her memoir of ‘ Emily Bronte,’ ‘ In-
sanity and genius stand on either side consump-
tion, its worse and better angels. Let none call
it impious or absurd to rank the greatest gift to
mankind as the occasional result of an inherited
tendency to tuberculous disease.’ Roderic Quinn’s
two brothers have literary tastes and are engaged
in literary pursuits, but with stronger chests
they have less imaginative power.
* * » *
When to a weak chest you add Celtic ancestry
you get a very promising artistic temperament.
Roderic Quinn’s mind transmutes all its impres-
sions poetically, with the peculiar Celtic bias
towards the uncanny and the weird. He runs
instinctively to rhetoric; and his rombustious,
periwig-pated prose sometimes reads stagey and
unreal. Thus his new book, ‘ Mostyn Stayne ’
(Geo. Robertson and Co.), opens—
The Mac Donnell Ranges lift purple peaks in a remote
region. They rise in a land of thirst, where white man’s
foot seldom falls.
No tourist visits them. Who essays the task oft re-
turns not. Yet to some they are ringed with fatal
allurement, such as whitens round the poles.
Jorkins, the Crown explorer, fell beneath their bewitch-
ment.
And so on. The staccato statement, the affected
phrasing—the ‘essays,’ the ‘oft returns not,’
the bewitchment’ in contrast with ‘Jorkins’
—grate on a cool ear and taste. And the worst
of it is that the author cannot always maintain his
style at the artificial Stevensonian level. When
his new wine gets into the old bottles the
bottles burst with disastrous results to the
reader. Phrases like ‘ one hand I inserted ’ (p. 62)
0,1 her incidental fighting having been done ’
(p. 69), give one a picture of a rude modern boy
thrusting a stick between the strutting i.ero’s legs
bringing him ingloriously to earth. These
lapses spoil some of the author’s most charm-
ing pictures. E.g. (p. 82)-‘I hid in shade,
waiting to glimpse [‘glimpse’ is irritating]
my love. She carne at last, attired [‘ attired ’
is awkward with ‘ bright ’ following] in bright
blue, flushed and beautiful as a tropic dawn. She
was tall and long of limb, and the curves of her
body were sweetened [delicious word !] by the
droop and cling of her robing. A music lurked in
her movement and her eyes ’’—what of her eyes *
her eyes [ O stale journalese !] sparkled with ex-
citement. Quinn was quite capable of inventing
fafled fcl him lraBe t 0 ° loBe the paBsaKß s but his art

connng.* But they, with fast-closed eyelids, murmured,
He lies, there is no dawn.’
Nevertheless, day broke.

A Plea for Andrew Lang.
Writes Frank Morton—
A recent Red Page paragraph seemed to join with
George Moore in rather depreciating Andrew Lang. I
differ. I decline resolutely to be convinced, for instance,
that A.L. “ has lost caste with the younger men.” (With
the youngest, possibly. That was to be expected.) For
Andrew Lang is one of the most characteristic and most
loveable figures in modern letters. Likely, too, that he
will prove one of the most permanently popular—always
admitting certain definite limitations.
* • » »
Andrew Lang has been an omnivorous reader, with a
remarkable faculty for the assimilation and reten-
tion of good things. He is almost uniquely versatile;
but in his versatility, or the evidences of it,
there lies no trace of the deadly “smartness” and the
wire-drawn “cleverness” of this tremendous day. A
preeminently kindly and sympathetic critic, always; a
romanticist of the innermost circle, now happily becom-
ing restricted ; an optimist of the optimists. No wonder
the youngest men, who have their intervals of insight, do
not always like him. The younger men, and others their
elders, value a man for what he is; the youngest moodily
condemn him because of what he fails to be. There lies a
great distinction.
* * * *
Andrew Lang is more than a dilettante, a me
dabbler in the pleasant trivialities of Letters,
understands English, and cau use it as few livin j
men have been able to do. A masterly stylis i,
with a style delightful in its limpid lucidity am
consistent brilliancy and firm dexterity of touc 1.
Further, ordinary ineffective translators are as plentif
to-day as New Women, and even a little more irritatin
Andrew Lang is one of the best and ablest. In th
adaptation of old French verse he stands almost alon
for he invariably preserves the form of the origins
Viewed at large, indeed, there is a piquant Gallic touc
in everything he does. But give him French verse, no ;
too modern—the verse, say, of Ronsard, or Villon, or D
Bellay, or (among the moderns) Murger—and you hav
him immediately and delightfully at home.
* * * *
Yet more frequently pleasing are some of Mr. Lang
original essays in French verse forms. For the real intr
duction of these forms into popular English, I believe h
divides the honors pretty equally with Austin Dob
son. The triolet, the rondeau, the rondel, the villanell
the alluring ballade, and the imposing and difficult chan ;
royal—these, and others of their family, are forms to b
thankful for, and their occasional presence in our litera
ture is largely due to Andrew Lang. He uses them
himself with admirable effect, although Austin Dobso
is his master here, and Oscar Wilde has written per
haps the finest English villanelle ; and the same daint
exotics have taken notable root in America.
* * * *
So one might proceed. From translations of French t
Original stuff in Gallic garb, and so to vert de
tpciiti (which is but seldom typically French
form, and only retains its soubriquet to keep aliv
the faot that it is not society verse), and agai
to what the Philistines persist in calling “mor
serious" work. Mr. Lang, says the Red Page, ha
“trifled along pleasant paths.” Precisely — pleasan
paths are well-fitted for the purpose, and to trifl
artistically and with zest is becoming a lost art. We ar
too prone, all of us, to sit with self-respectless reverenoe
at the feet of some prodigiously important person who i
eternally bent on doing something. In dense forgetful
ness of the possibility of pleasure in being something, we
are content all too frequently to leave the goodlier part
of our heritage unscanned. And we die with our one
idea ossified, still struggling fatuously to reform—some-
body else. ‘ What,’ says Thoreau (he no fool)—* what,
after all, does the practicalness of life amount to ? The
things immediate to be done are very trivial. 1 could
postpone them all to hear this locust sing. The most
glorious faot of our experience is not anything that we
have done or may hope to do, but a transient thought, or
vision, or dream which we have had.’ If Mr. Lang has
been a trifler, he has trifled in good company. With
Dyer and Drayton and Lovelace and Campion, and others
their congeners ; and with dozens of others more modern
and better known, whose names will readily suggest
themselves.
• * • .
Truth to tell, we seem to be menaced unceasingly by
stuff-and-sawdust Shibboleths of our own clumsy contri-
vance. We have come to think of Genius as something
necessarily frenzied and grave and stately and almost ter-
rible—and it is, whatever else it be, necessarily nothing
of the kind ; as something which finds readiest response
in the hearts of a fine fit few—and, as a rule, the self-
elected fine fit few are fatuously deaf to its appeal for
recognition, until the mass who are neither—God help
us!—“fine” nor “fit" cry out their great-throated
appreciation. To be vital to-day a thing (written thing)
needs to be “fresh”: no writing is laudable and ulti-
mately good if it be not “original.” These things being
so, Andrew Lang may never haye said anything
“vital,” but the Domain resounds to vital speech every
Sunday. Nevertheless, a great deal of his writing, and
the apparent tendency of the whole of it, has been vital
Practically all his stuff is, in effect, tonic, invigorating
eminently healthful. Even his newspaper-articles they
would fill volumes-are uniformly vivacious and meaty
tinged throughout with refreshing allusion-learning
which is cheerfully appetising. In his most play”
ful mood there is even something deeper and more
convincing than playfulness, in his most humorous pas-
sages something likely to prove more permanent than
,he upper average of contemporary humor. He has a
strongly sensuous vein in his clear lyrism, but with it
some easily-reeognisable share of the quality, difficult to
define, which John Addington Symonds, writing of
Edward Craoroft Lefroy, calls “ the spiritual apperception
of sensuous beauty.” A fine sense of the fitDessof things,
too : to borrow an acute phrase from somebody, he “ never
forgets that the lyre is a winged instrument.”
* * * *
In short, he is—Andrew Lang; and for the life of
me I cannot satisfactorily express the precise depth and
direction of my appreciation of him. He has, of course,
his limitations, his weaknesses, his foibles, his utiadmir-
able aspects : who has not? Primarily, he has written
too much, at too high pressure ; he has, in one notable
matter already touched on, elected (as some of us will
have it) to walk in darkness when clear light is abroad:
he must have the glamor of the ideal, and he dearly loves
the cheap splash and splutter of occasional lurid incident.
He believes (or says he believes) that literature is, first
and foremost, a light amusement—an enjoyable recrea-
tion and relief from reality—a something exquisitely
varied and comely designed pour passer le temps. He will
have no nearness to common life, the life that is—no
absolute assertion of the pains and penalties of it; litera-
ture, he says, is not for this. He would fain forget these
things, cloak them sedulously, push them out of sight;
If only the nepenthe be foaming there—the delightful
draught of dear forgetfulness, the outside of the cup may
take care of itself.’ Unspeakable heresy! Fancy
George Moore, the choleric, in face of such a confession 1
» * » *
Mr. Lang is whimsical in his tastes, and curiously
candid. He is unable (strange man!) to wax enthusiastic
regarding politics: he seems to think that that were
waste of time for him ; and time is a valuable commodity,
useful for the production of fairy-tales and other matters
of moment! He does not oare for Racine, nor for Words-
worth. The one is “ elegant ” ; the other (genius ad-
mitted)—uninteresting ! Zola irritates him ;as does that
very stupid person, W. Dean Howells: and yet “he is
a nephew of Voltaire, and a cousin of M. Jules Lemattre.”
He has his weaknesses: he is a wee bit intolerant of
people and schools he does not admire, but he is never
peevish or querulous ; and he is occasionally a little rash
in the expression of his appreciation of his friends ; he
went to almost ridiculous lengths in his early eulogies of
Rider Haggard. He combines with a keen sense of
the beautiful a quite remarkable indifference to Art and
Music and the Drama. Although he is genuinely learned,
he has long been an embodied irritation to certain of the
sensitive erudite. When he brings forth an idea he does
not make pother and dust enough. So clear and simple
so comprehensible is he, that he has even beer, suspected
of writing with an eye on the Gallery. Horrible!
* * *
So! I have said little or nothing of his classical works
-h.s scholarly and luminous translations, for instance, of
Homer and (better still) the Idylls of Theocritus ; nothing
Of his position and authority at certain great seats of
earning; nothing of his characteristically original studies
in Anthropology, and certain novel and disturbing con-
tentions appertaining thereto ; little, perhaps, that can
be profitable or interesting in any case. For one’s esti-
mate of the man is necessarily complicated by one’s
unavoidable affection for him. But his position is no
mean one, none the less ; and the Youngest will doubtless
cease their worrying by-and-bye. It does not count for
much anyhow. And nothing nowadays is serious enough
to matter. 6
TOUT FINIT PAR DES CHANSONS.
(Ballade en guise de Rondeau.)
AH ends in song ! Dame Nature toiled
In stellar space, by land, by sea,
And many a monstrous thing she spoiled,
And many another brought to be;
Strange brutes that sprawled, strange stars that flee
tlr flare the steadfast signs among t
What profit thence—to you or me?
All ends in song!
All ends in song 1 But Nature moiled
And brought forth Man, who deems him free
Who deems twas his own hand embroiled
The tangles of his destiny :
Who fashioned empires—who but he?—
Who fashioned gods, a motley throng ;
They fall, they fade by Time’s decree
All ends in song !
All ends in song ! We strive, are foiled,
Are broken-hearted—even we ;
Where that old sinful snake is coiled
We shake the knowledgeable tree,
We listen to the serpent’s plea,
“ As Bha, l ye know Right and Wrong ”
And this is all the mystery—
“ All ends in song! "
Envoy.
Muse, or in sooth or mockery,
Or brief of days, or lasting long,
Our love, or hate, or gloom, or glee
All ends in song !

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE LAND OF TIERS’ DREAMS.
“ And there shall t. we all soft delight
That shadowy thougnt can win.”
There lies a land, dear heart of mine,
Beyond the world afar,
B&yond the moon, sweet heart of mine,
Beyond each glimmering star.
It is a land of loving,
A land of loveliness,
For there doth sit enthroned
The lost sprite Happiness ;
And she hath banished Sorrow
To regions far away ;
There is no sad To-morrow,
But only sweet To-day !
It is a land of blisses ;
The air is soft with sighs
From lips that ache with kisses —
There breast on warm breast lies.
It is the fairest Eden
The seed of Adam knows,
But there no serpent’s hidden,
No poisoned apple grows.
It is a land of roses
Where gentle breezes blow,
And whisper tender stories
Of lovers long ago ;
To whoso doth sit doting
On love with musing mind,
Sweet thoughts come flying, floating,
Like rose-leaves down tee wind.
And tears are not forbidden—
The happy tears that fall
From eyes with love so laden,
They cannot hold them all.
And needs there other token
Who dwell therein are wise? —
The only language spoken
Is that of loving eyes.
But who is fain to enter
Must seek while life is young,
For from its faery borders
Grey age is backward, flung.
So, dear, ere Time grow older,
Ere Youth’s sweet spring is flown,
Ere love-warm hearts grow colder,
Let’s make that land our own ;
When found its golden weather —
Its roses—winding streams —
We’ll conquer it together—
The Land of Lovers’Dreams.
N.S.W.
ZADIG.
THE SEA-SHELL.
Friend of my chamber, O, thou spiral shell !
That murmurest of the ever-murmuring sea,
Repeating with eternal constancy
Whatever memories the wave can tell,
Whatever harmonies may rise and swell,
Whatever sadness in the deep may be :
They are the ocean’s, and desired of thee ;
Thou treasurest what thou dost love so well.
And all my heart is one voluted fold,
Shielding one face —and evermore it seems
Upon the threshold of the prying day,
Hid in the tangles of reluctant dreams ;
And in the noontide and the evening grey
Its light illumines secrecies untold.
M.L.
HUBERT CHURCH.
A BALLAD OF STYLE.
Having thrown aside that childish thing, the
simple style and luminous,
That pleased the easy-going folk of olden placid
land—
Lo! we revel in the technical —sidereal, bitumi-
nous,
Ethereal, leguminous, and glorious and grand.
For unless you tell your stories in a weird and
wondrous argot —
The which no mortal ever spoke and few can
understand—
You may lie for ever damned beneath a publisher’s
embargo ;
You may be indeed a classic—but you won’t be
in demand.
So we talk about the trammels that oppress the
soaring mammals;
We babble out a plenitude of ancient Buddhist
lore;
And we quote with ease the curses of the Afghan
on his camels,
And we blend them in our verses and our
sketches evermore.
Or at times we writhe and wriggle in a lingo
Aberdonian;
Our humor froths and bubbles in the “braid auld
Scottish tongue ” ;
And if Southron dare to murmur—why, we dub
him “base Bezonian,”
And tell him ’twas in “ Doric ” that our father,
Chaucer, sung.
And we paint Unreal Crimson and then call it
Realistic
(“Give us. color,” cries the public, “O, ye
Daubers, or we die! ”);
For a public reared on oleos has dainty taste
artistic,
And it’s woe to pen and pencil if the public pass
us by.
“ Hold the mirror up to Nature,’’cried old Albion’s
mighty teacher ;
And, obedient to his dictum, lo ! we bend a rev’-
rent knee,
Place the glass before the beldame, bid her note
each faded feature,
And then show her, on our canvas, what a beauty
she might he 1

litting pack-saddle, carried the old man’s swag,
tucker-bags, billies, and water-bag.

SATURDAY, MAY 29, 1897.
Patching the Federal Constitution.
This is about the time when the various Par-
liaments of Australia are to get together, and
talk over the draft Federal Constitution, and
suggest amendments to be considered at the
Convention’s next meeting. There is no
fixed or definite value attached to these sug-
gestions ; the Convention may adopt any or
all or none of them, as it pleases. Still it
is practically certain that if any particular
amendment is advocated by all, or nearly all,
the Legislatures it will be accepted, The
Parliaments of the separate provinces have
no legal authority over the Convention,
which is a Parliament representing six pro-
vinces, and a higher institution than any
individual Legislature ; but they have a moral
influence which counts for almost as much.
And it is all the more essential that they
should make good use of it, as this is their
first and last opportunity to do anything
valuable in shaping the Federal Constitution.
* * *= *
The great question at issue is whether the
Commonwealth shall be entrusted with as
much power as possible, or with as little as
possible, and the Convention has, in the
main, taken the latter view. No one can
enumerate all the authorities of the central
and provincial Parliaments, so it has to be
decided whether certain specified powers
shall be given to the Federal authority and
the provincial authority have all the rest, or
whether certain specified powers shall be
left to the provincial Parliaments and the-
Federal authority have all the rest. Here,
also, the Convention has taken the strictly
provincial side of the case. The Common
wealth Parliament will represent all Aus-
tralia ; it will command the largest share of
attention and all the most notable politicians
will get there, while the small ones who
can’t attain that elevation will be left
behind to be glorified aldermen in the purely
local Legislatures. The question is, there-
fore, whether the greatest share of authority
(for it can’t be divided with mathematical
accuracy) is to rest with the notable men who
can command positions in the Parliament of
all Australia, or with the small, parochial
men who are left behind by reason of then
insignificance, and the Convention has de-
cided that the aldermanic crowd shall get
the benefit of the doubt every time, ihe
purpose of the movement, as explained in
many grandiose orations by many wild-eyed
Daniel O’Connors, is to make the six Aus-
tralias one nation with common interests
and a common “destiny,” whatever that
may be, and to obliterate pro\ incial
boundaries and jealousies and squabbles
and the small meannesses that are born
of much subdivision and parochialism.
In practice, however, the Convention set
itself to discover just how little unification
it could get through with, and how few
interests it could unite under Federal con-
trol, and how many of the small provincial
interests and jealousies and squabbles it
could keep alive and in good working order.
Also, it must be mentioned to its discredit
that it succeeded admirably.
* * * *
If the respective Parliaments agree in
demanding a broader Constitution and vastly
greater powers for the Federal Government,
they will probably carry their point, even
with the parochial-souled majority in the
Convention. The matters which, in The
Bulletin’s opinion, specially demand atten-
tion are these : (1) The public debts should
be taken over by the Federal Government
either wholly or as far as possible, or at least
power should be given to the Federal Par-
liament to take them over, even without the
consent of the provincial authorities. (2) The
railways should be Federal property and
under Federal control. If they cant be
conveniently taken over now power should
be given to take them over later, with or
without the consent of the provinces. (3)
All rivers flowing through more than one
province, and all their tributaries, should be
under Federal control. (4) The powers to
be left with the State Governments should
be defined, and all other powers should belong
to the Federal authority instead of the other
way about. Wherever there is a doubt the
Federal and not the State Government
should have the benefit of it every time.
There are a few other matters wherein the
provincial tendencies of the Convention stick
out conspicuously, but these four are the
most-important.
The case for the consolidation of the public
debts can be put in a very small space.
These debts are always falling due and being
renewed. The credit of the Commonwealth
must be better than that of any separate
section of it, for exactly the same reason that
the credit of New South Wales is better
than that of Balmain or Bathurst or
Kiama, therefore it could renew these liabili-
ties at a lower rate and save much good
legal tender in the way of interest. Any-
thing that reduces the annual tribute pay-
able to the perpetual J. Bull is at least
worth consideration, especially now that the
British boom in Australian stocks is appa-
rently over, and the British creditor demands
£3 7s. 6d. per cent, where he lent a year ago
at 3 per cent., and this country is once more,
after a brief outbreak of cheap money, brought
face to face with the fact that J. Bull
is a very hard creditor. The difficulties in
the way are that the amount of debt per
head varies in the different provinces, the
interest rates are different, so are the dates
of repayment and the assets to be set
against the debts. But a great deal could
be done without raising these difficulties at
all. The smallest debt in proportion to
population is now Victoria’s £4O 9s. 2d. per
head. The Federal Government could
therefore take over from each province as
much of its debt as would equal £4O 9s. 2d.
per head without taking over any corres-
ponding assets, and proceed to convert it, as
it fell due, into a uniform stock at the lowest
possible rate of interest. Until the conver-
sion was finished any discrepancies in the
rate of interest could be charged to the
separate provinces. This would mean that
£142,000,000 out of £224,000,000 of debt
could be handed over to the Federal Govern-
ment without raising any of the awkward
questions involved in a complete transfer,
and if the superior credit of the Federal
Government saved even ss. per cent, on
conversion it would mean an economy of
nearly £400,000 per annum. Later on,
when the Commonwealth was fully rested, it
might think out some equitable method of
taking over the whole debt, but there is no
reason why the operation should not be
commenced in the meantime.
* * * *
The railway question is even urgent
than the debt problem ; the latter may be not
much more difficult of solution twenty years
hence than it is to-day, but the former is be-
coming every day more incurable. There
are two unanswerable reasons why railway
management and construction should be
put under Federal control at once. A uni-
form gauge must be established sooner or
later for purposes of economy, and a central
authority is required to force a settlement of
the matter, to decide on a uniform gauge and
to charge the cost of. the alteration to the
railway system of Australia in general, any
attempt to apportion it satisfactorily among
the five hostile departments of live interested
provinces being practically hopeless. Just
now it is only a question of altering, say,
7000 miles of line, whereas in some years
more it will be a question of altering 15,000
or 20,000 or 50,000 or more miles at a pro-
portionately increased expense. As regards
railway construction, the Federal idea in
building new lines is to give the producer
the shortest and cheapest possible outlet to
the nearest city or seaport. The provincial
idea is to force him to send his expoits, at
hjiv cost and inconvenience, ma the pro-
vincial capital, or at least to provincial sea-
ports. Therefore, the question is whether
the East Gippsland lines, which must be
built shortly, are to be so constructed as to
drag the local exports to Melbourne instead
of to their natural outlet, Eden in New South
Wales ; and whether the new West A lctonan
railways are to all point towards Melbourne
instead of leading, as they should do, to Ade-
laide ; and new Riverina lines towards Sydney
instead of Melbourne ; and new lines in
Western New South Wales to Sydney instead
of Adelaide or Spencer’s Gulf. In short, it
is a question whether many millions are to
be needlessly wasted in building the railway-
systems of three provinces more and more
askew, so as to be as useless and costly a,s
they can be, as a sacrifice to provincial vani-
ties and to the interests of two bloated cities.
* * * *
The reasons why, in The Bulletin s opinion,
something should be done, even at this late
hour, to shift the balance of power from the
provincial Legislatures to the Commonwealth
have already been explained. It is a ques-
tion of putting the principal trust in the poli-
ticians whose standing is high enough to
carry tlicm into the Parliament of all A us-
tralia or in the ruck of small men who aie
left behind. Many men of the present pro-
vincial crowd, when they climb into a wider
sphere, will probably see the matter in a new
light when it is too late for the new illumina-
tion to do any good. Just now Lyne and
Reid, of New South Wales, are conspicuous
among the narrow Feclerationists of their
own province. As Lyne and Reid of New
South Wales, the transfer of large powers to
the Federal Legislature looks to them like
an act of surrender, and they believe in
strictly limiting the Federal authority. A
year or two hence they may be in the Federal
Parliament as Lyne and Reid of Australia—
with their powers so strictly limited by their
own act that they will be little more than the
servants of the two Browns, and the three
Joneses, and the two Smiths, and Smailes,
and Goumly, and William Wilks, who were
left behind as not of sufficient intelligence to
deserve promotion. This is not a pleasant
prospect for any ambitious leader, and the
prominent men who keep up the provincial
end of the argument should think over it
hard. The Bulletin believes strongly in a
Federal Constitution of the Convention pat-
tern, if nothing better can be obtained, but
there is still a chance of something better if
the Parliaments like to make good use of
their present opportunity. It is absolutely
the only opportunity they will have, which is
all the more reason why it should be used to
the very best possible purpose.
The N.S.W. Foreign-trade Bag of Tricks.
When the present New South Wales Govern-
ment assumed office it pledged itself to a lot of
serious responsibilities. It promised to give up
a great deal of customs revenue for the sake of
Freetrade and in order to make things easy for
the blessed importer ; and though it never did
introduce Freetrade—for customs duties are
still the second largest item of revenue—it
sacrificed a good deal of valuable income.
Having sworn, in season and out of season, that
the Protectionist Government was accumulating
constant deficits, it had to promise—for the
sake of getting into office—that it wouldn’t
have any deficits. It also promised that it
would pay off the accumulated shortages of its
predecessors, and make the country prosperous,
and the people “ jolly and fat,” and that it
would shift the burden of taxation off the poor
and on to the rich, and that it would do many
other beautiful things.
* *
Being compelled, for the sake of being a
Freetrade party, to promise to do more than
its predecessors with less revenue, the Reid
Government brought up short against two alter-
natives. If it filled bv sheer economy the gap
it had (for the sake of its policy) to create, it
meant cutting down civil-service salaries about
one-half, or defaulting on the public debt, or
something else of a very large and original char-
acter. If it filled the gap by new taxation it
meant Is. 3d. in the £ income-tax and
about 3d. in the £ of land-tax, which would
have been altogether too overwhelming. Not
having the courage to do either, nor the
resource to find new and hitherto unexplored
sources of revenue, it struck three compro-
mises. It partly introduced Freetrade ; it
filled part of the gap thus created in the
revenue by new direct taxation ; it promised to
cover part of the remaining shortage by econo-
mies which haven’t yet developed ; and for the
rest it tried to make ends meet, or rather look
as if they met, by shuffles, evasions, lies, and
weird and wonderful cooking of the public
accounts. Sheer want of revenue has driven it
to these straits. It can’t raise the needful
amount from Customs duties without destroy-
ing the only reason for its existence, or In-
direct taxation without driving even the im-
porters from its standard, or by economy with-
out making economy develop into something
approaching sheer starvation. So it leads a
miserable, shiftless, shoeless existence, with its
political hair sticking up through the broken
crowm of its battered political hat, and is a
depressing spectacle generally.
The following are the principal devices by
which the Reid Government has, so far,
endeavored to make ends meet in the public
imagination: (1) In 1895-96 it partly dodged
the payment of the regular instalment towards
the old deficit; whereas Parkes and Dibbs
paid £150,000 a year, Reid only charged him-
self with £150,000 in 18 months, and thus
saved £75,000. (2). It evaded part of the pay-
ments towards sinking-funds in reduction of the
public debt; Dibbs’ payments on this account
were at the rate of £111,500 per annum ; Reid’s
for the first 18 months, at the rate of £BB 800
per annum. (3). It reduced the payments’out
of revenue for public works from Dibbs’ average
of £1,084,000 per annum to an average of
£905,000 per annum ; in other words, it charged
works to loans which were formerly paid out of
revenue. (4). In December, 1895, 16 months
after the Reid Government came into office it
charged all its own outstanding accounts 'to
loans, under the unaccountable pretence that
this was somehow essential to the starting of
the “ casli basis ” system, and called the amount
part of the “ Dibbs deficit,” though Dibbs was
even then ancient history. (5). It is now
collecting two years’ income - tax in one
year, in order to keep the finances of 1897
moderately solvent. (6). It tried to take some
£400,000 of loan moneys in as ordinary revenue
on the ground that previous Treasurers should
have done so in previous years for certain
reasons not very clearly explained. (7) It in-
cluded the alleged £333,000 surplus of 1896 as
revenue received in 1897, and thereby makes its
1897 deficit of £229,000 into an alleged new
surplus of £104,000. (8) It is still raising a
large amount of revenue (some £150,009 per
annum) out of protective duties. (9) The
“ cash basis ” not only served as an excuse for
charging some £476,000 of current expenditure
back to loans, but it enables the Reid
Government to charge the accounts of the cur-
rent year unpaid at June 30 on to next year,
and thus it permanently shifts forward about
£600,000 of its liabilities. Between what was
shifted back to loans and what is shifted for-
ward to posterity, the saving on paper is con-
siderable when spread over so short a space as
2J years. (10) In a general sort of way it
alleges that everything which goes wrong even
now is part of the “Dibbs deficit,” though
Dibbs has been nearly three years out of office,
and, apparently, if the Reid Government re
mains continuously in office till 1910 or later, it
will be found asserting that its shortage even
then is part of the “Dibbs deficit” just the
same, DibbS being a malign individual who will
be spending public money long after he is dead
and in his grave. It has never proved very dis-
tinctly that there ever was any Dibbs deficit
worth mentioning, but it gets over that diffi-
culty by treating it as a fact which is so beastly
self-evident that it doesn’t need proof. Its
attitude in the matter is that of the Almighty
addressing a black-beetle.
* * * *
Probably the Reid Government would keep
reasonably honest and straight if it had the
money with which to pay its way, it being much
easier to be honest than to lead the miserable
existence of shifts and expedients and hand-to-
mouth devices which is its customary lot. Butit
hasn’t the money. Parkes’s creed allowed him
to raise enough, or nearly enough, to keep
things going by a revenue tariff, and Dibbs’s
allowed him to find the necessary funds with-
out any apology or explanation, by a tariff
that was partly protective and partly for
revenue purposes. Reid got into office by
pledging himself to sacrifice a great part of the
Customs revenue, and though he is only keep-
ing his pledge by instalments still the loss
already incurred is very heavy. The promised
economies in expenditure haven’t eventuated,
possibly because a careful review of the posi-
tion showed that there was not the scope for
new economies that had been imagined. At all
events, apart from the saving made by charging
works to loans which ought to be paid out of
revenue, the Reid Government, which has a
decent reputation for economy, costs just the
same as that of Reid’s allegedly extravagant
predecessor. Premier Reid is pledged not to
increase the land-tax beyond the present Id. in
the £. He is pledged not to revert to the
policy of extravagant land sales, and as he
already raises almost as much land revenue as
Dibbs, not much less than Parkes, about
£400,000 a year more than Jennings and
£300,000 more than Stuart, any further ex-
pansion in this direction seems difficult. He is
pledged to sacrifice about £150,000 a year more
revenue by the gradual abolition of the remain-
ing protective duties. Next year he will have,
at the present rate, £150,000 less income tax
than he has this year, there being only one pay-
ment instead of two ; and this year ends don t
meet by a long way. If he raises the tax to
Is. in the £ he will even then only have as
much income from that source as he has this
year, and if he raises it to Is. 9d. in the £ he
will only have enough, on this year’s basis, to
keep things barely level. And the country, so
far as present signs go, wouldn’t take kindly to
an income-tax of that size.
♦ * * *
The present attitude of the Reid party is,
therefore, somewhat as follows : (1) There is a
surplus ; (2) Anyhow', there isn’t a deficit; (3)
If there is one it is really Dibbs’s deficit; (4)
If there is a deficit Dibbs had a larger one ; (0)
If there is a deficit it is caused by paying on
Dibbs’s shortage ; (6) Things are a great deal
worse in Victoria. And the party’s organs have
to follow in the same strain ; the business of a
party organ is to back up its leader in every-
thing, and curse the Opposition leader in every-
thing. They have to whoop up the weary,
hopeless tarradiddle about the finances, to
invent evidence that the country is prosperous
under Free trade, or that it was more unpros-
perous under Protection ; and to manufacture
proofs of the ruin of Protectionist ictoria and
Tasmania and Maoriland to show that free-
trade yields the better results after all, or to
maintain that where these provinces show signs
of prosperity their fiscal system has nothing to
do with it. Reid’s exploded list of tender-
prices was part of the same struggle ; so was
his bogus list of the Dibbs Government’s civil
service appointments ; so was the Telegraphs
sham catalogue of Sydney and Melbourne prices,
so was its bitter wail the other day about
the non-paying Victorian railways as an
evidence of the evils of Protection —the fact
that New South Wales has a worse list to show
as a proof of the evils of Freetrade being
wholly suppressed ; so was the Heralds
claim, the other day, that the increase of New
South Wales manufacturers in ’95 was a
glorious proof of the advantages of Freetrade,
regardless of the fact that Freetrade didn t
start till ’96. The existence of the New South
Wales Freetraders boils down now to the two


phrases, “Bibbs deficit” and “the ruin of
Victoria,” and both of them are getting to
be about threadbare. The best hope of the
party is that Federation may come along
promptly, and enable it to shift the principal
financial difficulty on to the shoulders of the
Commonwealth before the last of its dodges is
used up, and the finances of the Reid Govern-
ment have to burst on their own merits.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Japanese Invasion.
On 28th April the Q. commission on the mining
and pearl-shelling industries sat at Thursday
Island. Following are extracts from the evi-
dence taken, which was published in the north-
ern papers and carefully omitted by the Brisbane
Sup-Press :
In the evening Hon. John Douglas, the Government
Resident, was examined, and in the course of his evi-
dence, he said the Japanese influx was a more serious
question than the size of pearl-shell, and if the rate of in-
crease of lost year were maintained the whole place must
eventually fall into Japanese hands. At the beginning of
the year there were 231 Japanese in town in various occu-
pations, and 61 had since been added to that total. The
Japanese population in the town, on the ocean, and at
sea-side stations was probably 900. A Japanese boat
arriving next Monday (3rd May) had 200 Japanese on
board for this place. The majority would remain here,
while some would drift south. Ship and boat repairing
was absolutely in the hands of the Japanese, and there
was not a solitary white man boat-repairing here, although
the pearling boats numbered 207, and the beche-de-mer
360. They were also large property-holders, and the
Japanese business men were increasing, and property was
gradually falling into their hands. He regarded theJaps
as a positive menace to the white population. At the con-
clusion of his examination Mr. Douglas said there was
every appearance of Thursday Island and its vicinity be-
coming a Japanese colony, and stated distinctly a severe
blow must be administered if the place were to be kept
for the white race.
Mr. Hudson, the manager at Thursday Island for
Aplin, Brown and Crasiiav, also gave evidence. The
Japanese invasion he pooh-poohed, and said if the Japs
were prohibited from the industry it would perish. While
laborers were absolute failures at the business. Replying
to Mr. Hoolan, he said the Japanese were not large
property-holders, but he had reason to believe they
were money-lenders. He knew they owned boats,
and under cover were reliable laborers. He saw
no danger to Australian interests in the presence of
tile Japanese in large numbers. He cared nothing for
Australian interests, and had only one consideration, para-
mount and supreme —namely, his own interests. What he
cared for was to get labor for his boats. He only cared for
his own interests, by which he meant the interest of his
./inn. Australian interests, from a national point of view,
were nothing to him. The main thing was the interests of
his firm. In reply to Mr. Hoolan, Mr. Hudson candidly
said the words might be taken down.
The Postmaster, under authority from the Chairman of
the Commission, gave a return of the money sent to Japan
for the year ending March, 1897, which was £8455.
Mr. P. P. Outridge (of Clarke & Co., pearl shellers)
also gave evidence, and said that State interference was
necessary to preserve the industry for the white race.
White divers were superior to aliens, only they disliked
the occupation and refused to settle down to it. It was
lamentable that up to the present no remedy had been
tried. The depression on Thursday Island at present was
due to the presence of the Japanese ; no owners of boats
should hire or sell to Japs, and no divers’ licenses should
be issued to them. The other colored men have joined
in a common cause against the Japanese, who will extin-
guish them in time.
Mr. Thomas Farquharson (manager of Queensland Pearl
shelling Company) also gave evidence, and said he par-
ticularly desired to impress on the Commission the neces-
sity of repressing the Japs ; if matters continued as at
present, pearl-shelling would be completely absorbed by
them, and already their debasing influence was every-
where visible. Trade was depressed, and the business
people disheartened. The pearl trade would be of more
importance in the future than it had been in the past.
Hut its results would go to Japan if there was any change,
for the latter people were intensely patriotic and woulil
direct all trade and profit to Japan. The Japanese were
so established on Thursday Island that domestic servants
were entirely unknown, and all trade was drifting into
their hinds. The Jap, when entering the service of
pearlers, expressly stipulated for diving practice, with the
full intention when his term was up to start practising on
the first opportunity. The experience of the past 12
months iiad plainly showed him that the pearl-shell trade
and other branches of commerce were drifting over to
the Japanese. Thursday Island was becoming subservient
to the diminutive alien.
“ He cared nothing for Australian interests, and
had only one consideration, paramount and
supreme — namely, his own interests . ” That
speech may be taken as typical of the policy of
the half-dozen shipping and importing firms
which have their octopus-claws on the North
and its industries. Question is : What is
Queensland going to do about it ? What is
Australia going to do about it ? Year after
year for a long time now The Bulletin and
other radical journals have been advertising the
danger to ears deaf or heedless. Now the day
is at hand when the Japs will be so firmly
settled in Australia that nothing but gunshot
will shift them. Meanwhile, Premier Nelson
who “does not mind these things very
much,” and who announced a little while ago
that he was “making representations” to the
Jap Govt, which he “ had no doubt ” would be
attended to, and there was “ really no need of
drastic legislation ” —has trapezed to England
to crawl in the dust of the record reign ; the
Labor-party is moonshining after “Socialism in
our time,” or pulling strings in order to grab
Ministerial office ; and the bulk of the Q. popu-
lation lies torpid and indifferent. And a swift
new service of Japanese steamers is all the time
bringing the little brown men by scores and
hundreds to conquer Australia as their country-
men have conquered the Sandwich Islands. If
the alleged white Australian really has the
pluck and the patriotism that he declares, it is
time—past time—that he woke up and put
them to use. Otherwise, he will only wake up
to find himself the spittoon of a dominant
colored race, who will have acquired his land
because he is unfit to hold it.
The Masonic Sham.
The hollow pretentiousness of the Masonic
crafthood was brought under particular notice
by the array of some 1500 brethren at W. J.
Clarke’s funeral, and the newspaper reports
of their ancient religious service in connection
therewith, To the outside public the florid
beauty of a Masonic funeral oration—translated
from the Latin of ages ago, and only a little
marred by the interpolation of Scriptural ex-
tracts—comes as a surprise. Delivered, as in
this case, over a rich man and potentate of the
Order, it would suggest that Freemasonary
abhors grovel, despises selfishness, is jealous in
defence of the common weal, and admirable
from every point of view. Yet among the
aproned brethren who marched through the
Melbourne streets last week, “ maintaining
with sincerity the dignified character of their
“profession,” and “evincing their faith in a
correct moral walk and deportment,” any man
about town could pick out a very considerable
percentage of petty commercial tricksters,
inflated frauds, and grovelsorne snobs. A dozen
well-informed sight-seers, comparing notes,
might have passed a sweeping condemnation of
the Craft which unites so many bad citizens in
secret fellowship, no matter how loose its bonds.
The worthlessness of Freemasonry as a charit-
able profession is shown in the lives of most
persons who profess it. There are “good”
Masons dotted about the brotherhood, but these
exceptions needed no solemn swear to make them
what they are. Probably they would have been
even better fellows if they hadn’t joined a lodge
and formed the acquaintance of brethren whose
meanness by comparison tends to make small
virtues seem great. But though the Craft be
merely worthless as a religious organisation,
it is undoubtedly immoral in its politi-
cal bearings. The history of Melbourne
during the past seven years reeks of
legal corruption wrought by secret influence.
If Masonry denies that masonic influence has
worked in favor of corruption, The Bulletin
herewith fixes that Ancient Order in a corner
and wants to know why it didn’t turn its in-
fluence the other way, and maintain with sin-
cerity the dignified character of the Law.
Masonry stands convicted, by reasonable infer-
ence from facts, of shielding dishonesty, and
not all the spouting of a hundred grand-high-
cockalorums at a hundred impressive burials
could wash that conviction from the mind of
aDvone who thinks the matter over.
A Literary Obscenity.
The law in Westralia is a cast-iron affair with
the instincts of the gaoler and the gaol-flogger
in its little soul. The other day a Perth paper,
Western Table Talk, published a little poem
—one of those casual scraps of love-verse which
every editor gets by the bushel from all manner
of known and unknown correspondents. The
initials of the various lines made a stupid and
utterly indecent acrostic, and some lynx-
eyed person who was on the look-out for in-
decency—probably the writer himself—made
the matter public. Then the hog-law of Wes-
tralia swooped down upon the gulled proprietors
and committed them for trial for “ issuing an
obscene print.” Of course it was obscene, and,
obviously enough, it was issued by somebody,
and if tire issue can be fixed on anybody by
legal evidence, that person is doubtless punish-
able by the strict letter of the statute. But in
a decent community such a matter would have
been passed by. There are always fools who
love to hide obscene acrostics and tbe like in
apparently innocent contributions. No editor
has time, when he accepts a contribution, to
make sure that the first letters of each line
when read downwards or upwards, or the second
letters, or the third, or the twelfth, or the last
ones, or certain letters when read crosswise
from the beginning of the top line to the end of
the bottom one, or when read the other way,
don’t make up some indecent or libellous state-
ment. Therefore every editor is liable to be
taken in by the dirty and inane humorist who
writes this kind of elephantine mirthfulness.
No one seriously supposes that the proprietors
of Western Table Talk inserted this particu-
lar item knowing what was imbedded in it. It
wasn’t humorous ; it was so carefully concealed
that not one reader in a thousand would ever
notice or recognise it; and no sane journalist
would run the risk involved when there was
absolutely no profit of any sort to be made out
of it. Moreover, it isn’t absolutely certain that
the author knew what w'as in it. At all events,
an enthusiastic digger-up of unconsidered trifles
once showed the present writer a particularly
profane and indecent acrostic concealed in a
hymn in a volume which a highiy-respectable
sect uses, or lately used, every Sunday, and as
he presumes the hymnist didn’t compound that
out of an unclean imagination, the inference is
that acrostics do sometimes grow by accident.
Anyhow, the action of the W.A. Law depart-
ment has brought to the knowledge of almost
everybody a piece of casual obscenity which
would otherwise come under the notice of very
few, and its yearning after morality produces
more smellful consequences than tbe original
alleged immorality itself.
Mr. Pulsford in Reply.
Mr. Pulsford, M.L.G., replies as follows :
Your invitation to me to explain away my “ slander
upon that much-maligned financier, Dibbs,” is one which
I cannot accept, for the simple reason that I am guilty of
no slander. On page 329 of the Statistical Register, to
which you refer me, I read :
“On 31st December, 1885, the Consolidated Revenue
Fund showed a credit balance of £2901, but it was only a
nominal credit, as heavy liabilities were outstanding of
1885 and previous years, and the payments on this
account created a heavy deficit, which was considerably
increased by subsequent operations. To adjust the
accounts to 31st December, 1886, authority was given
under an Act passed in 1889 to issue Treasury Bills. . . .
The shortage in revenue was eventuallv found to be
£2,502,182.”
This, I take it, is the deficit popularly known as the
“ Dibbs deficit.” I think you will admit that Sir George
(then Mr. Dibbs) was Treasurer from 7th January, 1883, to
7th October, 1885, and from 10th October, 1885, to 2‘2nd
December, 1885. I think also that you will admit that in
the Jknnings-Dibbs Ministry—February, 1886, to January,
1887—although Sir Patrick Jennings was Treasurer in
name, that his colleague, Mr. Dibbs, was a potent factor in
the financial affairs of the province, and cannot escape his
full responsibility. It is this deficit of millions I had
in mind. The comparatively trifling deficit when Mr. See
was Treasurer (in the Dibbs Ministry of 1891-4), I do not
bring into the discussion. I admit The Bulletin is ex-
ceedingly clever, but I think it is beyond the power even
of The Bulletin to persuade people that the only deficit
with which Sir George Dibbs is connected is one of
£176,092 only.
The Bulletin points out, in reply, that there
is not one word in Mr. Pulsford’s letter to sup-
port the statement which he made in the Coun-
cil, and which this paper called in question.
Said statement was this :—-
During the last few years some hundreds of thousands_
of pounds had been charged to revenue to pay off the
deficiency left by the Dibbs Government, of which I
rather think the hon. gentleman himself (Dr. MacLaurin)
was a member. . . . The hon. member has spoken of
a difference of about £200,000 . . . the bulk of this
was represented by . the payment made. by the .Reid
Government in reduction of the old Dibbs deficit.
Mr. Pulsford’s logic is practically: (1) That
Bibbs is wholly responsible for the Stuart
deficit, if there was one, because he was
Treasurer; (2) He is responsible for the
Jennings deficit because he was not Treasurer ;
(3) He is responsible for the last Bibbs
Govt.’B deficit because he was not Treasurer;
(4) Also, he seems to be indirectly responsible
for the Reid deficit because he isn’t in the
Government at all. The case against Mr.
Pulsford is that not one penny has been
charged to revenue in reduction of the deficit
of tbe Bibbs-MacLaurin Government ; it was
all covered by Treasury bills, and the said bills
—issued against that and Reid’s after-deficit—
are all outstanding to this day. Also, the
whole amount which Reid has charged to
revenue during 2£ years in payment of any
deficit is ,£300,000, and he has added far more
than that to the accumulated shortage.
The Federal Chow.
Thirty-two (32) more Chows have just been
landed in Tasmania from the s.s. Australian,
which makes the third batch in about six
months. Sixteen of the latest swarm claim to
be naturalised, and produced the necessary
papers, but, on examination, only three of
these were considered genuine. The revenue
thus gained <£29o. The alleged destination of
the pigtails is largely and loosely described as
“ the East Coast tin-mines,” but, as a matter of
fact, the Chows are using Tasmania as a back-
door to Australia. The Land of Sleep-a-Lot
has no restrictions beyond the £lO poll-tax and
compulsory vaccination—a vessel may land as
many as her captain pleases. From Tas. they
filter across, or are smuggled across, to the main-
land. This looks as if the Chinese were willing
to pay<£lo per head for a few weeks’residence in
Tasmania, but bearing in mind the probable
proportion who get in on cronk naturalisation
papers it follows that it doesn’t cost the boss
Chow importers anything near £lO per head.
The Tas. Customs authorities aver that whole-
sale attempts are made to defraud the revenue,
and they quote instances of Chinese paying
importing firms a lump sum to be landed in
Tasmania f.o.s. Dealing wholesale, as they do,
the firm ships an agent with the immigrants
who coaches them in pidgin-English lies for the
entertainment of the Customs people. The
latter either have to adopt the rule of thumb or
place themselves absolutely in the hands of
their interpreter—also a Chow. And John
generally scores pretty heavily either way.
Though a certain proportion certainly drift to
the mainland in various devious ways, a good
many really reach the East Coast tin-districts.
The Anchor mine, Premier Braddon’s own
particular pet show, is in that same East Coast
district, and half-starved white miners are
wondering how long it will be before the rice-
fed heathen monopolise mining work in Tas-
mania. In any case the whole business shows
the remarkably fine sense of federal honor on
the part of the Tasmanian authorities, and ex-
plains that province’s frantic desire to retain
her “ state rights.” Unless federal action
(which The Bulletin has several times sug-
gested) is taken in this matter, pretty soon
it will hardly be worth taking at all.
The law’s gentleness to lawyers was exempli-
fied again last week in Melbourne divorce-case
of Bayles v. Bayles. Mrs. 8., the lively
daughter and heiress-apparent of a high-toned
solicitor, was applying for a release from her
husband, who is a legal practitioner and an ex-
member of papa’s respectable old firm. To the
surprise of a good many people, the case was
reported by the newspapers, not swp-pressed,
and in the light of sundry previous moral dis-
courses from the Divorce Court Bench, the
unhesitating decree nisi granted in this case
appears rather curious. The respondent made
no defence. Mrs. Bayles had the field to her-
self and told her own story as follows: In
1890 she accused her husband of unfaith-
fulness ; he owned up to the misde-
meanor, or several of ’em. She forgave him
and continued to live with him until February,
1894, when “being in very weak health and
physically unable to perform her wifely duties,
she took a trip to Europe and remained away
until October, 1896.” After these two and
a half years of absence Mrs. Bayles “ heard
from her husband, with whom she corresponded
regularly, that both he and her father were
anxious that she should come back.” Mrs.
Bayles, the long-lost wife of one whose ten-
dency to giddiness she had recognised, and for-
given, before she left him to his own devices,
accordingly came back. But, alas ! “ whilst
she was abroad she had heard from a relative
something which re-aroused her suspicions as to
her husband’s conduct. Consequently, although
he met her on arrival, she went to live with her
father. Then Mrs. Bayles collected evidence
of her husband’s familiarities with other females
during her long separation from him, and the
Court promptly granted her a divorce. Justice
A’Beckett it was who set the fair petitioner
free, without commenting on the queer character
of her case. As a very influential solicitor’s
daughter, proceeding against her father’s former
partner, she was treated with a consideration
which judges rarely, very rarely, show to the
ordinary kind of divorce-court suitor. It is
even conceivable that a stern matrimonial
moralist, such as Chief Justice Madden,
might have shaken his head gravely
at such a case as this. Mrs. Bayles was
“unable to perform her wifely duties.” She
went to Europe and stayed there recruiting her
health, until “ her husband and father wished
her to come back.” All this time she had been
in receipt of friendly letters, and since Aug.,
1895, she had had a monthly cash allowance
from her husband. Upon reaching Melbourne
she was affectionately greeted by ber husband,
but instead of going home with him, she re-
joined her father’s family circle as though she
had never been married at all. Then Bayles
conveniently took himself to Queensland, or
somewhere, whilst Mrs. Bayles took measures
to sever the marriage tie. And Justice
A’Beckett gave the lady a decree nisi right off
the judicial reel. No adjournment for further
particulars, no unfavorable comments)- Sassiety
laughs in its puffed sleeve.
The Westralian Government patty put forth
its supremest effort to defeat S. H. Parker,
Q.C., who descended from the Legislative
Council to contest Perth city, and to lead the
Opposition when returned. There was great
joy in the Government camp when Lyall Hall,
an ex-Victorian civil servant and member of
Perth City Council, but a comparatively un-
known man, defeated him by five votes.
Parker’s antecedents didn’t inspire confidence
in him as leader of the new Opposition which
is wanted to upset the Six-Family rule. He was
first an Oppositionist because he was not chosen
by “ Remember-me-no-more ” Robinson to
form the first Westralian Ministry; then, after
a time, he took office under his rival, Forrest,
but soon resigned, because, it is said, he was
too Conservative even for the Forrest Minis-
try. He retired for a season into private life
in the Leg. Council, but last year hungered for
office once more, and formed an Opposition in
the Council with himself as leader. The defeat
of Parker is regretted by the progressive
party, not because they would have liked to
see him turn Forrest out of the Premiership,
but because an organised Opposition would
have tended to keep the Forrest Government
up to the scratch. Parker would, in fact,
have been a noxious drug used in small
quantities as an antidote to another some-
what less noxious. To turn Forrest out in
favor of Parker would have been exchanging a
warm frying-pan for a comparatively cool fire.
Leake, the present Opposition leader, who
stayed in England all last session, and returned
only in time to speak on the motion of thanks
to the Speaker on the last day of the session,
was allowed to be returned unopposed for
Albany, which seat he won by one vote at the
previous election, and which he would never
have secured this time had the Government not
complacently allowed the seat to be uncon-
tested. He will now, possibly, retain the
leadership, unless it is given to ex-Victorian
land-boomer Illingworth. Anyway, Forrest
goes ’Ome in peace.
New South Wales Parliament should immedi-
ately take cognisance of the local Full Court’s
decision that the name of a “Gent One,”
charged with misappropriation of moneys or
other similar offences, shall not be indicated
until the final stage of the proceedings has been
reached. One of two things is clear—if this
decision is good for Gent One, it must be taken
as good for everybody, and, if it is not good for
everybody, its advantages must not be given to
Gent One alone. The Chief Justice’s statement
that unfounded accusations are sometimes
made against solicitors doesn’t justify an
exceptional treatment which means simply
craft-favoritism. The physician is as subject
to unfounded accusations as the solicitor; so,
also, the clergyman. Are those to be “given
away ” at first hearing and only Gent One to be
spared till the final ?
Thus the Cairns Post, N.Q.: —
It will hardly be credited that there are now In Cairns
37 Japanese women. They look very nice in their light,
flowing Oriental costumes, and, woman-like, know it.
These creatures are openly engaged for export in Japan
for a number of years—generally four—at a salary of
three or four pounds per annum. As prostitution-
machines in a town like Cairns, they are worth, on an
average, about £l2 per week to the Japanese pimps who
preside at the doors of the Chinatown hovels, and smilingly
receive the various members of Cairns aristocracy, many
of whom boast an acquaintance with the whole 37
Japanese women (!!!). Rows are now the order of the
night in Chinatown, and it is a common sight to see big,
burly, drunken Kanakas visiting these pretty little
Japanese, followed in turn by Chinamen, and then (noble
reflection !) by a party of two or three Cairns gentlemen.
The Japs are clever thieves, and it is a shilling to a
gooseberry about anyone leaving an establishment with
his pockets correctly balanced. They are also adepts at
the bully business, and the slightest altercation with a
lady is regarded as sufficient pretext to start an axe.
handle row, at the termination of which the visitor leaves
the presence of his fair Chrysanthemum looking kind of
anyhow. And there are 37.
Dear Bulletin,— lsn’t N.S. YV. divorce-law
disgraced by the possibility—to say nothing of
the demonstrated probability—of the following
sequence of circumstances ? Man gets down in
the world, and wife gets tired of the poverty-
sharing ideal of devotion. Thinks she might
do better with a fresh deal ; runs away to
parents with a bogus story of ill-usage, and
brings suit for divorce. Husband—having to
find money for her intermediate keep and costs
—can’t afford counsel on his own behalf. Wife
gets divorce, and husband goes to gaol for non-
payment of balance of oosls. This game has
been worked over and over again—generally
between daughter and mother ; the old man—
when there is one—being, as a rule, not equal
to the conspiracy and perjury whioh are such
trifles to “ the more moral sex.” A woman
with a design against a man will swear any-
thing, And the daughter-and-dam combination
has sent many a husband wrongfully, not only
to gaol, but to the triangles. Perhaps, after
all, the woman-franchise might help to remedy
some of the more glaring of men’s wrongs. It
takes a woman to understand what a woman is
capable of.— Benedick.
Chief Justice Darley —who errs with the very
best intentions—took the trouble to “explain ”
last week what he meant by curtailing to fine
only the normal fine-or-imprisonment sentence
earned by the Sydney newspaper-owners who
prejudiced Butler’s trial. And, by good luck,
be found a quite miraculous parallel to support
him in “looking at the position of these gen-
tlemen.” English Chief Justice Cockburn, it
seems, when sentencing two M’s.P. for con-
tempt in 1873, used almost Chief Justice Bar-
ley’s form-of-words in awarding fine and
refusing to award imprisonment; and, what is
more, found it desirable to “explain” next
day just as Chief Justice Barley “ ex-
plained ” next week. Exactly as Chief Jus-
tice Darley “ looked at the position of
these gentlemen ” and let them off the gaol
section of the sentence, so Chief Justice Cock-
burn thought it unnecessary to add that section
“ with gentlemen of your position in society.”
The parallel is so close, indeed, as to suggest
that Barley understudied Cockburn’s judg-
ment beforehand, and merely parroted it in his
own deliverance. And the fact that exception
was taken to Cockburn’s action is additional
proof how right The Bulletin was in taking
exception to Chief Justice Barley’s. The divi-
sion of. culprits into fish and flesh according to
their social position disgraces both courts
equally. Last week, The Bulletin was sorry
to find there was one court which seemed to
deal out justice according .to the apparent
status of the recipients—their clothes, possibly,
or their gold watch-chains, or their estimated
bank balances. This week, it is sorry to find
there are two.

The Corbett - Fitzsimmons battle will
shortly be reproduced with faithful details at
the Edison Electric Parlor, 162 Pitt-st., Sydney.
It was cabled that the great fight was recorded
all through by the Kinetoscope, and the colonial
branch of the Edison Laboratory before the fight
took place made its arrangements for securing the
record. The Edison Yitascope, Kinetoscope, and
Kinetophone, and the wonderful X-rays are still
on show in the parlor, and are attracting great
public interest.*

Brough’s new comedy, “A White Ele-
phant,” started rather late on Saturday night, and
the general impression during a ten minutes’
wait was that the pale beast had stuck in the
doorway and couldn’t get through. He wasn’t a
strikingly successful animal when he did arrive.
A want of perspective, or something equally
learned and abstruse, is what is wrong with the
“Elephant.” There is no central character and
no central incident, and no definite beginning or
end to anything, and the characters are so numer-
ous that they are difficult to sort out. The story
sets forth that Brough and his wife lead a mildly-
strained existence, the reason apparently being
that the lady gets up late and is only in her bath
at noon. The audience, of course, learns this fact
verbally and not by personal observation.
Brough’s elderly sister is secretly married to his
head clerk, who is hidden under the table.
Brough’s ward is secretly engaged to a man whom
Brough strongly objects to. Mrs. Brough’s scape-
grace cousin, a hardened co-respondent and stone-
broker, is engaged to an Amurrikan lady with
whom Brough badly compromised himself, in
very scanty attire, at a wild Parisian ball. Mrs.
Brough’s maid is engaged to the butler. Then
Mrs. B. is persuaded to escort her husband’s ward
to London to be secretly married to the
man Brough objects to, and her husband be-
lieves she is eloping with the stony-broke cousin,
while the cousin aids her under the impression
that she is bolting with the head-clerk. And all
these people, followed by Brough and his lame
father-in-law, arrive early on a cold morning at
the cold, shut-up family mansion in London, and
rouse up a depressed housekeeper with her head
tied up, and demand bacon for breakfast. As
there isn’t enough bacon the maundering old
woman speculates vaguely as to whether she can
borrow some from a similar cold old woman who
takes care of another enu>ty house up the street.
No very amusing complication ensues
when all these bewildered people get together.
There is no climax worth mentioning, and the
play tails off rather than finishes. It contains a
lot of good dialogue and a few incidents which are
more interesting than some other incidents, but
its tendency to straggle along towards nowhere in
E articular, and to disperse in various directions
efore it arrives there, is much against it. Six
characters have each an almost equal claim to be
called the principal individual. Mr. and Mrs.
Brough, Miss Titheradge, Ferris, Carey and
Ralph Roberts—the little man who first let loose
his curious metallic voice in the “New Boy ” —all
do good service, and shove the languid “ Ele-
phant ’’ along to its hazy destination with a vigor
and good-will worthy of a brighter mammal.
Miss Titheradge’s performance further confirms
The Bulletin’s previous good opinion of this
bright little actress, and it is hereby requested
that she will appear oftener for the future. Harry
Roberts, Miss Hardy, Wilfred Shine, and one or
two more, fill up the rest of the cast. “A Village
Priest ” follows on Saturday.
• * * *
An influential Sydney committee, including
George Dibbs, Edmund Barton, and R. E.
O’Connor, is working up a farewell presentation
to Mr. and Mrs. Brough. The Bulletin there-
fore suggests, in the interests of peace and good-
fellowship, that Barton makes the presentation
speech this time. The last speech—that on the
occasion of Boucicault’s departure —was entrusted
to Dibbs, and the large, good-humored, blundering
way in which G.R.D. complimented Mrs. Brough
on her perfect moral respectability was a hair-
raising episode. Dibbs’ breezy way of doing
things suggests a bull presenting a porcelain vase
to a friend with his front hoofs and making a few
feeling remarks with his horns. On that painful
occasion G.R.D. got away without raking down
all the scenery with his boots or trampling the
company under foot, but it was a very near thing.
* * * *
That old-time favorite, Edward Farley, has
been missing from the stage for a good many years
—barring a temporary resurrection as Dick Dead eye
in “ Pinafore,” the time when Juliet Wray was a
captain’s bright blue daughter—and his sudden re-
appearance came on the public as a shock. Farley’s
new venture opened with “ Maritana ” at Sydney
Opera House. “ Maritana ” is not, strictly speak-
ing, very new, and the Opera House has long been
regarded as the most luckless house in Australia,
but notwithstanding these circumstances business
has so far been excellent, and even standing room
was scarce the second night. The strongest fea-
ture of the co. is Miss May Glend inning, who
plays Maritana with a sweet, fresh voice, and a
capacity for clever acting which doesn’t always
go with such a voice ; also she brings to bear
on the character a picturesque frock, a bright
countenance, and a pair of feet that are
highly commendable. Percy Shannon’s voice
is also in good condition, and his Caesar de
Bazan is the same frivolous, airy, irrespon-
sible adventurer as of old. Likewise his
tattered finery and his melancholy hat, and the
hair which, by reason of his fallen fortunes, he
couldn’t afford to get properly cut in old Madrid,
look exactly, to a hair and a rag, what they used
to do ten years ago. Farley’s fine bass organ is
still in excellent repair, though his acting at times
is a little heavy and lifeless; and Mitchell’s orches-
tra is good, and the scenery respectable. The
ehorus is the weakest part of the outfit. Its cos-
tumes are moderate, but its legs, varying from the
appalling plumpness of the girl at the end to the
unredeemed boniness of the long person m the
middle, are woefully ill-matched, and the stout
lady who stands with her feet wide apart is an in-
centive to drink. A good part of the chorus can
bear inspection (within moderate limits) in the
matter of limbs, but the long person and the stout
lady arouse the kind of language which is usually
written on a piece of paper and handed up to the
magistrate. One or two other people, including
Lazarillo, are hereby let off with confinement till
the rising of the court; still, “ Maritana, at one
and two shillings, is good enough value for the
money. In fact, Miss Glendinning alone is good
value for the money. The “ Bohemian Girl fol-
lows on Saturday.
* * * *
There is, so far, no falling-off in the boom
at Her Majesty’s. Apparently the “ Sign of the
Cross ” has touched some unsuspected religious
sentiment in this land of beer and blanky, or the
fine old Latin magnificence of it rouses up the
hidden love of beauty which usually finds vent m
the wattle-poem. When one considers the gor-
geousness of the scenery, and the emblazoned
halls of the Caesars, and the jewellery on Neros
shaky hand and cataleptic head, it is almost pain-
ful to reflect on how the red-headed men presently
came down from the North and wiped their shaggy
red beards on all this glory, and drove their feet,
which were large and coarse, through priceless
ferns of art, and abducted the nice girls of Rome.
'here is a beautiful lesson in the “Sign of the
Cross ”; the pity is that the lesson is mainly
bunkum. It shows the pagans as tired and de-
cadent revellers, while the Christians are men
cast in a heroic mould who only require to
be more numerous to brace up the shaky em-
pire and give it a new lease of life. And
when the Christians did come uppermost they
proved to be quite unheroic ; they were the same
effeminate crowd of razzle-dazzlers as the idolators
who went before them, and the empire didn t
brace up, and the Christian legionary ran for his
life, if anything a little faster than the pagan
legionary who preceded him. And when The
Bulletin seriously considers Mercia, the Chris-
tian, and Berenis, the jewelled pagan, it decides
emphatically that the pagan girl is the nicer of the
two by a long way, and that Marcus Superbus,
when he chose between them, was a Roman wh«
didn’t know when to come in out of the wet. In
fact, the clever and attractive Berenis almost per-
suades this paper to be a pagan, only the constant
necessity for getting to press in time lea,ves it
very little leisure to be anything in particular,
and it can’t burn incense in the office for fear of
invalidating the insurance-nolicy.
* * * *
After a period of depressing ill-luck, busi-
ness at Sydney Palace has revived considerably.
The “ Living Models,” which is a cheerful scream
of joy, is still on the programme, but “ Milky
White” has been wisely substituted for the
“Dumb Witness.” The “Witness” was very
short, but it contained two dead bodies and a
bloodthirsty fight, and though it bad points about
it, if regarded simply as a compressed horror, it
was a rather gruesome episode. “Milky White
is a pleasant little comedy drama, and Gourlay’s
deaf milkman is hereby highly recommended as a
sketch of misanthropy in humble life, and as a
character who throws a lot oflight upon the short
measure in the jug one finds on the window-sill in
the morning.
* * # *
Leoni Clarke, the Cat King, is exhibiting
his menagerie of 170 cats and miscellaneous
animals at Coogee Aquarium, Sydney. They are
a quaint and clever combination, and honestly
earn their catsmeat by ground and lofty calis-
thenics and tricks of smart comedy, of which no
one would imagine the plain garden cat to be
capable. Moreover, they appear to much better
advantage as a separate exhibition than they did
when a tired pussy who wanted to be in bed came
on at a late hour to do its little piece in the third
act of a long pantomime. Clarke’s co. is a revela-
tion of unlooked-for intelligence on the part of an
animal hitherto mostly notable by reason of a
glassy eye and an untrained voice.
* * % *
Melville Stoltz’s variety co. gives its last
performance at Sydney Royal on Saturday, and
then goes on with its Australian tour. This ar-
rangement is based, of course, on the assumption
that Virginia Aragon doesn’t break her pretty
neck in the meanwhile over the performance of
some especially astonishing feat. If she does there
will probably be no more tour, but only a neat
little grave and a wreath of immortelles.
