The Tivoli programme remains very much
as described last week, the Selbinis being still
employed doing strange impossibilities on the
bicycle. The “silver-coin” show at the Royal
Standard is also alive and doing its 30th consecu-
tive week. It is likewise doing its 30th robust
horror or thereabouts, for Robert Henry’s co., as
a rule, dashes on a fresh horror every Saturday
night, and it gives six times as much gore, and
betrayed girl with her hair hanging down her
back, and stolen will, and unorthodox infant for
the money as any other show in Sydney.
* * * *
“A Night Out,” the inaugurate item of
the Paulton-Stanley comedy season at Melbourne
Princess’s, on Saturday, is one more case of the
middle-aged husband gallivanting with another
fellow’s wife to a restaurant, where they get
chased by misfortune and finally captured by the
police. Told in three long acts—the first being
rather slow —this narrative differs from a dozen
treatments of the same old theme in its French
construction and clever denouement. The things
that were bound to happen branch off suddenly in
unexpected directions ; also the best joke of the
evening makes a casual appearance just before
curtain-fall, whilst the finish is otherwise remark-
able for the escape of duplicity from a tight place.
For these and other reasons it seems probable that
“A Night Out” will do better business at the
Princess’s than fell, in former times, to the lot of
“A Night Off,” which was the brightest, most
convincing, and certainly the best-acted farce
ever staged in Melbourne.
* * *
The “Night Out” characters are quite
commonplace puppets, and the dialogue, as a nile,
is commonplace talk, but the bustling situations
and predicaments crowded into the last two acts
have 1 an air of freshness about them. The restau-
rant is an unusual place, containing a private
supper-room on one side of the passage and a
haunted dormitory on the other. rher ® f l ,
three beds in the sleeping apartment, and the
first man who engages it for the night desires
to slumber behind the curtains of a tour-
poster, with his clothes on, until the spirits
wake him up. The beds are meanwhile hired
by an idiotic father on behalf of his four daughters,
consequently, when these artless virgins dance a
mystic minuet in nightgowns, the startled sleeper
flies for safety to the supper-den of his gigantic
wife and her small cavalier, therefore the some-
what guilty pair disguise themselves as goblins,
and the whole population of the restaurant goes
mad. An attempt at explaining the later proceed-
ings is herewith postponed indefinitely, Enough
to mention again that the guilty pair cast suspicion
upon the innocent, and live to glory in the doctrine
that nothing succeeds like deceit.
* * * *
Harry Paulton, as the small husband of
easy virtue, is very much the same person as
Elton in appearance, voice, and method. in
short, the resemblance almost amounts to a charge
of plagiarism against departed William, his
reputation being younger and smaller than
that of the comedian who is now introducing
himself to Australian notice. There are points
of difference between the Paulton we see and
the Elton we remember, and the differences will
be summed up in favor of the recent arrival. He
never asserts his intention to be funny, yet he
gets there all the same. Miss Alma Stanley s
value to this particular show is mainly physical.
She plays a very secondary part with good humor,
and stands about half a yard taller than Paulton.
Mrs. Bryer, as the lawful wife of the small hus-
band, is another valuable foil. The other members
of the company, including Cecil Ward and two
new-comers —Ernest Shand and Miss Stafford—-
deserve a good word. In some cases the rest of the
company seems open to improvement, but the
openings are not very large.
*# # *
Harry Furniss, the illustrated monologue
entertainer, at Melb. Athenaeum Hall, proves to
be neither a disappointment nor otherwise. He
displays 150 of his original drawings upon a sheet,
in support of a discourse which is quite as
humorous as London Punch humor, and much
less heavy than the discourses of two previous
talking artists, who have dealt with battle and
murder. Harry Furniss deals with solemn Hing-
lish political institutions in a fairly broad and
lively spirit, his drawings are clever, and his sub-
jects interesting, as a rule. Moreover, he has his
discourse on the tip of his tongue, and the 150
pictures are all disposed of within 90 minutes or
so. Not a hollow, thin, or dry entertainment,
but essentially soft, like the comic address of a
pet parson at a chapel bun-fight.

There has been much interest displayed in
Melbourne over a peculiar halo round the moon.
A Bulletin man turned his glass on the
phenomenon, and discovered “God Save Our
Queen ” set round the pale orb of night in gas-jets.

description of the dresses at an Adelaide race-
meeting :
Not a little disappointment was felt at seeing the
Governor’s carriage drive away so early, but every one
was gratified to see that he was accompanied by both his
daughters.

COLUMN
In last week’s issue certain figures regarding the
finances of the last four N.S.W. Governments got partly
mixed up, the repayments in reduction of the deficit
being allowed for twice over. The complication alters
the figures considerably all round, though it still leaves
Dibbs as the most solvent financier of the four, and Reid
as, with one exception, the most insolvent. The following
is what was intended :
Jennings Government.
Deficit (1886) £1,484,462
Paid off old loans 31,549
Net Deficit £1,452,913
Parkes Government.
Deficit (1887-1891) £717,924
Paid off old loans 289,483
Net Deficit £428,441
Dibbs Government.
Deficit (1892-1894) £176,092
Paid off old loans 334,595
Net Surplus £158,503
Reid Government.
Deficit (1895-1896) £830,520
Paid off old loans 133,313
Net Deficit £697,207
This is the state of things up to 30th June, 1896, as shown
by the net revenues and expenditures in the latest N.S.W.
Statistical Register (pages 350 and 351).
* * * •
South Australia, in ’95, temporarily fixed up
£849,225 of its little deficit by the issue of Treasury bills
at £4 11s. 3d. per cent. They fall due this month, and the
Kingston Ministry has £184,738 accumulated, not out of
revenue, but from special land sales, towards meeting
them. The holders of the other £664,000 worth of bills
were offered 3 per cent, stock in exchange, but apparently
they didn’t accept, so the usual loan is to be floated
in London. So the £664,000 will probably disappear ihto
the mass of the general debt, and be never again heard of.
This seems likely to be the general fate of the shortages.
Tasmania’s deficit remains about stationary. Turner has
just managed to keep the accumulated Vic. deficit from
growing worse this year, but it hasn’t diminished one
penny ; in fact, this is the first year for a considerable
time that it hasn’t increased. Queensland boldly con-
verted its big shortage into permanent debt and dropped
the painful subject. N.S.W. has been laboriously paying
£150,000 a year off its deficit for eight years, and instead
of being £1,200,000 less it is about £400,000 more than it
was in 1890. By-and-bye, in all probability, some un-
usually tired Governments will dump the Tasmanian,
Vic. and N.S.W. deficits into the permanent debt and let
the unpleasant problem drop. South Australia appears
to be doing this now. The natural fate of such an un-
pleasant subject is to be dropped, sooner or later, with as
little row as possible—unless some reckless Ministry
starts a series of state lotteries to raise the needful
funds, and gets rid of the shortage that way, and thereby
wrecks itself with the local Nonconformist conscience for
ever afterwards.
* * * *
The S.A. loan transaction is a fine example of
how darned unimportant the Australian is in his own
country. The local holders of Treasury bills had the
option of taking in exchange 3 per cent, stock—appar-
ently at par, for nothing was mentioned of any discount
in their case. Failing their acceptance, S.A. offers the
necessary 7 loan in London at a minimum of £95, and
when the extra expenses are allowed for this really means
a minimum of £93 10s. If the loan had been offered in
Sydney or Melbourne the chances are that it could have
been raised at £97 with almost no expenses. At all
events, the N.S.W. Government and the Victorian State
Land Bank are both able to float 3 per cent, loans at par
in these cities, and the S.A. Government’s credit must
be very bad if it can’t raise the wind at £97. If the
Australian had only a little more confidence in himself
and a little less abject reverence for the financial Bull it
would be better for him.
S. A. also proposes to shift the sale of the bonds
of its State Land Bank from Adelaide to London in the
hope of realising a higher price. S.A. is run on Protec-
tionist principles in most matters, but the fact that it
may be better to pay £3 ss. per cent, to ourselves than
£3 4s. per cent, to somebody 7 else doesn’t enter into the
scheme of its philosophy. Considering the bad disasters
which have fallen on the last two Westralian loans in Lon-
don, it is just possible that the Kingston-Holder Minis-
try’s experiment at making S.A. still more dependent on
the foreign usurer may meet with all the bad luck it
deserves.
• • * *
S.A. State Land Bank has, up to date, lent out
£232,265, and, so far, has contrived to pay all expenses
out of profits and have £BOO to the good. As the ex-
penses, in the early stages of such an enterprise, are
necessarily heavy in proportion to the revenue, succeed-
ing years should show a respectable profit.

* * * *
The Bank of Adelaide shows a profit for the
last 12 months of £31,046 compared with £31,225 and
£31,048 for two previous years. The usual 7 per cent
dividend accounts for £28,000 ; £3OOO added to reserve
makes it 1152,000 ; and the remainder, added to previous
balance, makes £15,561 carried forward. The Bank of
Adelaide is an intensely steady-going institution with a
solid cash reserve and a steady business that never appa-
rently suffers from fits and starts and booms and the like
disturbances. Also its modest 7 per cent, dividend is
about as regular as the sunrise or the drought.
* * • •
The old question as to whether the A.M.P.
Society should open an office in London cropped up again
at the recent meeting. Tiie Bulletin is emphatically
among the “Noes,” for the following reasons: (1) The
expense rate in London would almost certainly be higher.
In Australia the A.M.P. is so well-known that a lot of
business comes to it without any expensive process of
canvassing and with no commission to pav. In London
it is very little known, and would have to hustle for busi-
ness in the usual costly fashion and compete against local
offices of as good standing as itself. (2) The rate of in-
terest realised on the funds invested in England would
be lower, and the reduced interest and increased expense
would both tell on the Australian policy-holders. (3)
The British business, by reason of its remoteness, would
be more difficult to supervise. (4) The Australian busi-
ness should be large enough to satisfy any office. When
the bone is so large it is dubious policy to grab at the
shadow as well. (5) The Bulletin is a consistent advo-
cate of Protection for the Australasian insurance indus-
try, as well as for other industries. It hopes to see legis-
lation adopted sooner or later, which will oust foreign in-
surance companies, both life and fire, from this country.
But if local offices keep extending their business to Eng-
land they obviously can’t take up this position, and the
present competition must go on indefinitely.
• * * *
In the report and balanfie-sheet of the Colonial
Mutual Life Assurance Society published in The Bulle-
tin of 15th May, Mr. Leslie John Park is described by a
typographical error as general manager of the Sydney
office. Mr. Park is really general manager of the society,
having succeeded to that position on the death of Mr.
Jaques Martin a few months ago.
« • * *
Sydney Telegraph item :—“ In the worst
period of our distress every life assurance office remained
unshaken, and, from the largest to the smallest, not one
of them even bent to the blast. In other countries where
more stringent regulatory laws prevail better results have
not been obtained, and, generally speaking, there is as
much public confidence in life assurance in Australia, and
as much to justify it, as in any part of the world.” This
is the sort of thing that is accepted as proof that there is
no need for State audit, and that a State life assurance
department is wholly unnecessary. But, as a matter of
fact, nobody knows for certain, simply for want of
“stringent regulatory laws,' whether Australian life-
offices were or were not shaken, or how much they were
shaken. A shaky bank reveals itself when there is a larger
withdrawal of deposits than usual. People can only with-
draw from an assurance-office by death (and they don’t
generally suicide for the purpose of finding out whether
their office is sound or not) or by surrender, and they
don’t rush to surrender wholesale unless a great alarm
sets in. The shaky bank is generally betrayed, sooner or
later, by the anxiety of the managers and directors, and
others in the inner circle, to sell their shares. The shaky
mutual assurance-office can’t be betrayed this way, because
there are no shares to sell. If the directors stick together,
and the auditors are as complaisant as most auditors —
that is to say, if they take the assets, or alleged assets, at
their face value without inquiry—a-bankrupt life-office may
go on unsuspected just as.long as the premiums which come
in cover the outgoings, and that may be half-a-centurv or
more. For instance, in 1895, the offices doing business in
Australia had a total premium income of £2,928,000.
Their total outgoings (claims, surrenders and expenses)
were £2,574,000. Therefore, they could have gone along,
to all appearance, as smoothly as ever, even if half or
more than half their assets were waste paper, and they
were all bankrupt, provided the swindle was worked
artistically enough. The Bulletin doesn’t suppose for a
moment that anything of the kind took place, but there
is nothing to prevent it taking place save the private
audits, and as the private auditor certified cheerfully to
the accounts of every rotten bank right up to the day of
the smash, they count for absolutely nothing. And the
fact that such a fraud is possible, makes it absolutely
certain that it will be perpetrated some day by some office
or offices—perhaps to the tune of many millions. Which
is The Bulletin’s reason for barracking at the top of its
voice for State supervision and a State insurance depart-
ment.

That delicate little plant, the Tasmanian Per-
manent Executors and Trustees Association of Launces-
ton, managed to earn £237 last half-year, and as the usual
5 per cent, dividend absorbs £2(LJ it naturally goes £25
to the bad. Previous half-year it earned £ll7 and paid
£262 dividend, so it went £145 to the bad. This institu-
tion, in the 9)4 years of its blameless existence, has con-
trived to accumulate £403, and it'has to hold on to that
little amount with both hands to prevent it slipping
away. And unless the co. sands its hands and gets a
better hold it looks as if the.£4o3'would slip.

The liquidator of the Dominion Bank seeks to
make C. H. James, the long-bearded father of the Mel-
bourne land-boom, insolvent, in the hope of upsetting that
gentleman’s fat settlements—said to be £loo,ooo—on his
wife. The question to be propounded in Court is : Did
James know himself to be hopelessly insolvent at the
time of the aforesaid settlements, five or six years ago?
The case will be characteristically Melbournian in its dis-
closures.

Ed. Bulletin, —Please listen to the grievance
of some of the unlucky. A young friend of mine
applied for a situation to a Sydney labor agency. After
paying ss. “ to join the membership of said agency,” and
another ss. for “ commission,” he was sent out to a place,
but found it had been filled prior to his application to
the agent. When he returned he 'asked for another
place or his money, and was/told he would have to pay
another ss. or lose the lot. What are we to do?—H.M.P.
* * ~ » •
The fares by Cook’s monthly excursion trains
between Sydney and Melbourne are now 70s. and 50s.
first and second-class return respectively. They were
formerly 60s. and 40s. The advance, which will not
materially affect the popularity of the excursions, will, it
is hoped, cripple the operations of the ticket-scalpers,
who are making a big thing over selling return halves of
excursion tickets to ordinary passengers during the
month, and employ touts on these trains to purchase un-
used ticket halves. In Victoria, drastic measures for the
extermination of the scalper are to be taken immediately.
A Bill is to be introduced by Government, and passed
as soon as possible, making the business of the ticket-
broker a criminal offence. It is to be hoped a similar
measure will soon be passed in N.S.W. Meantime the
combined annual loss to the railway revenues of both
provinces is little shortof £4OOO to £5OOO annually.
Tasmania is not to get her Great Western syn-
dicate railway, after all. Contrary to expectations, the
summer has passed without any ver}" big find being made
adjacent to the proposed gridiron, and so, and so-the
deposit which was to be put up won’t arrive, and as
Clarke is dead, and Officer and the others are very much
alive, the railway may be considered off for the present.
The Government will probably be forced to construct a
2ft. gauge line to the Wild West or resign. And there
shall be no more Fysh.

* * * *
Broken Hill Block 14 shows a profit for the
half-year ended March 31 of £17,077, of which £lO,OOO has
been paid in divs. The rest goes to swell the balance
carried forward to £29,305. The surplus of liquid assets
is £27,024 ; but of the £48,676 of assets only £152 is cash,
£28,644 is represented by concentrates and ore on hand,
£7240 by estimated surplus to be received on concen-
trates, £4546 ditto on bullion, and £5134 by value of
timber, coke, coal, &c.
A steady advance has been made in the opera-
tions of the Block 14 co. during the last couple of years.
In the corresponding period of ’95-’96 £27,639 was paid in
wages and salaries and £15,453 profit was made ; this last
half-year £40,238 was spent in wages, &c., and, as before
said, the profit reached £17,077. Fees (directors’), pos-
tages, and office expenses advanced from £llOB to £1544.
Block 14’s improved position is not due to ore
purchases, either. For the half-year ended Sept. 30, ’96,
the ore bought amounted to 32,958 tons, and the ore
mined 36,939 tons. For the half-year just ended the
figures were 17,744 tons and 48,315 tons. “At the present
time,” the directors say, “ more than three-fourths of the
total quantity is being raised from our own mine, and in
all likelihood this proportion will continue to increase.
The fact is that former ore purchases stood f he co. in good
stead, mainly because such enabled the full running of
our plant while the large ore bodies in the mine were
being opened up ; but, this being accomplished, the mine
now stands in a perfectly independent position.”
The developments in Block 14 mine during the
half year have been previously referred to as extremely
satisfactory—so far, at least, as quantity of ore exposed is
concerned. At the 300 ft. level, the Western lode is ex-
posed 800 ft. long by an average of 50ft. wide, and at 400 ft.
it is probably wider —at one point certainly 90ft. But it
is only just above paying point as to quality.
B.H. South has a bit in hand ; but the Adelaide
directors have been disinclined to announce a div., thou t
the Melb. people have been wanting one. By the way, a
Melb. weekly paper's anticipation of a div. in this co.
every second month is, in present circumstances, absurd.
.** * •
In the six months from October to March B.H.
Junction made a profit, according to the directors’ show-
ing, of £48,749. The total revenue was £93,508, so that it
was more than half profit. £30,000 was paid in divs.
The balance from the previous half-year was £20,445, and
£37,944 has now been carried forward. There was very
little outlay upon permanent improvements during the
half-year; but it will commence now. Wherefore the
directors have passed the May div., and announce that
“ they propose to defer the payment of divs. in future as
they think advisable, in order to accumulate a substantial
reserve fund.”
The fact is that, though they have not yet
braced themselves up to admit it, the new-chum Junction
directors lost their heads and miscalculated. Time after
time they were here told that the high-grade ores in the
mine could not stand the drain upon them. Now the
directors, in a choice scrap of English-more-or-less, tell
us that they “ have to report that their anticipation as to
the quantity of first-grade ore available for shipment
being' largely increased have not altogether been
realised”; also, “in consequence of the highly friable
character of the lode the manager’s resources have been
heavily taxed to provide for the satisfactory carrying on
of sloping operations consistent with the' proper securing
of the ground.” Obviously the ground was not properly
secured, for it collapsed. As The Bulletin said, the
manager could not drag out the ore that was called for by
the directors and secure the mine. Hence the creep.
Very magnanimously the directors don’t blame the
manager for the creep. But they don’t blame themselves
either, and therein are less just. They say, instead, that
“ the occurrence was quite unavoidable.’’.Further, regard-
ing this first-grade ore, though manager Phillips estimated
the quantity actually exposed 12 months ago at 199,536 tons
cwts. not mentioned), he now finds “it is impossible to
give anything like an accurate estimate of the quantity
at present immediately available, seeing that such an
extensive crush of ground has taken place and that a
thorough inspection of the ground has not yet been
made.” Notwithstanding which the directors reiterate
the assurance that this creep was really a capital thing.
Approximately 42,000 tons of ore was taken out
of the Junction during the past six months, and of that
21,800 tons was first-grade ore. In the previous half-year
the extraction was under 15,000 tons. On the manager’s
estimate of 12 months back there ought thus to be still
over 160,000 tons exposed, even assuming there have been
no developments. But there isn’t that much first-grade
stuff exposed, or anything like it. Wherefore the
directors rush the erection of a new concentrating mill to
deal with the second-grade stuff, and when that is ready
the Junction will have a bigger ore-dressing capacity than
Block 14 has at present.
Of the 100,000 Junction shares 39,735 are on the
London register, 30,976 are held in Adelaide, 23,704 in
Melb., 3100 in Sydney, and the rest in Pt. Pirie and
Broken Hill. Some time ago B.H. people held largely,
but they got out at the top prices.
A prominent Adelaide Broken Hill mining
man plunged heavily in high-class Westralian stuff,
bought on bills in England, and has had a bad fall. Big
Adelaide operators have taken up the bills and sold the
stock, and the victim has given long-dated acceptances for
the big balance.
**, * •
There is a little unemployed trouble at Broken
Hill. The supply had already overtaken the increased
demand, when a shortening of hands at the British and
Junction led to a justifiable outcry against the continued
introduction of men. Under the now almost universal
contracting system, an overstocked local labor-market
soon manifests itself in cut prices.
• * * *
The newspaper mining man at Carcoar (N.S.W.)
has risen to the occasion. “ The developments at Holl-
man’s mine are satisfactory. It is calculated there is
£lOO,OOO worth of gold in sight.” Under these circum-
stances, we should think they were. Carcoar, it may not
be too well known, has became a great opal-mining camp.
“ The Rocky Bridge opal-mine promises to be one of the
largest and most permanent opal-mines in the world. The
matrix is 120 ft. deep, and is sparkling with gems from top
to bottom.” That’s not too bad for an opal-mine !
“ About 120 acres of the richest portion of it has been
taken up by some Sydney specidators and is being floated
in London.” Why do all these big things go to London?
“The gems are of the milky class, with iridescent bars
running through them, a class of stone in great demand.
Experts say they are in every way equal to the best Hun-
garian opal.” But that’s not all. “In addition to this
there is a valuable gold-bearing wash underneath the
opal-bearing matrix which ought to largely enhanoe the
value of the property.” So it should.
* » * •
The Eleanora, Hillgrove (N.S.W.), has recently
put in its own electric-light installation, having wearied
waiting on the New England Electric Power Co. to supply
it. Its neighbor, the Garibaldi, which not long ago started
with a shriek, seems to have relapsed into that quietude
which, no doubt, Bull is sorry he ever disturbed.
* * * *
Orange, N.S.W. The Ballarat of N.S.W. g.m.
co. has been informed by the Mines Department that in
future a charge of 12s. 6d. a foot will be made for the use
of the diamond-drill. The co. has decided to pay the fee
“ and continue operations.” How very good of it 1
Months ago we alluded to a rumor that this co. was
working the mine at the expense of the Government, and
putting all call 3 in the bank to meet the ease when the
wet-nurse retired.
The output of opal from White Cliffs (N.S.W.)
amounts now to about £2OOO weekly ; there has been a
better demand for the gem lately, even inferior stone
finding sale; locally, this is attributed to the Record
Reign festivities. The English co. is the largest pro-
ducer ; it has 130 men on its blocks.
* * * *
Ere leaving Westralia for the jubilee junketings,
Premier Forrest put in a day or two at Kalgoorlie, where
he was banqueted with the usual result. “ The develop-
ments of Kalgoorlie were so splendid that, with the help
of other districts, they would make W.A. the greatest
gold - producing colony in the Empire.” It certainly
should be. It is by far the biggest, and no other colony
had a start with so many of Bull’s millions. Forrest
takes with him some “ beautiful specimens ” of the forma-
tion at Hannan’s, a golden casket for the Queen, and a
“ splendid specimen of telluride of gold.” He should get
his life fully insured if he shows them. Bull is full of
W.A. specimens, the sight of which makes his eyeballs
glare. He would probably take more interest in Forrest
if he brought ’Ome a few samples from the “ duffers ” and
“cats”—some of the “white quartz” from Bayley’s, a
few stones from the bottom of the Plunderderry, and a
“ piece of the formation ” in the Wealth of Expectations,
in which the Premier himself was largely interested, and
about the richness of which he made a memorable speech.
These would enable Bull to make a comparison between
the specimens usually sent to London and the stone left
in the mines. Forrest is also well loaded up with samples
of Bunbury butter and bacon. Esperance has recently
produced some fine melons and pumpkins, and two weeks
ago a big effort was being made “ to catch the Premier at
Albany with them.”
* * * *
Dr. Simon, who has been the means of putting
a heap of foreign money into Westralian mines, complains
of the hide-and-seek policy followed by many companies
out there. Estimates have been formed in London that
crushing could be done for 6s. and 10s. a ton, whereas
“ we all know that the cost varies from 20s. to 30s. per
ton for large parcels, and much more for small.” He says
they should all give the true cost of mining and milling,
and help one another to reduce it, and he is satisfied it
can be done. “ Nothing breeds suspicion like mystery,”
says the Doctor. He might have added that “nothing
kills confidence like lying.” Millions of money have been
scooped in by consistent, persistent, and almost universal
lying about W.A. mines. It helps to make a boom, but
always, sooner or later, kills it.
* * * •
Wittenoom, Mines Minister and Acting-Pre-
mier of W.A., has admitted that the reducuorf from one
man to three acres to one man to six acres, in the mining
labor conditions, had been decided on in view of the
threatened withdrawal of capital, and because of the
number of applications for exemption made on solid
grounds. This, too, will be given as evidence of the rich-
ness of the mines, and as the employment of wind has
more to do with a boom than that of miners, the writer
expects also to hear that the new boom is now coming
sure.
• * * *
Niagara, W.A. : “Two Japanese arrived here by
the coach yesterday, but the news of their coming had
preceded them. On arrival a party of residents waited
upon them, and intimated that if they did not return
again by the coach on the following morning, matters
would be made more than lively for them. They signified
their willingness to return if their company was not
acceptable, and left again for Menzies this morning.”
There will probably be trouble over this matter.
* » * *
W.A. mining news published in the S.M.
Herald :—“The Associated Gold Mines to date has sent
5000 tons of telluride ore to Wallaroo works for smelting,
and it is expected that the average yield will be about the
same as from the 160 tons already treated, which pro-
duced B*4oz. of gold per ton.” Great Scott! Why, this
would mean a yield of 42,5000 z. of gold. Of course, 500
tons was meant, but the poor, innocent old S. M.H. is too
blissfully ignorant of mining matters to see it. - The
hummer who sent it wears the belt. The 500 tons' have
since been treated at under soz. per ton—good enough in
all conscience, but these mine-barrackers cannot ‘leave
well alone.
* • * *
Hannan’s Brown Hill, W.A.—358 tons sent to
Wallaroo, S.A., returned 34840 z. gold—within a shade of
lOoz. per ton. Next to nothing is published in W.A. or
elsewhere of the developments in this mine. It has long
been known that a lot of high-grade ore existed above the
200 ft. level; also, that at about 180 ft. the ore completely
changed in nature and grade, and probably this change
more than anything else made the other mines in the
camp hesitate about going down. Has the high-grade ore
been picked up below the 200 ft. ?
• * • *
Kalgoorlie wire of the usual sort: “ A rich
strike was made in the Queen Margaret South, where, at
a depth of 100 ft., a large mass of almost pure gold was
discovered.” How much is a large mass? —a cwt. or a
ton ? And if even only a cwt., why don’t the shares jump ?
« * * *
Hamilton, manager of the Great Boulder, told
Premier Forrest recently to ask some of the London
brokers, promoters, and others, who had made money out
of floating W.A. mines, to invest a percentage of their
profits in development. But their business was to work
the public, not the mines. Men who make money by bur-
glary don’t usually spend it in offering rewards to the
police. Callahan, of Lake View, asked Forrest to tell the
Londoners that gold-mines were not found on trees and
they were not to be picked up anywhere. Another man
suggested that Forrest should do all he could to weed out
the “ cats.” Forrest, of course, promised to do his best
to remove the slump. Trying to “restore confidence”
after the boomsters have got off with the boodle is labor
in vain. Kalgoorlie is going to produce many tons of
gold, but without a boom. The so-called slump is only
the normal condition of things, and the sooner it is so re-
garded the better. A gold camp that can’t maintain a
mining-market of its own can hardly expect to have a
lively one in London.
* * * *
J. R. Hamilton, an old Bendigo quartz-miner,
who first Opened the reef now worked in the Queensland
Menzies mine, W.A., in reply to “ Victim ” gives the most
sensible description of the mine and its chances. He
admits bad management from the start, but thinks good
profits can be made even if the average is only 27dwts.
per ton. Also directs attention to the rich reefs worked
by mines at each end, which it is only natural to suppose
must run through the Q.M. ground.

“ Rumored that Mr. Kaufmann is trying to pur-
chase another big block of mines near the Great Boulder
on behalf of the London and Globe Corporation.” How
many mines are there available near the Great Boulder ?
Now is the time for Kaufmann to get them cheap. But
why not go for a “ large block of mines ” near the Wealth
of Expectations? There they might be got even cheaper.
By the way, what has-become of that great mine ? Is it
crushing? Has it ever crushed? Will it ever crush ? Or
is it crushed ?

Bayley’s Consols, once the rich water-mine of
Coolgardie, has ceased working water, and hired Bayley’s
South battery for three months to crush stone for gold.
• * * *
Poor Bull seems to be just awakening to the
state of the Hit or Miss (W.A.). The Financial Bulletin
says, from a perusal of the circulars, it is afraid that
something is radically wrong. “The shareholders should
not sit Micawber-like and await eventualities, as it js
obvious that there is something in the background that
requires explanation.” But the battery gave every
explanation six months ago. The prospectus said so
many ounces per ton, the mill said about the same
number of dwts., and, as the machine can’t lie very much,
something else must have been well up in that line of
business. The fall of the shares, from much above par,
to 45., is another full explanation. The market lies eve*
less than the mill.
* * * *
“No one is more to blame than the British
speculators themselves who, when the mad rush took
place to secure mining properties, never paused to con-
sider whether any leases that were under offer, were really
worth the enormous sums demanded by the owners, or
whether there was such a thing in the world as a ‘wild
cat.’ ” (Kalgoorlie paper on the slump.) “We rely” on the
thorough knowledge possessed by Premier Forrest to
“ inspire London investors with renewed faith in W.A.
mining.” Well, as neither the Premier nor a single W.A.
newspaper ever issued one note of warning at a time
when they should have had absolute knowledge that these
swindles were being perpetrated -everybody else on the
spot had that knowledge—hut all barracked for the boom,
neither one nor the other can now inspire confidence. The
bigger the drunk the worse the recovery. W.A. enjoyed
the stolen fruits of a gigantic boom, and it bas to suffer
for it in the teeth of the tons of gold coming from its one
big camp—Kalgoorlie, but for which the whole province
would have gone into liquidation, and the Forrests would
have been financially and politically burnt down.
* * * •
Wadnaminga, S. A., has long been disgraced by
reason of its gold pcckets. Usually there are a few rich
specimens in some Adelaide shop-window from some mine
in that camp, and they are kept on show so long that men
have been known to raise their hats for old acquaintance
sake as they pass. Of the latest find one boomster says :
“ You cannot put your hand on the stone anywhere with-
out covering hundreds of pounds worth of gold. If I
were to attempt to tell ail I saw there you would not be-
lieve it. You would think it was a wonderful fairy tale.”
No doubt, and he is a sensible man not to tell it. A
Broken Hill expert is “simply astounded at the richness
of the claim, and says if it were in W.A. there would not
be the slightest difficulty in floating it for half a million.”
But W.A. is not selling any mines of this sort at present
for the simple reason that there are none to buy them.
Two men are said to have dollied about £lOOO worth of
gold out of that Wadnaminga claim in 12 months. It
sounds well, like all the rest, but means a good deal less
than an oz. a day.

Central Plateau, Ballarat, 441 tons, 91oz. No
divs. in 4dwt. rock. A few months back these shares
jumped from 15s. to over £5 : when the most absurd
tipster of them all said they wr ye quite good enough, and
advised inquirers to hold. They have gone back to 155.,
and the tipster is busy tipping other mines so as to make
up his usual appalling werage.
* * * »
The difference between money offered and
money in the bank. Said to be two definite offers for the
Long Tunnel Extended amounting to £4O a share —one to
put the whole of the money up in 30 days, and yet the
shares are selling at £2B. Like everything else, the busi-
ness is regulated by the law of averages. Talk as people
and newspapers like—and it is a cheap thing to do—the
market remembers that not more than one mine in ten
placed under offer, however definitely, is sold.
* * * •
There is a scheme afoot for reviving the ancient
glories of Stockyard Creek, a well-nigh forgotten Eldorado
within easy distance of Melbourne. Three quartz claims
—the Victory, Gladstone, and Jubilee—have been worked
in a small way for years past, and the new scheme
involves the amalgamation of these with the whole of the
alluvial claims in the neighborhood, covering an area of
two miles, or nearly, of country still untested below the
surface.

The Tasmania directors are not in love with the
work of their chlorination plant. The trouble is complex
pyrites. It was seriously proposed to close the chlorina-
tion shop, but as a final effort Victoria is to be scoured for
a skilled metallurgist willing to tackle the problem.

The Gold Estates Co. of Australia is trying to
raise £lOO,OOO in London, by the issue of that number of
shares, to develope 385 acres said to be located on a con-
tinuation of the Mt. Lyell mineral belt. The property
has been “ exhaustively reported on.” Capital, 1325,000 - ;
and, as vendors are taking nearly all the purchase price
in shares, this has been regarded as “ one favorable
point.” The fact that the Tight Little Island is only
about 100 miles or so long is another. It is reasonable to
suppose that the Mt. Lyell belt will have to be confined to
the island, whereas, if it were ten times the size, some
mines might be floated that were “off the lode.” The
local Lyell pup boom having faded away, an attempt is
apparently being made in London to revive it there. It
is 100 to 1 that Bull won’t bite in Tas. after being so
badly bitten in Westralia.

The mystery of the Divining Rod is more
or less elucidated by J. Holt-Schooling, in
March Pearson’s Magazine. That certain per-
sons, of widely-different callings and stations in
life, should not only be able to locate the existence
of water, but also be able to state at what depth it
would be discovered, simply by perambulating the
arid area of investigation with outstretched
fingers—across which a stick lies, till it points
downwards when it arrives above water—has
always seemed magical or the trickery of charla-
tans. It need be neither; nor is the stick itself
of any particular importance, some operators
using a light metal (aluminium) rod, and others
trusting to their bare hands as indicators. Hidden
water conveys indications of its presence to some
persons, in whom it sets up very delicate and in-
voluntary nerve-muscular contractions. These
contractions cause movement in the delicately-held
rod, which is merely a tangible indication of the
nerve-muscular contractions that occur. The
water does not move the rod, and thereby com-
municate with the diviner ; but tlie (Jiviner trans-
mits movement to the yqcj.

SOCIETY
Sydney is making arrangements to rejoice
with a dull, sickening thud on the Record Reign
day in June. The arrangements for the heavy,
stodgy festivity are in the hands of Acting-Premier
Brunker. Reid, the only man of the Ministry
who really knows how to rejoice cheerfully, is, un-
fortunately, away, so the matter has to fall into
the hands of Brunker, and that stout and mildly-
vacant cow-vendor rejoices with difficulty. If
Reid let himself loose he is quite capable of hav-
ing a howling good time with lib. of sticky lollies
and a bunch of squibs and a live locust, and being
really happy; his substitute gives way to mirth
the way another man would give way to a ton of
bricks. His one idea is to get a lot of people
together and have more roast cow than usual, so it
is proposed to have an open-air free show' in Syd-
ney Domain, with illuminations and music, and
“ refreshments are to be handed round.”
The Bulletin sees that great outbreak of joy
in its mind’s eye. It is a wet night with a
southerly blast blowing. Two hundred thousand
people in streaming macintoshes are demanding
that somebody shall “pass the kyke.” Everybody
has got the other man’s umbrella in his eye, more
or less. Everybody is asking who the other man
thinks he is a-shovin’-of. A vague odor of mud
and macinproof and moist gamp and tobacco
and heaving human being goes up, and the
combined smell is that of a dirty wet dog two
miles long and one and a-half miles broad. The
refreshments are damp; the trees are damp and
drip on to the refreshments; 40 different brass
bands try to bear up with the rain lashing into
the trombone so that the cold blue performer is
involuntarily drinking through his instrument.
Underfoot is a deep layer of mud, trodden-on
cigar-ends, lost hats, old newspaper, cake, banana-
AN AFFECTING SCENE.
The poor distracted wife bent forward to catch his half-whispered words. “Alas !” he sighed,
“one distressing thought tortures me, and that is — how, when I am gone, you will ever be able to
provide” (here his poor sunken eyes wandered wearily round upon his nine grief-stricken children)
“how you will ever manage to provide bicycles for all these.” (And then he turned his face to the
wall, sneezed twice, and was up and about as usual the next day.)
peel, orange-peel, and other debris. Pickpockets,
rowdiesand painted harridans calling out “ducky”
rage on the outskirts or the crowd, and all the
long evening it bears up manfully under much
“God Save.' Towards the small hours, however,
it grows cantankerous and partly drunk and
chilled to the bone, and bilious with much kyke,”
and it wants to tread on the head of a policeman,
and the exhausted brass band is fainting at its
instrument, but blows for all it is worth to the
bitter end. The audience gradually disperses and
is assaulted and robbed in bye-streets on its way
home. The police Rather up the lost infants and
the drunks, and the last of the illuminations fizzle
out, and the last Botany tram runs over the last
reveller who has gone to sleep on the rails, anc
flattens him out, and the show is over.

A great row raged at Melb. Govt. House
on the day of baronet Clarke’s demise. With_an
excess of zeal, Aidekong Nevill no sooner heard o
the death than he ran up the Union Jack to naii-
mast and left it waving in the breeze. ror tour
hours the emblem stayed in its position, and then
Baron Brassey came home and rushed up the
carriage-drive with his veins standing out of his
forehead, yelling hysterically to haul it down.
When the flag of England had been reduced to a
multicolored heap the representative of Her
Majesty turned to the noble Nevill and gave him
a bad quarter of an hour, after which the rest ot
the day was spent squaring everybody to keep the
thing quiet. It appears that Govt. House flags
are only permitted to be half-masted in the event
of a member of the Royal family going under,
and, in signalling the death of Millionaire Clarke,
Nevill innocently committed a terrible breach or
international etiquette, which might have J' 0
the consul for Costa Rica withdrawing from Melb.,
or some equally appalling catastrophe.
» * * *
Disgusting how Melb. florists exhibited the
wreaths and other flowery things to be used at
Baronet Clarke’s funeral. The haberdashers who
were paid for the cere-cloths, the cabmen not in-
cluded in the undertaker’s eight mourning coaches,
and the man who squirted the street to prevent
ashes to ashes mixing with dust to dust, were un-
obtrusively silent. , , «
Slice from Melb. Argus account of the
Clarke funeral: —
Then followed the lesson, that beautiful elegy of the
Church of England over her dead, which concludes with
the words, “ O death, where is thy sting? O grave, where
is thy victory ? ”
Considering that the “ beautiful elegy ” is not the
especial property of the Church of England, but
is taken from an epistle of St. Paul, the credit for
the composition is slightly misapplied. It is to be
feared that editor Haddon studies the dog stud-
book more than his New Testament.
» * * *
How absurd the statement that the late
baronet Clarke was a man of no ability ! He
possessed in a remarkable degree, considering his
inoffensive style, the ability to avoid companion-
ship in public. Clarke walked abroad un-
“ nobbled ” and alone. Now and then a hardy
button-holer of the Parliamentary type might
stick-up the moody capitalist in the street, but
half a minute later Clarke would be walking on as
usual, leaving the other fellow to his own dark
devices.
The last act in the Clarke funeral melo-
drama was performed by torchlight, when the
undertakers unloaded at the graveside “ five vans
full of flowers sent as offerings of respect to the
memory of the deceased baronet, which it had
been impossible to stack on the two floral cars that
figured in the procession.” Ye gods ! No wonder
that the fashionable Melbourne florists showed
their respect for the deceased by putting their
windows into mourning.

* • • •
The bitter cry of a crippled mother, whose
daughter had married a Chinaman, was heard out-
side North Melb. police-court last week. The
poor old girl had made a wild, unavailing effort to
get her offspring imprisoned, or committed to a
reformatory—anything rather than leave her to
the Chow’s lawful embrace, and now the vic-
torious bride was flaunting out of court accom-
panied by Mow Wah and three of his yellow com-
patriots. “ Mary, Mary, for God’s sake come to
me, and don’t go away with those men until you
speak to me,” wailed mother. But Mary put out
her tongue, and departed on a tram-car in charge
of the heathen quartette, whilst the aged parent
stood weeping amidst a larrikin mob The old
girl’s folly had found her out. She was friendly
with Mow Wah a while ago : thought him a
decent man, and drank tea at his market-garden
domicile, never dreaming that the unsightly
faw-ner “ would take a girl of lb from her
home.” And all the time Mow Wah was spread-
ing his little snare to catch the fledgeling whosub-
sequently flew with him to Ballarat to be his
love. Mary’s fate should point a moral for sun-
dry other mothers, and warn them against expos-
ing their own flesh and blood to the hideous hyp-
notism of a Chinaman’s friendly leer —whether in
or out of Sunday-school.
* * » *
The Parramattites are arising in their
wrath to slaughter the proposal to lease to
King’s School 22 acres of their park. The trustees
appear to have been seduced by representations
that the desired portion isn’t of much public ac-
count, anyhow, and that if the school can’t get
the ground it will probably take the light of its
countenance elsewhere. These considerations are
entirely beside the question, if, as contended by
the townspeople, the trust was appointed for the
very purpose of preventing for ever any such
alienation as now proposed. If the dedication-
deed—which the trustees are challenged to publish
—is plainly to this effect, it should settle the ques-
tion in short order.

A popular Coolgardie publican was lately
ruined by being burnt out, just after his insurance
policy had expired, and it was arranged to give
him a benefit in the shape of the gross takings on
a given day of all the other local pubs. Also it
was announced in the papers that “ arrangements
have been made that, in the event of anyone
getting a little hilarious, the powers that be will
deal lightly with them on Monday morning.’

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their ojjal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

tennis champion, is, besides being grand-niece of
writers William and Mary Howitt, daughter of

Mrs. Williams, who got a divorce in Melb.
the other day, declared’that Williams Ipft her be-
cause he hated to see her soil her hands with toil ;
but he hated worse to soil his own hands, and so
Mrs. Williams had to work her mangle or perish.
She stuck to the mangle, and this was too -much
for the Spanish blood of Williams. He left, and
has never since been seen. The Bulletin is of
opinion that Mrs. Williams will be simply snapped
up. Another divorce arose from incompatibility
in re the lighting of the morning fire. Hubby
used to hoist his wife out into a cold world to huiid
the kitchen tire, and chase her with a razor if she
seemed reluctant. When The Bulletin considers
the misery this matter of lighting the morning fire
has induced in otherwise happy homes, it feels in-
clined to canonise the man who invented gas-stoves.

The recent
case of a man
“accidentally
killed ” at a Tas.
sawmill brought
to light some of
de ceased’s nar-
row escapes.
Once, a piece of
timber came back
over the saw and
broke his collar-
bone. Later, he
was sawing laths
and one “ came
back” and passed
through his
mouth and out
through the mid-
dle of his cheek.
Then half a log
fell on him and
pinned him to
the ground.
The last wrestle
with a piece of
timber settled
him, by striking
him fairly in a
vital part.
* *
The otherday
at Maryborough
(Q.) a small boy
was seen crossing
the bridge ar-
rayed in five hats, with four bundles of clothes on
bis back and a pea-rifle stuck in his belt. A citizen,
glancing oyer the bridge-parapet, descried four
urchins swimming the stream right vigorously.
. . . They had only possessed one penny among
them to pay the toll, and this was how they
solved the difficulty.
The age of magnanimity is evidently not
past, for in a recent big Australian divorce-ease,
which caused the very Nicest people to agitate
like a blanc-mange, only the names of the demi-
monde co-respondents were set out on the pleas—
the sins of the frail sassiety damsels being appar-
ently overlooked or forgiven. Yet there would
have been little difficulty in tracing these fair
sinners, if indeed they were not vvell-knowu, for
the keeper of a temple of Venus, who admitted the
patronage of the respondent, described at least
one of his frail companions down to the buttons
on her gloves. The dailies mercifully excluded
these exact details, but many a heart must have
fluttered until it had scanned the pages of the
daily papers as the sun rose.
One demi-mondaine, whose evidence was very
materia], could not be found up to a few days
before the hearing of the case, and desperate
measures were taken to obtain her attend-
ance. A trusty private detective found that this
same nymph was the chosen of one of the most-
respected of the province’s Fatmen, who holds big
positions in his particular creed, and takes good
care to advertise the fact. This genial old boy
was accordingly interviewed and informed that it
was greatly regretted, but he would have to be
subpoenaed to elicit the whereabouts of the missing
witness. At ten o’clock next morning the barri-
ster found Miss Aspasia waiting at his office door.
When she got into the witness-box her evidence
was interesting—in fact, so much so that the major
part of it did not reach the public eye. She
stated that one afternoon the respondent visited
her bower with a “girl in a shot-silk parasol.”
After an interview of an hour the lady left, but
Don Juan still remained in the place, and scarcely
had his inamorata cleared the front gate before he
started paying his addresses to the lady of the
house - and with success. Ere long, however,
there was an interruption in the shape of another
girl friend of Mrs. Aspasia, who was admitted
and introduced just about tea-time. The pro-
prietress then left the room to prepare the meal,
and on returning to announce its readiness dis-
covered that Don Juan’s attractions had proved
insuperable to the newcomer also. After tea, the
original sassietv damsel with the shot-silk um-
brella returned, and the quadruply faithless Don
Juan went back to her loving arms as good as
new. Don Juan did not turn up to confute the
allegations, and at latest he was in Guam or some-
where.
* * * *
Woman is getting on to an equality with
man right enough. In Adelaide recently, accord-
ing to the tale of a local paper, a lady was carried
beyond her destination by a tram, and was wroth
with the conductor thereat. She was so wroth
that she became hysterical and slapped him on the
ear. Whereupon, without any sign of auger or
discomposure, he gave her two on the nose, then
stepped leisurely on his tram, rang the bell, and
departed. The last that was seen of her she was
draining off the blood on to the road and the
subject had apparently dropped.
A FAVORABLE SYMPTOM.
JOHNSON (who knows little Miss Smith, the jug-bearer) : “ Well, how is your
mother getting along?”
LITTLE ANNIE SMITH: “Oh, she’s getting along fine she’s able to drink
beer again!”

That S.A. earthquake-scare produced, per-
haps, a heavy crop of liars, but the one artistic
story, in a wilderness of commonplace fiction, is
that of the tradesman who rushed down street, hair
on end, yelling, “That earthquake changed the
spelling on my shop from Smyth to Smith, and
I’m a ruined man ! ”
THE BISHOP’S COMPLAINT TO THE
SYNOD.
The gentle pastor, in his peaceful British parish,
heard the call
That, from the under-world, came up for one to
save. He oped his ears,
Raised his kindly eyes, and calmly murmured :
“ O Lord, what is the guerdon ? ”
And once again the wail came from under sea full
charged with fiscal import:
“ Ten golden centuries of sovereigns shall be thine
to guide our souls aright.”
Humbly he rose and said : “Lord, I take on Thy
Southern Cross. Forth I’ll go
And face the untamed kangaroos and other quad-
rupeds, and do my mightiest to win
Thine erring sons in that land where birds are
mute and sin and infamy abounding.”
Hu came, the courtly Bishop, and labored hard to
teach his Riverina sons
The art of knocking down a cheque for glory of
the Lord and His true servant,
Instead of honoring the custom of chronic inhibi-
tion of “ blue ruin.”
But, lo ! those sinful ones were deaf to his good
words, and turned not
From their way, nor ceased their playful course of
wild, hilarious guzzle.
And a bank crash came, and the invested funds of
the Established Church
Gave forth not more than half the promised sti-
pend. And the goodly man
Was sore at heart, and filled with trials and tribu-
lations thereanent.
And then he stood before the synod and declaimed
at the baseness of his trust
Being thus abused. And there, too, uprose a
simple one, who said : “ Oh, brother,
Why mockest thou at five hundred quid when
many in this country homeless range
And penniless as was our Master?” But quick
his Lordship fixed him with a look,
And cried, in ringing tones, ’neath which the mild
one quailed : “ Stow thy gaff,
For nowadays we bishops have to hustle for our
grub.” And the Synod
Smiled approvingly, and having thus affirmed the
principle, piously adjourned
For creature comforts—the only heavenly bliss
upon this wicked, wicked planet.
May 14.
D.H.
THE “BUSSY” SPEAKS.
'Ave you ever rode upon an omnibus?
’Ave you ever noticed ’ow we git along?
’Ave you ever ’eard a “lydy” make a fuss,
Or a gentleman use “ language ” pretty strong ?
If you ’ave you’ll understand my song o’ woe
(Though—crikey I—these1 —these are not our only woes),
We git no time for “ tucker” or “smoke-oh” ;
We ’aven’t ’ardly time to change our clo’s.
It’s early morning when we leave the yard ;
We don’t come in till nigh on twelve at night;
1 reckon that our life is pretty hard,
But to keep our ’omes we all must make a fight.
And you blame us coz we “ don’t go fast enough ”
D’ye think we ought to go like railway cars?
You “cheek ” us and you swear at us and “ bluff”—
An’ you blame us for the jolting and the jars.
An’ when the blocks is slippery with rain,
And we’ve got to keep the brake upon the wheels,
An’ ’orse’ll slip p’r aps he’ll rise again,
An’ p’r’aps lie’s down ’is head between ’is ’eels ;
Or if you stop us ’alfway up a hill,
It’s then we’ve got to use the whip and rein—
You never think the strain’s enough to kill
The horses, when they try to start again.
We never gets no “ thank you " nor reward ;
We never get no blooming Chris’mas tips ;
One thing we gets when we have lost a pard,
We gets some crape to tie around our whips.
“ ’Ow do we live ? ’ -we isn’t blessed with wealth ;
We can’t afford to act the blooming snob ;
/ keens the kid, the missus, and myself
n a weekly screw o’ six-and-thirty bob.

stamped. Name arid address must appear on MS.

tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 25, 1897.
Mi Dear Myee—
The evening-party given by the Chamber of
Commerce at the Town Hall, last Thursday, was
one vast hum. Guests crowded in, and it was a
race between music and chatter all the time, with
the music finishing a bad second. When the organ
pealed the jabbering ceased, but individual per-
formers seemed to be going through their efforts
in dumb-show. Some women stuck to their talk
even in the storm of Wiegand’s chords, and only
left off at last when the entertainment was over,
exhausted, but dauntless, in their attempts to talk
down the city organ. Violinists are bitter in their
complaints of the talking nuisance this winter.
At a crowded afternoon-tea, a soloist stopped
short in the midst of his runs, backed off the plat-
form without bowing, and retired abruptly; but
everybody was too busy chattering to notice the
sudden ending, and only heaved a sigh of relief
that the interruption to confidential conversation
was over. Another day, last week, I saw an
indignant tenor redden with wrath and walk off
with haughty mien, but his resentment was also
thrown away on an otherwise-occupied audience.
Cheap opera seemed to catch on with a first-
night house at the old Opera House on Satur-
day, when E. Farley’s co. put forward “ Mari-
tana.” That Spanish gipsy is getting a very old
girl now, but she still has her admirers. In the
full circle were numerous singers and musicians,
and also some evening-gowned persons, who gave
the theatre a fashionable tone. Miss M. Glen-
dinning, who played the heroine, has long dark
tresses, much too plentiful to be a wig, and a
graceful yellow robe in the last act; and Don
Caesar still wears those fascinating boots, with lace
hanging over the tops, that made him my child-
hood’s idol. A bewildering change of monarchs
occurs in this version. A little king patters about
in purple and black until the end, when he is sup-
planted by a tall despot in pink and silver.
The deadlock about the appointment of a suc-
cessor to Canon Kemmis at St. Mark’s, Darling
Point, arises from the old, old squabble of High
v. Low. A sassiety congregation always wants a
well-organised service with plenty candlesticks
and millinery and as much trimming to the service
as the pew-rents will allow. As the stipend is
only .£51)0 a year, out of which the late incumbent
paid a curate, the question so often asked why the
church doesn’t attract able men is easily answered,
for St. Mark’s is one of the plums of the diocese.
Rev. Mr. Bond, nominated of the parishioners, is
located at Devonport, England, and is strongly
recommended by many officers of the fleet whose
influence is strong at Darling Point; and it is no
secret that his nomination is due to the great suc-
cess at St. James’, King-street, of the recently-
imported Rev. Carr-Smith, of whom he is an inti-
mate friend. The Synod nominators (who unani-
mously oppose Mr. Bond’s election) include the
Rev. Pain, of Darlinghurst (uncle of Barry Pain,
the author). He is popularly supposed to “run ”
the diocese, as his tastes are “ low ” and agree
with the Bishop’s.
To make things hum m St. James’s parish, the
energetic Rev. Carr-Smith aforesaid, who is
depleting pews in adjoining churches, has hit upon
a new inducement to auditors by advertising for
ladies and gentlemen willing to rehearse Gregorian
music, a doleful kind of antiquated melody much
in fashion at present. This is the latest of the
many devices (including smoke-concerts, flower-
hunting expeditions and picnics) which the rev.
gentleman endeavors to utilise as means to correct
the worldly tendencies of his congregation.
The annual meeting of the Home Read mg
Union happened last week in the large hall of the
University. Home-readers appeared in weird
clothing, as a tule ; a fashionable gown was a bright
exception. Announcements were made at the
door ; one large cluster of damsels being loudly
heralded as “Miss Blank and party,” and about
two dozen sailed in as “ the party.” An American
girl wore her hair in that tantalising way which
only an expert can attain to without adding at
least a dozen years to her age. A dusky belle was
hung around with gew-gaws in the style of a
South Sea princess. An interesting discourse on
Australian poets was delivered by a University
grad. ; he told the story of a child who was re-
buked by her mother for using “ such terrible
language,” and replied: “It’s not terrible lan-
guage, mother ; it’s Australian poetry out of the
Reader.” The lecturer quoted some thrilling
passages from English poetry on flowers, and de-
clared that until like associations should arise in
connection with local plants, all Australians must
remain Peter Bells. He also remarked that it
would be hard to be romantic about the flannel-
flower until it should possess a nameless suggestive
of sore throats and rheumatism.
The charity ball is flourishing in the suburbs at
present to a very large extent, and, on the whole,
promoters have been eminently successful in
solving what is always a great problem at subscrip-
tion dances—the supper. Dancers pay five shil-
lings or half a guinea for a ticket and expect a
gorgeous spread with liquor ad lib. At a recent
festivity, owing to the warm evening and an un-
expected demand for refreshment, the liquor ran
out early in the proceedings, and the thirsty men
were hunting around only to find cold water or
black coffee. Warned by this misadventure, the
committee of the next ball laid in a surplus ; the
night was cold and rainy and the attendance
rather meagre. Consequently, much uneaten
“ tucker ” was left on hand. One of the observed
of this dance was a popular leading lady who had
dressed her hair in a way that broke the monotony
of the eternal high-placed, twisted knob ; her coil
was low on her neck and had roses pinned in the
twist. Bangs are still in favor : the parting and
the puffing over the ears in the ’Murkan style add
years to the wearer’s age.
Mrs. Brough’s costumes have been so often
marked for future adaptation by the ambitious
copyist in front that there will be a distinct loss,
sartorially as well as artistically, when that lady
departs. Her gowns in the “ White Elephant ”
give useful hints for everyday wear; the morning
robe of pink has a “ princess ” front, low-cut neck
adorned with rich-looking lace; the skirt of her
pearl-grey evening dress falls in many pleats, and
the bodice is trimmed with chiffon and general
glitter, and has flouncey sleeves. Miss fern pie
adorns the gay and frisky widow in bright red,
and gives her a gold-headed cane like the Dibbsian
present to the Queen. Lily Titheradge doubles
her age by sporting an evening dress of dowager
cut. The slim Ferris acts the part of a Hon. with
fidelity, and puts much manner into the way he
rubs the back of liis head when agitated.
A “ metaphysical ” episode has become the chie
talk of the moment, and is causing trouble in
the ranks of the believers in the mind-healing
doctrine. ..
The new wing of the Art Gallery was formally
opened on Monday afternoon by Gov. Hampden
(accompanied by Lady Hampden, in a neat black
frock livened with pink), who, with the trustees
and some of the Ministry, was railed off from the
other inspectors of the loan-collection on a little
raised platform. There were speeches and cheers,
and the Minister of Instruction, in an “h -less
address, thanked His Ex’lency and Lady Am den
for coming. Contributors to the hung pic-
tures received cards for admission before the
ceremony and gazed lovingly at the gems from
their own walls. One of the most admired por-
traits was that of “ Trilby’s ” sister. Most of the
old masters are kindly placed in a separate
room, to be avoided except by enthusiasts.
The largest picture in the show is one lent
by Miss Walker; it looks like an advt. for
somebody’s soap. The face of “a Hungarian
lady,” lent by the French consul, has haunting
Chinese-slanted eyes. The Union Club’s 'Vicat
Cole had a crowd around it all the afternoon. The
’Arbor, from every point of view, gave an indigo
tinge to many water-colors. Many of the pictures
are of the kind which “no gentleman’s drawing-
room should be without.’’ Some few are so good
that it is a pity they cannot remain in the Gallery
permanently. The Trustees are naturally much
gratified at the very liberal response to their invi-
tation, which turns out to have been admirably
conceived —for few people had any idea of the
variety and beauty of some of the canvases
which lurk shyly in the “ big ” houses of
N.S. Wales. In fact, the community has
awakened to wonder if it has really been
“artistic” all the time without knowing it.
After the opening the populace surged up the
steps into the new hall, and the crowd already in
surged back, and there was much crushing, which
the policeman on the staircase seemed to ignore.
The crowd was so large and shabby that it
appeared as if every dead-beat in the Domain was
seized at one and the same moment with an
intense desire to gaze at art.
It will doubtless cause much rejoicing among
the wives of the visiting colonial Premiers to find
that the ladies are specially thought of in. the
jubilee procession next month. The procession is
being rearranged with the object of giving them
all a view of Queen Victoria. The colonial con-
tingent will get a good ten-minutes’ start and
wait for the rest of the show in front of St. Paul’s,
while the Premiers and their wives will alight and
stand on the steps. After the service the pageant
will be again broken in two, the troops from this
ide bringing up the rear. They will thus be
effectually shut out from all the glory and march
along dejectedly as a second-rate little minor
pageant—all the excitement and whooping going
on in front with the royal cavalcade, which
will be about a mile long. The fag-end of the
crowd will be left languidly beholding “ only the
colonials. ”
Fashion journals give illustrations of the new
head-gear for the loyal; the “Jubilee hat” is
still uglier than the ordinary topper, and is said
to have been evolved by some geniuses belonging
to a London club. The crown (in the pictures)
rises conically, and looks as if it would be the
better for being sat upon and flattened to the usual
shape.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The few poor remnants of Melb. ‘ society ’
made quite a show at the Princess’s on Saturday
night. A party from Government House spread
itself over two boxes, the sleeves of Mrs. Free-
man-Thomas’s theatre - cloak completely filling
a whole stage box. Quite the best-dressed women
on view were some dozen Rachels and Rebeccas in
the d.c., who were scintillating with assorted
gauds and rustling with satin. One little woman,
whose hair covers the whole gamut of shades from
‘ sunshine yellow ’ to * raven’s wing ’ in a perplex-
ingly short space, looked like a French doll whose
features had ‘ run ’ in the moulding. In a stage
box two pretty girls, both looking like under-
studies of Francillon and crowned with vividly-
colored roses, resolutely turned their backs on
the stage, and gave the audience a most admir-
able picture of social loveliness all the
evening. ‘A Night Out,’ which sets forth the
troubles of the * Pinglee and Perger ’ families (as
the Australian members of the co. call the leading
male characters) is a * dizzy ’ French vaudeville
clad in a coat of whitewash —not a thick, winter
coat, but a light, summer ‘ duster ’ very thin in
parts. The jokes are of a kind which turn all the
women of the audience preternaturally grave and
make the men shout and then wonder what they
have been yelling at. Emphatically not a piece
for evangelical clergymen. As a dress show the
farce is disappointing, and the vast leading-lady
looks as if she had bought some of her clothes at
Booligal. When she first appears she wears
a tawdry blue satin and net robe, low-necked,
though it is supposed to be 9 a.m. The hlack-and-
steel evening-gown of Act 2 is smart, but by no
means fresh. In Act 3, Miss Alma Stanley looks
like a blazing hayrick in flame-colored silk, with
hat and parasol to match, and she actively demon-
strates what a dangerous color this is for blonde
women to wear, for her own fair face looks
violently yellow in it. The green and fawn gowns
worn by Mathieu’s four daughters would serve as
excellent models how to fashion garments for
girls during the difficult age—between 12 and 16.
The floppy felt hats they wear are pretty and
artistic, but their nightgowns are the most
“ actual ” garments of the sort ever seen on a
Melb. stage.
“ The funeral of the late only Victorian-grown
baronet was the grandest procession of the kind
yet seen in Melbourne. High-class military
funerals we have had in plenty, but one in which
soldiers, sailors, Masons, and the ordinary black-
draped civilians all joined is, so far, unique
locally. As the procession passed, one looked in-
stinctively for the hoary-bearded Druids and the
block-tin knights of the whitesmiths. The private
carriages were occupied by their owners, too, in
direct contrast to a recent grand funeral, where,
among the dozens of private carriages which fol-
lowed the procession, only two contained
mourners ; and no farce is more tragically-
comic than a stream of unoccupied vehicles fol-
lowing the dead. Two carts, much resembling in
form butchcrs’lorries, piled up with floral offerings,
followed hard upon the gun-carriage bearing the
coffin ; and the fragrance of these paraded blossoms
was apparent along the whole line of procession.
Six waggon-loads of beribboned wreaths and
crosses wended their way to the cemetery by
another and circuitous route, arriving there after
the burial and heaping upon the' f*?; ve waiting
rrnnv feet high. Among the crowds waiting
“Se one heard on all sides personal memo-
ries of the deceased’s openhandedness;. not a
tenth part of the man’s princely generosity got,
or ever will get, into print, At the cemetery the
Liedertafel men sang a moist and mournful dirg<
while the wintry rain baptised their bare heads.
Despite the gaudy military and Masonic trap-
nimrs the elaborate display of wired floral extra
vagance, and the gush of some of the newspapers,
there was a genuine ring of sincere and heartfelt
regret throughout the whole proceedings,
whom money can make rich and generous and y
leave them simple and unostentatious, as it did
‘ Big ’ Clarke the Second, are very rare.
“ The Melb. florists hit on an ingenious device
for advertising their customers’ a '
‘ gentility ’ at the same time. All Monday the
windows of the city flower-shops were c ro '™™
with wreaths and crosses, each bearing a hug
label inscribed with the donor’s name ™ large

small script. A discussion was going on before a

“What the portion of Melbourne gathered on
the housetops of Swanston-street, craning at the
windows, and o’erflowing the pave, to the loss of
dignity and perspiration of many police—noticed
most when Sir W. Clarke’s funeral cortege passed
them, was the very perceptible odor of camphor
that apparently exuded from the Freemasons
brigade. It was but too apparent by the wrinkles
in their garments, without the chemical essence,
that many of the sartorial masterpieces had failed
to see the sun for quite an age, and had that morn-
ing been lugged out of obscure recesses to do honor
to the day. The owners’ mournful looks at the
coming rain shower lent quite a grim humor to
the proceedings, which, after all, were scarcely as
dull as an Eight Hours procession.
“ You could not help contrasting the pompous
funeral of Melbourne’s only baronet with the last
tokens of respect paid to its only baron a few
months earlier. When Von Mueller was borne to
his grave a red flag tied to a black-draped pole
was the chief embellishment of the ceremony, and
public interest was mainly confined to a small
crowd of Flis compatriots. These gathered, weep-
ing, in the little back street at South Yarra, where
the poor old botanist had labored so long, whilst
a disconsolate German band blew the march from
‘ Saul.’
“It will surprise many Melbourne people to
hear that there was a lot of romance in the court-
ship and marriage chapters of the ‘ new baronet’s ’
history up to date. At the early age of 12 he
plighted his troth to the juvenile Miss Amy Cum-
ming, and the engagement was one of sassiety’s
small jokes. Anon amorous Rupert was sent to
Hingland for a university education, such as had
been given to his pa before him. But the young
stranger in a strange land sowed his wild oats
with so much vigor that he —well, he left the
ancient seat of learning of his own accord and
was having the highest of times in London when
some of the family friends lassoed him and landed
him once more on his native salt-bush. Amiable
Amy Cumming was by this time quite a ‘bud,’
and she cast off her boyish sweetheart on account
of his misdemeanours. Whereupon Rupert lapsed
into a mild form of melancholia, and the heart-
broken lad of 18 was prone to button-hole his
acquaintances and pour his opinion of the buxom
inamorata into their weary ears. When it was
arranged that a cousin (Tom Clarke’s son) should
take the love-lorn Orlando to Africa to go big-
game shooting, the thought of a rampageous lion
chawing up a ‘ future baronet ’ was too much for
his one-time lady-love. She forgave him, and he
decided to leave the lions in peace and get married.
“The late ‘W.J.’s’ second son, Ernest, was
also a bit of a ‘boy,’ like Rupert, and a Mel-
bourne cabby tells a comical tale of the youngster
in a state of rebellion, bolting down the street
with his worthy parent in full pursuit. Cabby
came to the rescue of the latter, and the chase re-
sulted in Master Ernie’s capture. He was finally
placed on board a training-ship in British waters,
and will soon blossom into a naval lieutenant or
something else with gilt buttons on it. First-born
Rupert is more effeminate than his younger
brother, and he speaks with a lisp and walks with
a limp. The dowager Lady Clarke’s eldest son,
Clive, died, as every one knows, just after passing
his military exam, at Sandhurst. Her surviving
eldest is another lame member of the family, and
of poor physique. Now that he is ‘heir-pre-
sumptive,’ however, he will look much larger than
before. The results of the Rupert Clarke-
Cumming alliance have been two girls only, the
eldest blind from birth.
“Bayles, the absentee respondent in the gilt-
edged divorce case, has been for years quite a
sassiety pet. Tall, lean, grey-haired, well-
groomed, blase, he claimed attention as one of the
distanwy figures of Melbourne ball-rooms. In his
new character of enfant terrible the young man
has still some fair supporters, who say—but never
mind what they say.

your indulgence,’ with an emphasis on the ‘ dulg,’
after which he dropped formality, and got along
very well with an ingratiating air. ‘Good old
.Shaft 1 yelled the gods, and the sentiment was
generally endorsed. It is astonishing how easily
an earl can appear a delightful person.
“ A wealthy Hebraic gentleman, booked for the
character of co-respondent in a divorce-suit to
occupy judicial attention next month, has gone off
for a holiday to a neighboring province. It would
seem that he is repairing his shattered nerves to
meet the legal foe, and yet they say that a few
hundreds out of his mighty thousands would have
hushed the whole matter up. To your tents O
Israel ! ’

The audience learned that the chief cause of th

spot. Not that he was naturally cruel. The one
thing needful to a man of Henry’s temperament
was afternoon tea. He wanted soothing.
