Mitchell and Co’s “Newspaper Press
Directory ” is, so far as Australasia is concerned
by far the best published. The 1897 edition fully
maintains the high standard of editions preceding.
The Bulletin finds Gordon and Gotch’s
“ Australian Handbook ” very valuable for refer-
ence—containing, as it does, many facts, figures
and maps which are put together in no other
handbook published. The issue for 1897 appears
to be uncommonly well arranged, and the accuracy
of the multitudinous details does editor and pub-
lishers very great credit.
Hamilton Mackinnon, fatally-shot at Mel-
bourne while larking with a friend and a loaded
revolver, was old enough to know better. But he
did it, all the same. The deceased, who will be
remembered by old-timers as a burly, Japanese-
looking young fellow who played good cricket on
the M.C.C. Ground, was in the Bank of Victoria
with Marcus Clarke, of whom he was an intimate;
he afterwards edited the Clarke memorial volume.
He was a cousin of the Argus Mackinnons, and
once edited the Advertiser at Wagga under the
proprietorship of the festive A. G. Jones.
The masterful Tozer, acting-Premier, is
going to pick out ten delegates to attend the next
Federation Convention on behalf of Queensland.
He declares that his Bill to that effect will be
before the Assembly within a few weeks, and
brags that it is bound to pass. Also, Brisbane
Courier has vaulted clean over another fence. It
says that Kanakaland’s chance to be present at
the Federation Convention must not be jeopar-
dised by popular election. It hoorays for selection
by Tozer.
Dear Bulletin, —Last week at the Con-
ference of the Chambers of Commerce (Sydney)
Mr. De Lissa read a paper on the codification of
Mercantile Law. Treating of the Companies Act,
he gave the following erroneous statement of
company law :
Until a very recent period, the latter end of 1895, it
was supposed that a trader or firm could convert his or
their business into a company with limited liability, by
complying with the formalities of the Companies Act as
to the formation of a company, procuring the signature
of a memorandum of association by the requisite number
of seven members, through the allotment to clerks or
other nominees of t‘l shares ; and many trading firms
have been converted by this means into companies, sup-
posed to secure limited liability to the members thereof.
A judgment of the English Court of Appeal (binding
upon the colonial courts), has decided that such a
transaction is a device to apply the machinery of the
Companies Act to a state of things never contemplated
by that Act, inconsistent with and opposed to its policy
and provisions. It is stated in the judgments that the
Act contemplated the incorporation of seven independent
bona fide members, who had minds and wills of their own,
and were not the mere puppets of an individual who,
adopting the machinery' of the Act, carried on his old
business in the same way as before when he was sole
trader ; and that to legalise such a transaction would be a
scandal. It was also held that the assumed sale by the
trader of his business to the company incorporated was a
nullity.
The writer was evidently referring to the now
celebrated case of Broderip v. Salomons and Co.
It appears that Aron Salomons formed a limited
co. to take over his business. The capital was
.£40,000 in £1 shares, of which 20,000 were issued
to Salomons, and one each to his wife, daughtei,
and four sons, the remainder being unissued.
The co. took over the business, the consideration
being the 20,000 fully-paid shares issued to Aron,
and .£lO,OOO debentures charging the co. s asses
in his favor. The co. ultimately went a ,
tion with debts amounting to over an
assets rather less. Aron, by virtue of his deoen
tures, claimed the whole of the assets, and oo
proceedings to enforce his security, “ikii i
Vaughan J., and then the Court of Appeal, o
held, as Mr. De Lissa says, that the formation ot
the co. was a mere device to enable tealoinf* 11 ?
carry on his private business with a hmi
liability contrary to the true spirit of the C
panies Acts and was therefore illegal; they dec'
that Salomons should indemnify the co. aga,'
its liabilities. Mr. De Lissa, however, ouiitte
state that the case was taken on appeal, in ■ •>
to the House of Lords, which unanimously
versed these decisions. Isn’t Mr. De Lissa ser
in itself a cogent argument for the codinca
which he was achoeating?-
RUS. ROBERT.


Discussing' the Date.
Many are in doubt
When the twentieth century begins.
Many are trying to figure out just when the
twentieth century begins. Some say it begins on
the first day of 1900, and others insist that it does
not commence until the first day in 1901. Now
there are many anxious to sit up the night before
and see the new century “in,” and for this reason
the exact date is desirable. Problematical dates
occur many times during one’s lifetime, and are
difficult of Solution. The exact date of the re-
covery of W. H. Mitchell, Esq., J.P., of Caster-
ton, when suffering from flatulenby and indigestion
he would be unable to give ; but it was soon after
he commenced taking Warner’s “Safe” Cure; and
in his own words he explains : “I deem it just to
my fellow creatures who may be victims to flatul-
enoy or indigestion, and the many ailments result-
ing therefrom, to tell them that I received
infinitely more benefit from a few bottles of War-
ner’s ‘ Safe ’ Cure than I did from the numerous
prescriptions prepared for me by the medical
faculty for years.” This important date will be
the same under similar circumstances ; and it is
easy to prove its accuracy. It is well worth the
effort of a trial.*

Queensland’s Attorney-General, who in-
vited himself to the Record Reign celebrations, is
now known as “ the Lap Dog.”
Now that W. J. Clarke has gone, Fitz Gi-
bbon is about the most popular identity in Mel-
bourne. Under the thin surface of good-humored
ridicule there is a general belief that Fitz. has
always tried to make himself useful. And people
feel grateful to the old bard, anyway, as a source
of innocent amusement.

Philip D. Lorimer, the “ sundowner poet,”
who lived for a time in the gorges of the Blue
Mountains to absorb the inspiration of the weird
scenery, is now, poor fellow, very ill in Rookwood
asylum. Paralysis this time.
Mr. Brewster, the old Australian, who
died recently in England, was, according to all
the Australian daily papers that noticed the death,
“ the nephew of the Right Hon. Sir Abraham
Brewster,” and so on. Not one of them men-
tioned Mr. Brewster’s father.
Ben Crisp, a Sleepy Hollow (M.L.) teetotal
nonagenarian, claims to have lived longer in Aus
tralasia than any other record-maker. He arrived
in Tasmania 78 years ago, and went to Otago
(M.L.) just 60 years since. He left England in
1819, and has not since seen his native country.
Mrs. Hunter-Baillie is dead. She was a
sister of the wife of the celebrated Rev. Dr. Lang,
and, being rich and very generous, became a sort
of lady Presbyterian Pope. Among other things,
she spent £30,000 on a church in memory of her
husband, and gave £2OOO to Sydney University.
A foreign vocalist, recently in Sydney,
made a point of demanding insertion of advertise-
ments in the two morning dailies whenever he was
to sing in any church. One R.C. priest refused to
advertise, and the wrathful singer wrote to the
choirmaster: “ Your Father , he is no
gentleman. I will sing for you, of course, but I
will personally kick his reverence.”
One Melbourne paper rushed out on top
of W. J. Clarke’s funeral with flaring, full-page
process blocks of Rupert Clarke, the new
“baronet,” and “his lady,” with all titles
attached, and further over, in the hind part of the
journal, printed a small, shabby picture of the
defunct society •• nabob. Exquisite taste ! The
baronet is dead, grovel to the other fellow.
Yet another S.M. Herald “ personal” :
Among the passengers by the Victoria, which arrived
on Saturday from Europe, via Ceylon, was Mr. Suther-
land Orr, of Madras, India. Mr. Orr is a nephew of Mrs.
Sutherland Orr, celebrated for her life of the poet Brown-
ing, a work that in biographical literature has attained

Well, suppose Mr. Orr is the lady’s nephew—
what then ?
At the banquet held at Bunbury (W A.)
to celebrate the first truck-load of stone tipped to
form the breakwater there, the father of Premier
Forrest was down for a speech. Now, it seems
that the old man is rather jealous of his son’s
fame, so he got up and said: “I hear of ‘John
Forrest ’ here, and ‘ John Forrest’ there, but what
I want to know is where the devil John Forrest
would have been without the old woman and me?”
Gov. Brassey has an especial fondness for
funerals. He was intended by nature for an
undertaker or cemetery chaplain, but, by some
mischance, was sent to the wrong address. It is
usual for a Governor to simply send his carriage
to a big, public funeral, but His Ex. goes himself,
looking lugubrious, with a black-edged handker-
chief in his hand. He went to J. B. Patterson’s
funeral personally ; he went to Clarke’s, and acted
as a pall bearer. His manner is so funereal that
he would prosper if he were to hire himself out for
corpse functions.
From 1841 to 1897, S.M. Herald has been
edited by ex-clergymen—Mansfield, West, Garran,
Curnow. It is understood that a lay Fairfax will
come next.

years. He was the first to establish a newspaper
on the goldfields.

enterprise. The Monaghans brought money into
the country, invested it in various directions, and
lost heavily. And now, just in the nick of time,
an old uncle dies and leaves the happy Monag-
hans heirs to a quarter of a million.

John George Richardson
Oakes, who died at Bathurst,
N.S.W., the other day, was
grandson of Chief Constable
Oakes, of Parramatta, in the
early days. His grandmother,
who died not many years ago,
aged 90, was the first white
woman born in N.S.W. It
was Chief Constable Oakes,
says tradition, who was asked
if he would swear to a cer-
tain matter. After hesitating
for some time he cheerfully
said, “Yes, I’ll chance it.”
“ I’ll chance it, like Oakes’
oath,” is an expression still
heard amongst some of the old
hands in Parramatta.

IN THE FAR WEST.
Mahlstick : “Bart. Clarke’s worst act was
giving Melb. Public Library those four hideous,
colossal, dough-dobbly, slobbery, sitting statues of
Queen Victoria, Prince Albert, Wales, and Alex-
andra. Summers sculped them in evident disgust,
merely for the cash. How delighted would this
genius have been had Clarke given him an order
for an allegorical group to emblemise Australia. ”
The Earl of Dudley, mentioned as the
likely successor to Brassey in Victoria, is said to
have insured his life for a million pounds, or so,
like his eccentric father before him. Old Dudley
started a craze for this form of gambling among
the British aristocracy. Heads—l live a long
time : tails —you lose. For the hereditary peer
who happens to find himself dying at an early age
there is satisfaction in the thought that his noble
corpse will administer a good hard knock to some
beastly commercial enterprises.
“Patra” from Melbourne to The Bul-
letin :
I lately saw a most gracious act performed by Jim Kelly,
eldest brother of Ned and Dan, and sole survivor of the
once notorious family. He, with some dozen others, was
waiting on a recent Saturday at a little wayside railway-
station when the train from Benalla drew up. A shabby,
draggled-looking woman nursing a baby, and with two
small children hanging to her skirts, got out and asked
how she could easiest get to diggings. The station-
master explained that she had come to the wrong station,
and that there was no possibility of getting to her desired
destination that night. The woman began to cry, and
explained that she was to have joined her husband at the
diggings, and that all she had in the world was three
shillings, and asked the S.M. if he would take her and her
children back to Benalla for that, “ though what we
shall do when we get there is more than I know,”
she wound up tearfully. The S.M. refused. I saw a
tall, bearded man of middle age tramp off the station and
make his way to the little store close at hand. He soon
returned with his arms loaded with parcels, then he
bought “one and a half second singles” to Benalla.
These he brought with the parcels to the weeping woman.
“Don’t fret, Missus,” he said, “here’s your ticket to
Benalla, and here’s rations that will keep you and the kids
for a few days. Go back to Benalla and then write to
your husband and tell him how it is you didn’t turn up.”
The woman broke into loud thanks, and just then the
Benalla up-train steamed in and the bearded man, taking
one of the toddlers fn his arms, bundled it into the car-
riage with its mother and then took his place in a “second
smoking.” Several people on the station well knew the
man to be Jim Kelly, and asserted, illogieally, it seemed
to me, that he was “kind-hearted like all the Kellys,”
and related several anecdotes of charity and kindness
performed by the members of this family.
Premier Seddon looked forward with the
keenest pleasure to meeting his old friend, Hori
Grey, in London, but at time of writing it is
doubtful if the great Pro-consul will be above
f round when the Maoriland leader reaches
ingland. Grey had a sincere admiration for
digger Dick, though they were not always on the
same side »f the House. A memorable scene, not
mentioned in Hansard and suppressed by the
papers, took place one night in the mid-eighties.
Seddon was acting as extra whip for the Stout-
Vogel Ministry, and an important division hung
on the vote of a Southern member who had been
on a gargantuan spree for a week —as a matter of
fact, the debate had been kept going for some
hours to give his watchers time to get him sober
enough to pass muster at division. At last they
thought he would “do,” and Seddon got “the
office ” and stopped the debate. The division
bells rang out, and in walked the boozer, not in
the custody of the emissaries of the Govt., but lean-
ing gracefully on the arm of Grey, who was leading
the Opposition. Seddon was thunderstruck, and
made a rush for the voter, who was being deftly
navigated into the “noes” lobby by Grey. An
unseemly struggle took place on the floor of the
House. Grey tugged one way, and Seddon the
other, and at last, amidst great excitement, Grey
secured the vote. Speaker O’Rorke looked on
sphinx-like and said never a word. When the
tellers brought up the division-lists, Seddon, pant-
ing with excitement, said, “ I draw Mr. Speaker’s
attention to the fact that there is a drunken man
in the House.” O’Rorke, who could always rise
to an occasion, said, in terrible accents : “ I see
no drunken man. Does the hon. member for
Kumara persist in the objection ? ” Seddon moved
as though to rise, when little Shrimski, knowing
that another word from the angry Seddon would
defeat O'Rorke’s benevolent subterfuge, and
effect the ruin of the drunken member, pluckily
garrotted the future Premier, while half-a-dozen
others, seeing Shrimski’s intention, took a
hand in the fun, and Seddon for the nonce became
the “mill ” for a pyramid of legislative “ sacks.”
“ Does the hon. member for Kumara persist in his
objection ? ” thundered forth O’Rorke, but as he
wasn’t bound to take official notice of the gurgling
of the half-suffocated Seddon, or his “d it,
Shrimmie, lemme up,” he proceeded to announce
the result of the division.
The surprising announcement that W. J.
Clarke had once been a private in the Vic. yeo-
manry cavalry paved the way for the further
revelation that J. S. Butters was his brother
warrior at the time. Therefore, if Butters, the
evergreen, would like to be carried to Melb. ceme-
tery on a gun-carriage, he has only to buy a piece
of artillery and die. Centuries, however, are
likely to elapse ere Butters will be ready for a
soldier’s grave.
Ricardo: “Alphabetical Clarke, W.J.T.,
father of the Bart, left W.J. about ,£BOO,OOO
worth, of which the personalty was comparatively
insignificant. W.J.T. was only financially con-
valescent, after being hit by the Colonial Bank,
or he would have left a good deal more. W.J.
immediately struck out boldly, capsizing his
father’s system, and in the ensuing 16 years, or
more, the estate went on gaining hand over hand,
despite what W.J. fooled away, in religion or
racing. In 1887 the current estimate of the whole
was a million and a half, swoopgd up to two
millions in the boom, a figure several times quoted.
Not being so much of a city man, Clarke did not
mushroom, or toadstool, like your Davieses and
Finks. Otherwise, he would be run up towards a
Vanderbilt. By reverse action;, the bust did not
knock him like others. Still, as Dogberry loqs /
‘ One that hath had losses.’”

Lusk’s School History standp N.S.W. over
£9OO to date, and is still “ under revision.” Of
the two principals—one (the author) has taken
himself out of the sphere of Australian history ;
and the other, Edwin Johnson, late Under-Sec.
for Education, has quitted all history, and rests
quietly at Waverley. The taxpayers and future

The vast fortune of the late W. J. Clarke
came for the greater part from his father, “ Big ”
Clarke, without doubt the shrewdest man that
s'eepy Hobart ever produced. Regarding the life
of Big Clarke some wondrously, silly stories have
been circulated, ranging between the generally -
accepted yarn that he was a sort of gentleman
farmer with a heap of capital when he landed, and
the whispered one that he landed in chains after
receiving a lifer for burglary at the Old Bailey.
As a matter of.fact, Big Clarke landed at Hobart
with his liberty! but very little else, and for the
first couple of .years in V.D.L. earned his living
driving a water-cart. Of frugal habits, old Clarke
at last amassed enough to open a butcher’s shop,
and in that shop he laid the foundation of his
huge pile. Clarke was a man 5 well off when he
came to Victoria, and he took little time in adding
to his riches. Rupert Clarke, who now takes the
cash and the title, is even more of a nonentity
than his late father, W. J. The one shining act
recorded of the new baronet so far is that he filled
the piano with champagne at his final bachelor
dinner.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The least that Premier Kingston’s enemies
wish him is a Privy Councillorship, and they are
willing to have another Assembly election should
it be G.C.M.G.
Twenty years ago, the population of Aus-
tralasia (including, of course, Maoriland and Tas-
mania) was 2,515,551, of whom 6561 were insane.
Now, according to Dr. Manning, N.S. Wales alone
has 3845 lunatics.
Papa Brunker, in the recent N.S. Wales
J.P. appointments, wrestled religiously with the
question of woman’s right to the post. He
admits that in many cases a woman on the Bench
would be an improvement, “but innovations are
so dangerous.”
Westralian Govt, has promised to give
Perth City Council .£2OOO towards its Jubilee
burst. What a vast and appalling headache Wild-
catland will have one of these days, when its
booms and bursts are all over, and there is nothing
left within its borders but empty bottles, and hag-
gard sobriety, and the echo of a weary curse !
A BUSH CUT.
“ Faith! an’ a noice guy Ned has made av yez, Paddy, wid
that hair-cut. Shure, look at all yer timples bare
thare! ”
N.S.W. Leg. Assembly, in carrying the
second reading of the Bill to reduce the Public
Works Committee from 13 to 7 members, has
barely saved its reputation. Even now it sticks
to 3gs. a sitting for the chairman and 2gs. for the
other members. Three times the Leg. Council
passed a better Bill, but the Assembly succeeded
each time in avoiding the subject. The Council’s
Bill reduced both numbers and emoluments. The
public demands a non-political board, but, in the
absence of this, the Council’s view of the needs of
the situation is more just and democratic than is
that of the Assembly.
The defeat of the Age’s nominee at Kara-
Kara (Vic.) election is possibly the first sign of a
rural revolt against David Syme’s dictatorship.
Young Dow, who ran the winner to a head at last
election, is the son of old Dow, and old Dow is
identified with the Age and its policy in the dis-
trict which he formerly represented. Young
Dow, this time, stood as the supporter of a suc-
cessful Government, whereas last time he was
merely the adherent of an untested George Turner.
The Age took his return for granted under these
promising circumstanoes, and yet the result showed
that Dow had lost 25 per cent, of his previous
support.
The leadership of the S.A. Opposition is
next session to be in commission. Downer, Solo-
mon, Honrburg, and Copley will act in concert in-
stead of Copley by himself.
Mr. R. D. Meagher (who expects to be in
next N.S.W. Parliament) is lecturing “ under the
auspices of N.S.W. National Protection union.”
This ought to be a big lift for the N.S.W. Protec-
tion union.
Sydney Telegraph, 19/5/97 :
Maclean, Tuesday.
Over forty Hindoos arrived by steamer yesterday for
Wyrallah, where hundreds of them are already located
growing cane.
And the Anti-Mongrel Immigration Bill is still
pigeon-holed in London, waiting Queen Vic.’s ap-
proval.
S.A. has now not a single pensioner, and
the only public servants entitled to pensions on re-
tirement are Chief-Justice Way, Judge Boucaut,
and Judge Bundey. There is about .£300,000
due, however, for retiring allowances under the
Act of ’7B, but the sums are not payable except
through the death or the withdrawal of the per-
sons entitled to the money, which mean while bears
4 per cent, interest.
The Jubilee business takes its most ob-
jectionable form in a perfect epidemic of shameless
cadging. Because a venerable lady in the monarch
line of business has, by dint of taking care of her-
self, managed to keep going for a protracted
period, quiet, inoffensive persons are stuck-up by
bishops, priests, doctors, philanthropists, editors,
judges, shopkeepers, professional beggars, gover-
nors, and other persistent persons, and are ex-
pected to show their joy by subscribing t» funds
innumerable, advertising the aforesaid, or finding
billets for them, or so on. Why should they ?
They can’t help the lady’s longevity.
As the N.S.W. brewers and
publicans know to their cost, the
casefor Freetrade —the assumption
that a tax levied on any article
is passed on to the consumer —
rests on a fallacy. For instance,
the Parkes-Burns Ministry in 1887
imposed a tax on beer, and in-
creased the spirit-duty from 12s.
to 14s. per proof gallon. Did Bung
pass these taxes on to the drinker
by increasing the retail prices ? No
fear. Prices never changed, even
up to now, and the squealing of
the “ trade ” proves that it is not
the consumer who pays the taxes.
The Queensland Labor-party
talks a vast quantity of unspeak-
able drivel re the Q.N.B. business
being left “ an open question,” not
part of the “Labor platform,”&c.,
&c. If the proper expenditure of
millions of public money be not
part of the Labor platform—if the
cleansing of corruption in high
places be not part of the work of
reformers—if democratic legisla-
tors can be allowed to split and
“ vote as they like ” on the most
important financial question of
the decade, then the attitude of
the Queensland Labor-party (or a
section of it) is intelligible, and
perhaps pardonable.
The direct Westralian Oppo-
sition won only eight seats, and
some of these by the barest
majorities. At North Perth the
single Oppositionist with 73 votes
got in four votes ahead of the
nearest of several Ministerialists.
At the Swan the Opposition can-
didate polled 116, while three
Ministerialists polled 91, 82, and
13. At Yalgoo lawyer .Moorhead
was defeated by Kenny, the poll-
ing being 56 and 48. The Govern-
ment secured several narrow vic-
tories, Lyall Hall downing the
Opposition big gun, Parker, in
Perth by 5 votes, while the Oppo-
sition candidate who held the seat
was defeated at East Freemantle
by 6 votes.

Sydney Telegraph’s very latest proof of
the ruin brought on Victoria by Protection is the
statement, in its issue of 19th May, that of the
Vic. population “97 per cent, are females.”
Ninety-seven percent. ! Ninety-seven females to
three males!! 1,113,641 women and 35,247 men !!!
Great Kangaroo !! ! !
Lyne, M.L.A., on the Federation Bill: “I
object to New South Wales being deprived of her
undoubted right to deal with her rivers.” This
would be quite logical but for one thing. So long
as the people along the Murray, Darling, Lachlan,
Murrumbidgee, Culgoa, Barwon, Namoi, Gwydir,
and almost all the other N.S.W. rivers, and the
Victorians along the Murray, Goulburn, Loddon,
Campaspe, &c., claim and use the right to drain
off their flood waters into S.A. territory, then the
whole river system is fairly the joint property of
the three provinces. If “her rivers’ are N.S.W.’s
sole property, then S.A. has a just claim for
damages when they are allowed to drown out S.A.
territory. No province has any more right to let
its private river do damage in its neighbor’s
country, than an individual has to let his private
dog do damage to the property of his neighbor.
If the waters are to be shared with S.A. in flood
times, when they do harm, S.A. has a right to a
share in drought seasons as well. Otherwise, the
best thing that province can do is to send along
a brief request: “ Please come and take away
your river.”
When S.H. Parker,Q.C.,
descended from Westralian
Leg. Council to contest an
Assembly seat, and to become
leader of the Opposition if he
got it, the natural assumption
was that the anti-Govt. Perth
Morning Herald would sup-
port him, and the West Aus-
tralian, the Govt, organ, op-
pose him. But the latter
mildly acquiesced in Parker’s
candidature, and only rebuked
him for some electioneering
pyrotechnics reflecting on the
credit of the province and the
Govt.’s conduct of Westralian
finances. The Morning
Herald, on the other hand,
repudiated Parker as the ex-
ponent of the ’Erald brand
of Opposition policy, and
threw at him all the scornful
language that could be drawn
from the editorial ink-bottle.
There was explanation of the
milk in each cocoanut. Herald
editor Lovekin wanted the
Council seat which Parker had
vacated, but the latter had
headed a deputation to George
Randell, who was vacating the
Perth Assembly seat in his
favor, asking him to contest
the Council seat, and promis-
ing him support. This crime
figured in the editorial indict-
ment as “insincerity to his
friends.” Parker in Council
had stood in the way of the
succession of Editor Hackett,
M.L.C., of the West Aus-
tralian, to the Presidency of
the Chamber when President
Shenton should retire. The
cat often jumps, apparently,
in the wrong direction in Westralia, but never
without reason.

A correspondent :
About the dummying' of homestead leases in N.S.W.,
which The Bulletin said the other day “goes on merrily.”
The fact is, many of the administrators of the Land
Acts are so much in sympathy with what technically is
dummying under the law, that they fear to come down
heavily in any, except the most extreme, cases. For
instance, the applicant always has to saj' that nobody
but himself has any interest in his application. Pretty
often that is tolerably close to perjury. A partner
HIS WEAKNESS.
SQUATTER JUNIOR : “ My dad's going to found your cows."
COCKY JUNIOR: “ Yah! that’s nothin’ fer him—he pounds
yer mother!”
in a business may be, often is, applicant ; and he looks
after the homestead lease as part of his share in the work
of the business. I know 30 such cases at the least. Or,
two or three members of a family take up adjoining
blocks and practically work them together. Each of them
would often starve if they didn’t. Land boards and
ministers shut their eyes ; and before they open them a
case of quite a different kind, but apparently the same
slips through.
Good old James Service, of Melbourne,
says he is against the Referendum because the
world has existed 6000 years without it, and that
is fair proof that it isn’t at all necessary. It is
almost a pity the depositors of James’s Com-
mercial Bank, when he appealed to them to recon-
struct that lamentable institution, didn’t reply in
his own words that the world had got on for 6000
years without reconstruction, so the defaulting
establishment had better shut up and James and
his colleagues proceed to explain in the usual legal
course how the trouble came about. Considering
how Service has twice been allowed to get his bank
out of the mire by a perfectly new and revolu-
tionary device, his jibe at the referendum because
it isn’t 6000 years old is a very dull and ox-like
piece of mirthfulness.
AN EQUIPOMOLOGICAL ITEM.
“ Why’s ’is bloomin’ legs bandaged?”
“’Cos ’e’s got the Codlin Moth!”
Contrast the platform of Q. Labor-leader
Glassey in ’93 and ’96 with the common-sense
policy he enunciated at Rockhampton the other
day. His, and others’, frothy demands for “ social-
ism in our time ” and other Lane-like visions cost
the party at least half-a-dozen seats,and much
useful sympathy. But for the ranting Glas-
seyites, the Boodlewraith would have: had to go
comparatively straight, whereas
The hopelessness in N.S.W. of expecting
even the simplest legal reform, where it clashes
with the prefits of solicitors, is shown in the three
abortive attempts, by legislation, to provide that
mortgages, under old system title, may be dis-
charged by a simple acknowledgment of the
mortgagee that he has been paid all claims under
it. This was intended to replace an unnecessarily
long reconveyance, for which from two to five
guineas was charged, according to its length and
the amount of the mortgage. For the short statu-
tory discharge, it was hopeless to charge more
than a guinea. Both Assembly and Council are
full of lawyers, surely capable of fixing up such a
simple matter. But, curiously enough, flaws have
been found in each, of the three legislative at-
tempts to effect this reform, and the old recon-
veyance is still in use—and charged for.
One reason assigned jwhy Aus-
tralia should join the Anglo-Japanese
treaty is that Newfoundland has come
in without any demur. Australia might
also come in without any demur if it
was a case of executing a commercial
treaty with Greenland or Paraguay or
Jupiter. Newfoundland is about the
only place on earth except Timbuctoo
that no Jap is ever likely to get
to.
In the Eastern Suburbs (Mel-
bourne) —the Pat-man electorate repre-
sented successively by Gillies and
Madden—the votes representing man-
hood are 541, and those representing
property—plural votes given for the
ownership of bricks and mortar, bridges,
trams, gas-mains, and other inanimate
property—number 800. It is about
time the Turner Government made an-
other vigorous attempt to pass a One-
man-one-vote Bill. Even if it takes
years to get it through the Council, it
should be passed by the Assembly, and
sent up to the House of Sargood every
session with awful regularity. Then
that House would be compelled either
to accept it or to advertise itself every
year (the only pity is that it can’t be
compelled to do it every month) as the
one stolid snag in the way cf reform,
and to keep its selfishness and fossilism
and scandal well before the public.
Queensland started its Parliament four
years after N.S.W.— 29th May, 18U0. The only
members of its first Legislative Assembly not
dead or forgotten are ex-Chief Justice Lilley and
ex-Premier Herbert.
The Dr. Fitzgerald who died in Cape
Colony lately at an advanced age, went to Wel-
lington (M.L.) as hospital doctor in Hori Grey's
early days. When Grey removed to the Cape
he opened an hospital there, giving it his own
name, and wrote to Wellington offering the resi-
dentship to his old acquaintance. Fitzgerald
accepted the appointment, and put in his working
life at the post.

In Chicago there are sold each week about
50,000 cattle, all by weight, too. It ought to be
a good place for auctioneers, but there is probably
a lot of competition, and perhaps Homebush
knights of the hammer are as well off. Last week
Pitt, Son and Badgery, Ltd., knocked down (not
literally) 1131 cattle and 21,477 sheep. *

A lady member of a big theatrical co. was standing in
the wings during the recent Melb. season, chatting with a
male member of theco. who is blessed with a large Roman
nose. Miss looked him up and down, and compli-
mented him on his costume—“ but,” says she, “ I really
wouldn’t wear that nose ; Mr. wears a nose, and
someone else wears one—there are too many noses alto-
gether.” “ But, madam, this is my own nose ! ” “ Oh,
no ! I've been too long in the business to be taken in like
that.” (Then the little talk ended).
Theorbo : “ Melb. demise : Armes Beaumont’s brother,
D.A., affectionately known as Dan and Dab, or Dabbv.
All his life he was a fair-haired, chubby boy, and nobody
can believe he was 54, Armes being now well on to 61).
Dan was emphatically the musical man of a musical
family, because he went in purely from taste, and was an
indifferent executant, anyway. Mrs. Fox, sister of the
Beaumonts, has quite dropped out of memory'. Armes
lifted her on to the operatic stage, but she was never at
home.”
A tip for Little Gulliver for jubilee time : Nature, in
building him, devised an extraordinary caricature of
John Bull in miniature. Let the widows’ mite decorate
himself with appropriate whiskers and the regulation
rig, and warble a written-to-order song as J. 8., and the
lo.val persons of this continent will take him to their
bosoms with a rapturous, suffocating hug.
Ed. Bulletin, —It is not correct that Card. Moran never
presided at an Australian christening until he operated
on the 48-hours-old baby of Mr. “ Mick ” Meagher. The
Cardinal christened one of conductor Hazon’s babies (a
girl) about four years ago. The ceremony took place in
St. Mary’s Cathedral, Sydney, with Lilian Tree and Dr.
Laure as sponsors. —T. B«-D.

Snazelle writes from Birmingham, where he appears
every Sunday night, to say that Australia will see him
again early in ’9B. It is not likely, however, that he will
screw enough courage together to face another Warrnam-
bool audience. Snazelle, in a clap of anger, roused by
interruption, told the people of the sleepy potato-
town that they were “not worthy to be entertained by a
member of the Carl Rosa Opera Company and a man of
brains.” Having cursed them collectively and indivi-
dually, Snazzy fell foul of the piano, and when the
audience departed he was praying for the strength of
Samson to wreck the roof upon their heads.
Dear Bulletin,—The revival of “ Romeo and Juliet ” by
Mrs. Brown-Potter has set one of your correspondents
speculating as to the best Romeo that has trodden the
Australian boards. On that point there may be some
doubt ; but as to the greatest Mercutio there can be none.
Brian Darley first, and the rest nowhere.—Roscian.
But for Hamilton Mackinnon, accidentally shot while
larking with a pistol the other day, Marcus Clarke would,
perhaps, not be quite so well advertised. After the
demise of the novelist, Mackinnon organised the series of
light opera appearances, “Child of the Regiment,” and
so forth, in which Mrs. Clarke tried to raise some money,
but failed. Nor was the Clarke cult strong enough to
bear her up financially when she appeared in a quite un-
known comedietta of Marcus’s authorship.
W. H. Williams, veteran Melb. tenor and printer, has
furnished the local ’Erald with an “appreciation” of
good old basso Angas, deceased, whose colleague he was
in the early ’so’s, at the Independent Church, Collins-st.,
Melb. The choir at that time included only two bass
singers—Angas and David Mitchell, father of Melba. In
’57, it seems, Angas joined the choir at Christ Church,
South Yarra, and remained there, the recognised musician
of the Sunday crowd, until a few weeks ago. In the ’6o’s
he and Williams were brother members of the “ Orpheus
appearing under engagement at the original
Vic. Exhibition and the Theatre Royal when Barry
Sullivan was king. Of Angas, as an artist, his old col-
league says that “he could not sing out of tune or in-
correctly, because he always learned his music by a strict
process of .reading, often having recourse to the sol-fa
method, in connection with which system he once stated
that when he learned it he called and knew each note of
the scale by a special quality of its effect on his mind, as,
for instance, the fourth being the weeping note. This
forms a good object-lesson for all learners, and many pro-
fessionals., He had an abnormal range, from the lowest
of the basso profundo to the upper tenor G., doing the
run in the ‘Benedictus’ of Mozart's ‘ Twelth Mass’with
facility.” , It was characteristic of Melbourne that the
daily press forgot to notice the death of this modest old
artist. He was buried at St, Kilda cemetery with full
musical honors, and the Metropolitan Liedertafel will pre-
sently erect a monument to his memory.
Marshall's Loronette, in which tit-bits of Australian
theatrical history are often popping up, lately reprinted
the cast of “ School for Scandal ” as produced at old Melb.
Princess’ in Sept., 1862. Probably there is no stronger
performance of Sheridan's comedy on record. J. C. Lam-
bert-reckoned quite equal to the elder Farren—played
Sir Peter Teazle ; Robert Heir and wife were Joseph Sur-
face and Lady Teazle ; Harwood appeared as Sir Oliver ;
Barry Sullivan as Charles Surface ; Mrs. Alfred Phillips as
Mrs. Candour ; Dick Stewart, senr., as Moses ; and Joseph
Jefferson in the little part of Crabtree. Two members of
the Potter-Bellew company figured among that notable
crowd of 35'years ago—viz., George Ireland, cast for the
youthful Careless, and E. Gladstone as Trip.
Melb. : “ I do not remember ‘ The Merchant of Venice ’
here since Bates’s Shvlock. A vinous man, he was found
dead in Royal Park early one morning, not without sus-
picion of foul play.”
H. “ I have been talking with a histrio who acted a
part to G. V. Brooke’s Shylock. He assures me G. V. B.
was very quiet in it, with no rant. So that reading was
not altogether invented by Bellew—and Irving.”
Haresfoot : “ As it strangely happened, I never saw
Mr. Bellew act, except the Marat abortion, until this
season, and am surprised at his moderation and natural-
ness in ‘ Francillon ’ and the ‘lronmaster.’ But his
beautiful Romeo is a lolly-stick for the maidens. His
blank verse is remarkable for a flattish mispronunciation
of certain vowels spread in a gilt-leaf way over the whole,
infecting every line, not exactly turning O or E into A,
but as bad. The same defect is dimly perceptible in his
prose. Having known him as a young man with his
father’s elocution, I cannot imagine where he picked this
up.”
H. : •• ‘The-Sign of the Cross’ is suggested by Massin-
ger’s superb play, ‘The Virgin Martyr.’ Antony, a vic-
torious young Roman General (not Cleopatra’s, of course),
falls in love with Dorothea, a beautiful Christian. He
rejects the love of the Emperor’s sister, wherefore an
emissary is employed to ruin Dorothea in the most
effectual way, but retires baffled and converted. Theo-
philus is chief persecutor. He flogs his own grown-up
daughters on the bare backs for turning Christian, and
sends them to execution. After this he is himself con-
verted, and gladly goes to the stake. Dan Barry, please
note.”
They were dear, giddy, girlish, gushful things, and they
were among the “ gods” at the Princess’s, where, in these
degenerate days, society does sometimes go with an old
dress and a young brother. The play was “ The Merchant
of Venice.” All the evening Curly Bellew’s legs had fas-
cinated them as a wilv serpent the silly hen, and little
feminine thrills and whispers passed amongst them, whilst
younger brother growled because he couldn’t hear the
words of the actors. Presently the eldest sister could
contain herself no longer. “His legs are simply divine,’
she said, incautiously lifting her voice. “I don’t see
much in them,” growled the young brother ; “ Mrs.
Potter's ain’t much, but I’d sooner have ’em than his.
Then the sisterhood scowled on him, and called him a
wicked boy, and threatened to beat him with their pro-
grammes.
Rickards, finding himself cold and out of sorts when he
got to London this journey, tripped to Port Said and back
again before settling down to business.
Scenic-artist Harrv Grist is chanting “Gorsave” in a
business tone of voice, for the Jubilee brings grist to his
mill. One of his illumination jobs is a £3OO order from
Melbourne Stock Exchange, the central feature of the
design being a portrait of Her Gracious, 14ft. high, and
upon this wickedly misleading work Harry is piling pig-
ments regardless of expense.
Dr. Pat. Maloney, the Collins-street physician, vouches
for it that Gaiety girls suck red lozenges of a particular
composition in order to make their tongues of a nice pink
tint, which adds to their prettiness when singing or talk-
ing on the stage. What a Gaiety Girl’s tcngue would be
like (considering suppers, &c.) without a little artificial
tinting, the doctor hesitates to explain.
The founder of that popular place of amusement, now
known as Sydney Tivoli Theatre, passed away in Sydney
Hospital, from an affection of the lungs, on 9th inst.
Thurlinius Johnson erected and opened in the sixties, on
the site of the Tivoli, the gay and giddy Scandinavian
Music Hall, in which a remnant of the fast-disappearing
cabbage-tree mob, and the bucks and baldheads of the
town, used to revel in a “ free-and-easy.” No charge was
made for admission to the show, and the guests seated at
small tables were supplied with wines ana spirits or light
refreshments by girls attired in fancy costumes with very
short skirts.
Rose Musgrove’s bright performance in “ Lelamine ”
has, naturally, been over-valued by people who didn’t
realise the dullness of its surroundings. The damsel
showed a general aptitude for comic opera work as far as
she went, but she wasn’t put to a severe test in any par-
ticular line of business. To speak of her as a capable
successor to Nellie Stewart, on the mere “Lelamine”
evidence of her smartness in a soubrettish character, is an
absurdity.
Perhaps the crowning point of Lily Langtry’s stage
career—she is now 45, by the way—is her recent burlesque
on divorce. She got unhitched in California on the
ground of her husband’s desertion and neglect to main-
tain her. Think of any man trying to maintain Lily
Langtry ! Gilbert and Sullivan have nothing to equal
this.
Pattie Browne returns to Australia with some purchased
plays in time to open in Melbourne before the Cup. She
is slight and sprightly as ever.
Melbourne Town Hall organ is harder to handle than a
stump-jumping plough. David Lee, just dead, was a
large, athletic person, and could cope with it for an hour
or so without suffering serious injury, but the average,
slim, tender, musical person might as well go pile-driving
for his daily bread as attempt to subdue this malignant
instrument, and as it will cost £2OOO to fit the organ with
the necessary labor-saving machinery to make it tract-
able, the Council has resolved to await the appearance of
an organist who can thump like Fitzsimmons and pedal
like Megson. Applications, accompanied by chest, biceps,
and calf measurements of applicant, may be sent to the
town-clerk.
Grattan Riggs’ claim to be the only pro. who has walked
from Hobart to Launceston is, unfortunately, disputed.
“Pinero and H. A. Jones have broken out again simul-
taneously with new plays. Pinero’s—“ The Princess and
the Butterfly ” —is the fantastic tale of a weary baronet
and tired widow princess—the one having grown middle-
aged in respectable dissipation, whilst the other devoted
the best years of her life to nursing an invalid husband—
who mutually agree to turn the matrimonial question
over in their minds for one calendar month. They are
not warmly attached to one another, but they think they
might get that way after due notice has been given on
both sides Then the baronet finds a sudden affection for
a girl young enough to be his daughter, and the princess
accepts the hand of a johnnie almost young enough to be
her son, and the curtain falls. It is said to be the queerest
of plays, charmingly built and furnished with dainty
dialogue.
H. A. Jones, serious as usual, makes the hero of his new
play, the Physician, a specialist in nerve disease. A beaute-
ous girl persuades the specialist to undertake the cure of her
jianci, who lectures on temperance and suffers from some
mysterious affliction. She wants to have him cured of
the affliction that he may rail at drink all the louder, for
she likewise is an enthusiast in the Good Cause. The
specialist, in the first place, loses his heart to the beauteous
one, after which he discovers that her pet apostle is a
dipsomaniac in lamb’s clothing. In a self-sacrificial spirit
he attempts to reform this miserable impostor on the
strict Q.T., but the fellow goes forth upon the burst, and
the halo of sanctity drops from his swollen head ere he
succumbs to a surfeit of rum. The girl, glad to be quit of
her first love, bestows herself upon the noble-minded
medical man, and probably becomes a moderate drinker
after marriage. Critics speak well of “The Physician.’’
The new bridge connecting North and
South Brisbane will be ready on Ist June, but the
aldermen of the two councils have decided to defer
the opening until June 22. The horrid incon-
venience is to be endured for an unnecessary three
weeks, so that various loose slabs of unctuous
grovel may be dealt out to the aggrieved residents.
J. B. Castieau, clerk in the office of the
Vic. Chief Commissioner of Police, has won the
A.N.A. prize of .£5O (open to amateurs only) for
an essay on Australian literature. J. S. Jones, of
the Argus office, has captured one for a similar
amount on industrial development in Australia.
Alex. Sutherland and Tlios. Palmer were the
judges, but were unable to arrive at a unanimous
decision on the first prize, so Prof. Morris was
called in as referee with the above result.
Sydney Truth asserts that the foolish zeal
displayed by N.S. W. Crown Law authorities, in
recently prosecuting it for seditious libel, was offi-
cially snubbed by Joe Chamberlain himself.
According to a Canadian paper, Chamberlain, as
the result of a Cabinet conference, cabled out to
the Reid Government a dispatch to the following
effect:—
Her Majesty’s Government deprecated the prosecution
of persons for seditious libel such as that against John
Norton, the editor of Sydney Truth. While such articles
as he publishes are to be discouraged, his prosecution is
ill-advised, especially as similar articles are continually
appearing in England without prosecution, or even excit-
ing more than ordinary comment. Advise no further
proceedings he taken in the matter.
This despatch is alleged to have been cabled to
Sydney, and duly burked by the loyal Ministerial
Sup-press.
Western Table Talk, a Perth society
journal, obituarises the late Gov. Robinson
Sir William Cleaver Francis Robinson, K.C.M.G., has
passed away to realms above. Western Australia is in
mourning—it has lost its parent, its patriot, its pride. . . .
We admired our departed friend for his gubernatorial
ability, and loved him for his delightful social and home
life. . . . Sir William Cleaver Francis Robinson, K.C.M.G.,
was in life dear to Western Australia, and in death his
memory is cherished with a love that is ever binding. If
it were possible to learn the last words of the departed,
it would be little speculation to guess that they were in
eulogy of Western Australia. For, as we loved him, so
did he love us. We deplore the temporal removal of Sir
William from us ; for we believe that —
“ He is not lost ! though closed those lustrous eyes,
Though mute those lips and cold that classic form.”
Fortunately, newspapers never circulate on the
banks of Styx.

' Writes “ Scotty the Wrinkler ” under the
caption “The Other Side ” Editor Bulletin,—
We, you and I, are on this side, what is on the
other side of that high wall? I’ll tell you. A
shed in which a man is lying, his head upon his
grey jacket of dog’s-wool and oakum ; and nobody
cares ; only two men seem to know that the man
is dying. lam one, and remnant of a
great horseman and cattleman is the other. What
does anyone gare ? I care a little, because I kneW
the man when few could meet or beat him in
the field or elsewhere, and I hate cruelty to
animals by animals, whether the former be human
or otherwise, knowing that the latter generally
hold the form of man. I cut out portions of the
newspaper version of this most scandalous affair
and give my opinions also:
The morning after his admittance to Wagga Gaol
Edward Dwyer, aged 52, ill and weak, was seen by the
visiting surgeon, who recommended hospital treatment
and this was continued until he died on Sunday last ’
the prisoner was carefully attended to, and the instruc-
tions of the doctor were thoroughly observed.
The fact is that the man got one pint of milk when
he could no longer swallow it, and died on a mat
like a dog.
In reply to a bond juryman, the witness stated that the
man was more a subject for a hospital than a gaol.
A bond juryman asked if witness was aware when the
deceased was admitted that he was given a hot bath in
the morning and a cold shower again in the afternoon
which made him cry for mercy'?
The visiting surgeon to the gaol stated that he saw the
deceased the morning after he was admitted, when he
found him to be suffering from disease of the heart and
dropsical swellings, the result of chronic disease. He pre-
scribed hospital treatment and ordered medical comforts,
which he deemed requisite. The immediate cause of
death was failure of the heart’s action through weakness.
The last is a capital joke: my heart of hearts
would also/ai7 if I had “ disease of the heart and
dropsical swellings, the result of chronic disease,”
especially if I got a hot bath and then a cold
shower until I “cry for mercy.” Now I know
what medical comforts are : a mat to lie on, one
ounce of sugar a day, and nameless, tealess tea,
besides which there was beef-tea when the man
was practically dead.
A bond juryman asked if the beef used to make beef-tea
for deceased was not taken from the allowance of Soz.,
which each prisoner was entitled to receive. The deceased'
he said, died on a mat, and with only one blanket.
Witness said the cook made beef-tea from the meat
which was always over after each man’s allowance had
been deducted.
The jury, after retiring into the trial-yard for rome
time, returned a verdict that the deceased had died from
natural causes, and that death might have been accele-
rated by insufficient accommodation.
The reporters oust the fact that the “bond”
juryman asked more than that and that the
surgeon tautologued : “You can have a post-
mortem if you like. I did what I con-
sidered proper for a prisoner,” and finally
refused to answer the “ bond ” juryman, one
of the six prisoners called by the P.M. “ the
other side.” You must know that the six free
jurymen sit on one side of an open staircase and
the “ bondsmen ”on the other. There’s much
more if I cared to say it. I stayed, not slept, in
the single cell tenanted by an epileptic and the
dying man and myself. The papers called it
10x10 feet—it is 11x0. I measured it. December
in Murda Murda is hot enough in such a coffin,
for such it became soon after the frame of the
anthropoid was carried into it. I invite the atten-
tion of the chief medical officer to this matter,
SOOTTY THE \VHINKLEII.
Capital is Labor’s elder brother.”—
Parsons. And under the J. Bull law of primo-
geniture the elder brother takes the whole estate,
doesn t he ?
Suicide by the inhalation of ordinary coal-
gas now amounts to a craze in U.S. A. Eight deaths
from this cause were recorded in New York city
in one week last Eeb.

T. Relph made a successful attempt on the
Ya. and J4 mile Australian records last week at the St.
Hilda track, Melbourne. The new times now stand at
24 4-5ths sec. and 51 3-5ths sec. respectively. Relph was
paced by the Dunlop team, and rode Dunlop tyres.*

The Eye-sight measure per post by the in
ventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough, the
well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney, enables
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection—
long sight, short sight, old sight. Full particulars
from Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
If the cabled news of his death be correct,
Charles Cavill dropped out just as Capt. Webb did, in
attempting a feat too big for his strength. Champions in
every field are continually pushed forward by the neces-
sity of beating their own records, and by-and-by they
touch climax, and another human fly is flicked off the
wall of the Impossible. The public is a hard taskmaster.
Do something better than ever it has been done before,
and the public sighs sa.isfaction-and clamours dissatis-
faction if next time you do not eclipse your own best, bo
Stevenson had always before him the spectre of his
most successful book; so diver Peart felt the popular
pressure urging him to put a little higher, and a little
higher, and a "little higher the platform from which he
leaped to his death. This yearning for better things is a
fine thing for the race, but it’s mighty tough on the
individual.
Charles Cavill was apparently trying a 28-mile
swim along the beach near ’Frisco ; and at present there
are only surmises as to his end. Previously he had swum
across the Golden Gate, ahd around the Sea Rocks—feats
never before accomplished—and on one of these occasions
he acknowledged that before he finished one of his arms
was paralysed with cold. ’Frisco waters are bleak swim-
ming almost any month of the year. Cavill at his death
was champion of America —having won the honor from
Yankee professionals on his last visit a few months ago.
General sympathy is felt with the fine athletio family from
which he springs.
» * *
The Inter-University eight-oar race, rowed on
the Parramatta River on Saturday last, proved merely an
excursion for the Sydney crew, who took the lead at tne
start and won from end to end. The other eights, Mel-
bourne and Adelaide, were poor to a degree. Adelaide
pulled a plucky race, and seemed to throw some life into
their efforts, but Victoria did not appear able to put
enough weight into their work to keep themselves warm,
and finished up as they started, rowing in perfect form.
Sydney was a tip-top crew thrown away—their opponents
were unable to extend them.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, — Be Dave Riddle and his
father’s horse Streak-o’-Light. Young Riddle is, after all,
not the first lad 12 years of age to take a horse over a
6ft. and jin. jump. The well-known little jumper High-
lander, who delighted thousands of Victorians at the
Royal Show, in Melbourne, some seven years ago by clear-
ing 6ft. 3iin., was ridden by Master Frank O’Leary, a lad
of 10 summers. It was a wet day with a greasy take-off,
too. I saw it mvself, but, to be sure, have gotthese facts
vouched by Mr. Tom Hart, who then owned Highlander.
“ Honor to whom,” &c.—Three Railer, Wangaratta.
*** ' *
The silliest of all silly sports might have re-
cently been seen in full blast at Manly. The paper-chase
of schoolboydom is natural enough, but becomes ridicu-
lous when old and middle-aged adults take part. If
grown people want to run across country, they can do so
without “ hares ” or “ scent ” —though at best a man loses
all dignity under such circumstances. On the occasion
referred to, a harrier, rising fifty, was allowed to win a
score of younger and faster rivals agreeing to lag behind
and give the genial “old ’un” a chance for once. Yet
that venerable and much-loved party looked as pleased at
the finish as if Deerfoot and Ridley, Richards and Lang,
Cummings and George were struggling in the rear.

Surcingle : “ The demise of Davis—Sam—re-
moves a bit of a lion from Byron Moore’s path. Sammy
knew all the ropes, not only as a Borough Councillor of
Essendon and Flemington, but also as ex-jock, and horse-
trainer. He was Byron’s prime foe m that deep-laid
scheme for charging to the Flemington Flat, and thus
getting even with Caulfield.”
Dear Bulletin, —You stated that Bevan, who
was unsuccessful in the long jump at the recent Eng-
lish Inter-University Sports, jumped a had third to
Roseingrave at the Championship Sports held at St. KUaa
last Nov. As secretary to that meeting I desire to say
that you have been barking up the wrong tree, for Bevan,
who jumped at Oxford, was not a competitor at the St.
Kilda Sports, and never jumped against Roseingrave.—
James W. Doyle, ex. Hon. Sec. V.A.A.A. Melbourne,
April 26,1857. [Was it his brother ?]

The Macleay River (N.S.W.) aboriginal cricket-
team, having won the Hart Challenge Cup two seasons
consecutively, become permanent owners of the trophy,
and what to do with it puzzles them. One gfey old coot
suggests to “fill it whisky and drink urn! Old King
Billy, however, gives out that it be “ sent alonga Queen
Wikitoria.”
• * * *
Sydney “silver” gamblers growl because the
totes, even those for a long time conducted squarely, are
getting “ strong” and say that faked results are now
more the rule than the exception.
* * * *
The Sydney stipendiaries announce that, after
June Ist, anyone convicted of running a tote will be fined
at least £5O, and, for any further offence, the prescription
will be six months’ hard. Also, something similar is to
happen anyone found laying the odds publicly. Chest-
nuts 1
* * * *
The blackmailing of Sydney tote-proprietors by
the police may be taken for granted. To live by illegal
gambling is to have the profits sweated by ‘ guardians of
the pieces.” In London, Manchester, or any other English
city where bookmakers drive a quiet commission business
in sequestered offices, the first thought of the bookmaker
every morning is—how to get to business without being
greeted by the policemen who await his coming at various
points along his likely lines of route. Their salutations
have to be answered with a “ tip ’’ —such is the unwritten
law of the Bleeds and Pursians, which altereth not.

“ At Surrey Bicycle Club spring meeting the
perpetual Challenge Trophy, presented by Sydney B.C.
for the Half-mile Scratch, brought out 21 riders, writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent. The Oval
irrass track was soft and also rough through recent rains
and patchy turf, and the times for the four heats were
from lmin. 22sec. to lmin. 36 2-ssec. J. M‘W. Bourke, a
medical from St. George’s Hospital, won the fastest heat,
and the final with lmin. 20 2-ssec. He deserved it, for
although a slabby stripling, he rode pluckily and all the
time. As usual, most of the races were cab-horse crawls
with the brief final flutter of the hired - horse when
within sight of the stable. 0. F. Barden won the pro.
880yda. handicap from scratch in lmin. 16 4-ssec.—slower
than his heat.”

* * * *
Victoria’s insistance upon rowing a cash cyclist
in its eight-oar-crew may have an effect upon the pro-
posed visit of a crew to England. The British amateur
definition is stringent to a degree, and will, without the
shadow of a doubt, debar all the oarsmen who this year
rowed with, or against, the cash cyclist. Victoria, wmcn
was so keen upon sending a crew to England this year,
cannot be expected to grow hysterical over the pro-
posal for next year, with its best eight debarred from
forming portion of the crew.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin,—The suggestion that ‘‘big,
solid bookmakers” in Melbourne are panting for the tote
to be legalised, because “a ring exists among the small
men to offer immensely long odds on a horse they have
stiffened, thus turning aside money that would be legiti-
mately laid,” &e.. &c., is the merest nonsense. Immensely
long odds are never offered about anything in this part of
the world. And everybody who knows anything about
the matter is aware that the biggest bookmakers were the
most determined opponents, in secret, of the Tote BUI
the last time it came before the Vic. Assembly. * e X? ra f
anti-tote letters appearing- in- the Melb. dailies at that
time were written by a barrister who was,.the myrmidon
of a well-known Ikey Mo.— Melbourne.

When Spalding’s baseball team visited Aus-
tralia, nearly nine years ago, people thought we would
Boon be as prominent “on the diamond ” as in other de-
partments of sport. But we learn that the Australian
baseballers now in the States have been easily defeated
in every' match so far played.
* * * *
“Breaker” Morant’s annexation of the buck-
jumping* prize at Hawkesbury show was attended by a
scene just like that embalmed in Banjo’s “ Amateur
Rider.” “ What, Mm goin’ to ride the buckjumper ! /urn,
a newspaper Johnny ! ” was the scornful remark of a
Hawkesbury native. But “The Breaker” fairly aston-
ished those natives, and after the ordeal he could have
swum in free whisky for a week.
» * * •
Ranjitsinghi is proving himself the greatest bat
of this or any other age, and if he comes out with the
English team next season, the various grounds will be
hard pressed for room to hold the crowds. To hope to
bowl the Indian on such a wicket as Ned Gregory pro-
vides on Sydney Association Ground will be hopeless
indeed. The oniy chance to get him out will be with the
veriest of “ soft stuff,” to give the fieldsmen a chance, a
course of action too much neglected by our captains.

W. L. Kerr, the champion amateur cyclist of Aus-
tralia, who sailed from Sydney by the R.M.S. Oruba
on February 27, has commenced training.
Great Scott! how will these papers find room to express
themselves if Kerr should happen to win a race presently ?
* * * *
Returned members of Giffen’s cricket-team re-
port that Perth is the thirstiest place on this continent,
and easily leads Coolgardie in drunkenness. Perth is full
of all the failures and dreadful examples of aristocratic
English families, and one cannot happen down any of its
streets in early morning without finding an assortment of
faded British swelldom asleep in the gutter.
• * * *
Jerome Murif, of Broken Hill, who has ridden
across the continent from Adelaide to Port Darwin on a
bicycle, is an Irishman, 34 years of age. He did the
journey in pyjamas, which he had found to be cooler
than knickers, and rode all the way with a revolver slung
on his handlebar. The most remarkable thing about the
journey is that he undertook it entirely “ on his own” re-
gardless of the fact that, had he chosen, he might have
earned two or three hundred pounds to do the journey on
behalf of a firm of makers. He did not even receive a
free bicycle, though, when he returns, he will probably
have the price of his wheel refunded by the agent, who only
discovered, when Murif had half-completed his journey,
that the machine had been bought at his shop.
• * »
Dear Bulletin,—Died in New Guinea, recently,
Peter Sullivan, the almost-famous Sydney boatbuilder.
Beach has to thank Sullivan, and his partner, Joe Don-
nelly, for giving him the chance to become champion
oarsman of the' world. When Beach came to Sydney a
raw countryman, Sullivan and Donnelly, whose shed was
then at Glebe Point, took him in hand, found training,
boating, and backing for him to row black Reynolds,
George Solomon and others in light skiffs. S. and D.
were the men who discovered the latent talent in the
after champion, and it was only when Beach’s success as
an oarsman was assured that others took him up. The
first time I saw Beach, he was taking a shower, after a
row, in Donnelly’s shed, and I was so struck with the
man’s magnificent physique, and the determined cut of
his jaw, that I could not help remarking to Sullivan,
“ What a pugilist thrown away !”—W.
* • • •
The S.T.O. Birthday racing was a decided
success, though the first day the attendance was small,
and the two chief events were won by outsiders. The
Royal Stakes winner, Devoted (Splendor-Devotion), had
performed ignominiously in a minor race at Moorefield a
week before, but, well ridden by young Frank Fielder,
she beat the well-backed cracks, Hopscotch and Delaware,
her nearest opponents being the country mare Semiramis
and the two-year-old Kilt. Why Delaware was not
fastened on as a “ good thing” for Birthday Cup, despite
his defeat in Royal Stakes, is a mystery, as he is perhaps
as good as anything in N.S.W. at a mile and a-half, and
here he was handicapped at 31b. less than w.f.a. Making
nearlv all the running he won a good race from the two
outsiders, Talavera and Glendennon, the hot favorites
Rebel Queen, Cremorne, &c., finishing down the course.
The consistent but unlucky Kilt (Lochiel-Mantilla) was
rewarded with a win in the May Stakes, starting at 6 1.
By winning the Turf Club Handicap on Saturday, the
Chester horse, Projectile, incurred a 51b. penalty for the
Cup, in which he ran a good fourth; he is improving
rapidly.
The scratching of the much-fancied Aurea, The
Tola, Precaution, and Crfime-d’Or forS.T.C. Royal Stakes,
and Hindoo for Birthday Cup, was a bitter blow for
Sydney backers; and when Aurea and several of Hor-
dern’s well-backed representatives went down much de-
light was expressed thereat. Mr. Hordern’s colors were
regaining their popularity, but the “Hindoo” trick has
again relegated them to the limbo of execration. If
owners and trainers think they can bamboozle handi-
cappers by such capers they are mistaken, for, as in the
case of Cartridge, &c., weight will be put on, not taken
off. To protect the public, a rule should be passed
making it compulsory that all horses not struck out of a
race two clear days before date of running should go to
the post in default of a very valid excuse for their absence.
For the past month Delaware has not done a
fast gallop over a longer distance than 6 furlongs, and yet
he won S.T.C. Birthday Cup (H mile) from start to
finish. Tricolor did no Sydney Cup preparation, but he
won all the same. Oases such as these
cropping up, and yet trainers will persist in galloping
their horses’ hearts out on the track.

After winning Newmarket Stakes (Eng.), Galtee
More has become a hotter favorite than ever for Eng.
Derby run next Wednesday, and, barring accl l*® n^s ’,"l e
will start the greatest favorite on record. The odds

By easily winning the Doveridge Handicap at
Derby with 9st. 101 b., the Irish-bred Clorane proved him-
self the best handicap horse seen in England since
Isonomy was at his best 17 years ago. When the mail
left, his future engagements included Queens Prize,
Kempton, lOst. 21b. ; Jubilee Stakes, lOst. ; City and
Suburban, lOst. 61b.—feats beyond Isonomy. Taking the
line through Paris, Clorane must be better than Carbine
or Abercorn at their best, but the test is not reliable, for
the English Paris may not be the animal familiar to Aus-
tralians—very likely is not by a long way.
• » * *
Wray, the Shoalhaven (N.S.W.) sculler who
went to England with Stanbury last year, won his first
match on May 3, when he easily beat Englishman Haines,
the conqueror of Charlie Stephenson over the Tyne course
on 20th March last. If Wray is as good as is generally
supposed here, Gaudaur should prove about the only man
on the other side with any show of extending him.
* * * *
The fact that there hasn’t been a drop of water
in the Castlereagh (N.S.W.) River for the last five months
draws special attention to the conspicuous notice near
Coonamble bridge, cautioning people against “fishing in
that river with nets.” Fishing with spades would be
more appropriate just now, and, by all appearance, for
some time to come.
* * * *
Peall’s second victory over Roberts makes the
merest shade of difference in their relative handicap posi-
tions. If the winner were willing (which he isn’t) to
accept a 47i per cent, allowance, instead of the full 50
per cent., the champion might be expected to “ take him
on ” again for a very big stake. In the course of their
two spot-barred matches Roberts scored 46,973, whilst
Peall made 24,000 points. At this rate, Roberts would be
freely backed to give the little man 11,400 in a game of
24,000 up. And yet Peall is the second best player in
England.
* « * *
Giffen’s eleven experienced some of the delights of
life in the golden West during their match with Kalgoorlie.
The cricket ground resembled a paddock prepared for the
sowing of lucerne-seed, in which the ball buried itself.
Frequently the players were hidden from the spectators
and each other by clouds of dust. Willy-willies traversed
the ground, carrying skyward on one occasion the re-
porters’ notes of the match. Nevertheless, the Austra-
lians played the game, and would have given the local 18
a “bad doing” in another few minutes, had not time
been called.
* * * *
Australian cyclists are desired (by advt. in their
trade organ) to note that Mrs. Somebody has opened a
massage boudoir, “ for development of muscle, training
for speed, and removal of bodily fatigue. Special terms
for clubs.” The idea of one weak woman developing the
muscle of several clubs—and at reduced prices, too—is
rather painful.
* * * *
On one road from Sydney to Parramatta are no
fewer than four mad-houses —Callan Park, Gladesville,
Rydalmere and Parramatta. These institutions were
passed in turn by a visiting Vic. cyclist. “What!” he
yelled, “is that another of ’em? Why, the whole country
must be full of lunatics 1 ”
• * * *
Later accounts show that Corbett was knocked
out by the old-fashioned blow in the “ mark ’’—pit of the
stomach—familiar to readers of Tom and Jerry. It was
puzzling how the left could take much effect over the
heart, which is the spot for a right-hand blow—formidable,
because the heart is about the only part of the body which
the right can conveniently reach — orthodox position, of
course.


A collection of pictures, by Albert Han-
son, is on exhibition at 12 Bridge-st., Sydney.
Particulars in “ Amusement ” advt. col.*
Electricity, in its modem developments, is
finding keen competition from admirable recent
inventions for the consumption of gas. One of
these is a patent incandescent gas arc lamp, an-
nounced elsewhere by Brand Bros., of Melb. and
Sydney. A conspicuous feature of this lamp is
its lighting tube, which does away with the neces-
sity for a pilot light, being also much superior to
the latter, which deteriorates the mantle. This
lamp is cheaper by far than electric light.*

Two grovel items from Launceston (Tas.):
The City Council agreed to a special rate which will
amount to £2OO for the carrying out of celebrations by the
Mayor in connection with the Queen’s record reign. Ine
Council refused a donation towards the Maternity
Hospital.
So like a Council!
“An Irish member asked the Under-
secretary for War in the Commons about the case
of a private in the Royal Rifles sentenced to seven
days’ imprisonment for wearing a shamrock in his
cap on last St Patrick’s Day,” writes The Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent. In reply the S. for W.
said the patriotic soldier was imprisoned for dis-
obedience of orders in not removing the grass
trinity when commanded, but, he added, Lord
Wolseley had removed the register of the penalty
from the record of the son of Erin—after the seven
days were served. The questions as to what com-
pensation the victim was to get, and whether it
was not true English soldiers wore roses on St.
George’s Day, were swamped by the baa-ing of
the Govt, benches. So Private Grindle walks the
earth a melancholy man eternally moaning,
1 Ochone ! ochone ! give me back my seven days,
and the Irishmen of the Royal Rifles may be a
little doubtful of the direction of their Lee-Metfords
when next that commander leads the regiment to
the fray.”
A new and profitable Melb. industry is
dealing in ready-made houses, etc., the boss of the
business being one W. M'Lennan. He has for
sale in his store-yards ready-made wooden cot-
tages, two-storey houses, iron, tin, and wooden
churches, public halls, flour-mills, mechanics’ in-
stitutes, dance-rooms, billiard saloons, even 16-
roomed villas. The bursting of the boom and the
exodus to Westralia and Scuth Africa have left on
the hands of Melb. banks and building societies
thousands of run-em-ups of all sizes, which bring
in practically nothing, but which, if left standing,
will still have to be connected with the sewerage
system at £lO a-piece. These McLennan buys up
in terraces, even street - fulls in the outside
suburbs, and re-sells to land-owners at one-third
the cost of building. He will sell you a 5-roomed
house for <£2s,and deliver it in Ballarat for £2O or
Bendigo for £25. A 6-roomed double-fronted villa
will go at £4O, a 12-roomed 2-storey house at £l3O,
and so on. McLennan during the past two years
has removed nearly all the seaside township of
Mentone, near Mordialloc, and with it created
the new township of Deepdene on the Lilydale
line. Since the boom he has been shifting 100
houses a year from Melb. to Geelong. Bendigo
and Ballarat also now largely consist of bits of
Melbourne. Once he shifted a Kyneton hotel
half-a-inile with the bar open and in full swing.
Another time he brought a house from Ballarat to
Melbourne with the family living, eating and
sleeping in it all the three days’ journey.
Finucane, Bananaland Immigration-agent:
“The mangosteen is a fraud of frauds ! ” The
Javans must have imposed on him with ancient
crab-apples. The mangosteen is one of the most
dainty and delicate fruits in creation.
An Oscar Wilde story concerning Lady
Jeune, who is notorious for the mixedness of the
celebrities she obtains for her functions. When
Neal Creame, the wholesale street-woman poisoner
was condemned to death, some one informed Oscar
that there was a prospect of his being reprieved
“ I hope not, I sincerely hope not,” commented
Oscar, with his ineffable manner. “Why not?”
was asked. “ I should have to shake hands with
him. I should be sure to meet him at Lady
Jeune’s.” 7

AN INTERESTING NOTICE OF A MOST

MARVELLOUS TESTIMONIALS.

- - - MIRACULOUS CURES.
A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfahne Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and so!e
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice; the
very fact of their having been before the publio
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
ifor these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W.A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts «">f the earth.

Mr. Button writes later:— “The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,
when three hunters and trappers were travelling about
the ranges near my place on the Rose River, one was taken
very ill ; his mates intended taking him in a waggon to
»Vangaratta (about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor.
I gave him a few of your Pills. I am pleased to inform
you that, having to pass their camp next day, I found
him up and pegging-out his skins and looking well. This
speaks volumes for your valuable Remedies.”
