A Lady’s Troubles.
FOUND HOUSEHOLD DUTIES
FATIGUING.
WANTED TO REMAIN IN BED ALL DAY.
HAD NO AMBITION TO DO ANYTHING
AND FELT TERRIBLY LANGUID.
From the Australian Courier.
“ Up to the spring of 1890,” says Mrs. Margaret
Layfield, of 69 Surry-street, Darlinghurst, Sydney
(in answer to a reporter’s inquiry concerning her-
self), I was comparatively healthy, and could carry
out my household duties without feeling in the
slightest degree fatigued, and would enjoy a sound
and restful night’s sleep. Then I began to suffer
from weakness, and a feeling of depression. Iha
a fulness and a pain at the chest and round the sides;
I was also troubled with palpitation of the heart
and a sense of overbearing exhaustion, lost a great
deal of sleep ; in fact I kept tossing about the bed
all night, in the morning I awoke unrefreshed,
and looked at the day before me as one of positive
torture. Frequently I had to remain in bed all day,
and call in the assistance of a neighbour, being too
weak to do anything; I also had a terribly languid
feeling, Having inherited a good constitution, and
being always adverse to calling in a medical man,
I endeavoured to battle through my troubles, trust-
ing that a certain amount of determination would
throw off this terrible nausea. Exhausted nature,
however, at last gave way, and I was on the eve of
sending for a doctor, when a friend drew my atten-
tion to a remarkable cure of dyspepsia and nervous
debility effected by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People. I got some of the Pills as an experi-
ment, and at once proceeded to take them in ac-
cordance with the printed instructions. After a
few days, and before I had completed the first box,
I felt a visible improvement, my appetite returned,
and once more food agreed with me. I continued
taking the pills, and am now happy to relate that
I seem as well and strong as ever, and have suffered
no more from that terrible indigestion. I feel deeply
grateful that chance threw in my way Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills, as I tell everyone; m fact I should
consider myself a heartless woman if I should not
make this thing public, and endeavor to alleviate
the troubles of my suffering sisters.”
Dr Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People are not
a patent medicine, but are a thoroughly scientific
preparation, the result of years of careful study on
the part of an eminent Edinburgh University phy-
sician, and they were successfully used by him in
his every-day practice for many years before being
offered for general sale. They positively cure
Rheumatism, Sciatica, Lumbago, and Neuralgia,
and are a specific for all diseases of women, such
as anaemia, poor and watery blood, female irregu-
larities, nervous headache and hysteria. 1 hey
are not a purgative medicine, but brace up and
permanently strengthen the whole system.
To obtain Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills apply to
your chemist, or send to the Dr. Williams Medl-
ine Coy., Sydney, who will forward (post paid)
on receipt of stamps or post order, 1 box for
2s. 9d., or i dozen for 14s. 9d.*

“ There are but two kinds of men in the
world —Englishmen and Rebels. ” — French.
A Maoriland small boy is in trouble for
stealing some money and spending it in the joyous
recreation of having two sound teeth dragged out
without gas. It is difficult to imagine what that
boy will develop into when he grows up.
H. : ‘‘ln Hansard, for 1821, at Melb.
Public Library, I turned up the first public refer-
ence to Queen Victoria. Brougham, in the Lords,
refers to her as ‘ a little lady, now two years old,
who. may perchance be Queen of England.’ Then
the intoxication over the girl Queen ! The public
craves glorious youth in royalty, as well as in all
other sorts of play-acting, and what not. How
they did grumble about the succession of old men
—two Georges, one William—though they shouted
for them as much as for—well, no matter.”
Kee Sam, of Melbourne, recently died and
went to be with Joss-Confucius in a better Flowery
Land. It transpired at the inquest that Kee had
been treated by a Chow physician who gave him
pills, each the size of a duck’s egg. There were
said to be 40 different ingredients in the medicine*
including blood, grasshoppers, ashes, bone-dust,
clay, dates, honey, sawdust, and ground-up in-
sects—which is very Chinese, and also very
Christian. When one considers how beautiful the
Christian says his Heaven is, and the horrid
messes he takes to prevent him going there, the
spectacle of a Chinaman wrestling with a pill the
size of a moderate ink-bottle isn’t so absurd after
all. The yellow man’s heaven isn’t so beautiful as
the white man’s, so he naturally takes a larger
pill to keep out of it.

Parson Hosking, the teetotal lecturer who
alleged that Maoriland pressmen were ‘ ‘ the greatest
liars out of Sheol,” lately finished up a mission in
the Hawke’s Bay province with the statement
that it was “ the most immoral place in the
country.” The average sensational parson is
much addioted to explaining exactly how immoral
places are, while the ordinary person wonders
helplessly at the vastness of his knowledge. It
must take a deal of midnight marauding in the
bye-ways where Portwine Annie and Painted
Sarah wander abroad after dark, and much haunt-
ing of undress orgies, and a lot of stealthy linger-
ing at back gates, and lurking in many alleys of
many towns to enable any parson to say truthfully
and exactly which is the most immoral place in
any country. A certain amount of immorality
stalks abroad openly, but the bulk of it has to be
traced by getting personally on the roof of the
woodshed and looking through the window, or by
whooping in the festive halls of Portwine Annie
and Painted Sarah and playfully pulling down the
back-hair of these sirens. Therefore, the copious
information possessed by some preachers is a sus-
picious and blushful subject.
Dear Bulletin, —Re cyclones. I also can
swear that Never tire is not singular. Neither is
Bowen. In ’72 a hurricane struck Townsville fair
between the eyes. Besides other damage, it tore
the steamer, Black Prince, from her moorings and
cast her up high and dry 300yds. inland. The
skipper had both legs broken and his spine in-
jured. The hat was sent round and he was placed
in a pub., where he was alive and hearty in 77,
when I last saw him. In 74 a cyclone hit Rock-
ingham Bay, and lifting the heavy pilot whale-
boat out of the water sent it sailing through the
air like a leaf. It struck the roof of the P.M.’s
house and was picked up next day by the native
troopers at Cashmere telegraph-station in the
ranges. At Cobar, in ’9O, the roof of a large pro-
duce store was lifted off its walls and planked
down over my adjoining cottage in the middle of
the night, completely extinguishing it (the
not the night). I thought the Day
of Judgment had come. Next morning the
owner wanted to sue me for damage to his roof,
because my.chimney was sticking up through
the iron, and it took all hands and the cook to get
it off. Also at Jembaicumbene —but, enough.
Bowen and Nevertire are not alone in their
cyclonic bust-ups.-
YARRAN.

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*

Liz Kane’s Confession .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Liz Kane’s me name, in Fitzroy reared ; I was
A-keepin’ dad before I switched the bloke ;
He took me to the diggin’s just becos
A city life soon sent him stony broke.
He never acted square a day ; but mopped
Our boodle up in booze—he was a tank.
His growlin’, swearin’, drinkin’ never stopped ;
The things my mother gave me, all, he drank.
I felt it awful lonely there, alone.
I told him so: he cursed, and said “ Wot jam ! ”
I’d nothing there to love, or call my own,
So fancied that I’d tame a little lamb.
The station missus sent me one at last —
A pretty pet, with soft, kind eyes, so mild ;
[ reckoned now my lonely days were past—
I loved my lammie like a little child.
1 always feared he’d hurt my little pet,
I felt he would ! the way he’d jaw and nag—
So when he’d come I’d always “do a get ”
And plant my little darling in a bag.
One day, while I was cleaning up, inside,
My pet was planted out behind the shed ;
He, mooching there, my lammie must have spied,
He dropped a rock upon it—killed it dead.
That hopeless, bitter pain I’ll ne’er forget—
It drove me mad ; I loathed his very name.
Next day I said, “I’ve got another pet ! ”
He said, “I’ll dam soon serve it just the same.”
I saw him hunt about to find the bag :
I ran, behind a tree, up in the glen.
So long ! no more you’ll drink, and beat, and nag;
I saw him drop the rock ! . . . and then ! . . .
and then! !
The Chowesses in China might, perhaps,
Have heard that Bang 111 It all the crockery
broke!
I’d filled the bag with dynamite and caps,
The blokey went “ to blazes ” in the smoke.
The jury foreman said he was afraid
There wasn’t much before the jury chaps ;
’Twas plain the bloke was either lost or strayed,
Through foolin’ round with dynamite and caps.
I’m back, y’r rev’rence, stitching at me trade ;
I’ve had no luck in married life, that’s flat.
(I wish with dear old dad I’d always stayed) . . .
D’yu think they’ll put a girl in hell for that ?

Melb. : “Re photographer Allan’s seces-
sion from the Salvation Army. It is said his
reasons are because of the Army becoming a trad-
ing business, and because large funds are accumu-
lated while officers are starved.”
Dear Bulletin,—According to Wesleyan
parson Waddell, it is as absurd to assume that
God can’t be expected to reverse His natural laws,
to promote the well-being of man, as it is to main-
tain that, to save a passenger’s life, an engine-
driver shouldn’t be expected to stop liis train, or
even run back for medical assistance. Unhappy
illustration for Waddell and co. ! The engine-
driver might know—though the passenger couldn’t
that the stoppage or the run back might involve
everlasting smash and the sacrifice of a hundred
lives for one. The “ well-being ”of Waddell, for
anything he knows, might involve the ill-being of
somebody else ; and, if the rev. gpntleman really
believes the universe is engine-driven, it would be
only decent of him to assume that the Great
Driver understands His own business.—Pagan.
In Melbourne they have also been humili-
ating for rain, and the aggregate local piety went
to church in its best suit and its newest frock, and
its duck of a hat with the loveliest possible feather
in it, and its jewellery and its bangle, and all its
other earthly vanities. It calls this sort of thing
“humiliation,” but it certainly doesn’t look like
it. The Bulletin is still waiting to see the aggre-
gate Christianity of this country show its faith in
prayer and humiliation by humiliating in real
earnest. If the pious ladies of Toorak will leave
at home the hair they bought at the barber’s, and
put on their oldest frocks, and procesh to church
in their bare feet, without their gloves, or hats or
jewellery, and with their own hair hanging down,
they may look a trifle ridiculous, but at least they
will show that they believe in their own professed
creed—or else that they think their feet can stand
public inspection and that their hair looks nice
that way. A barefooted procession of society
ladies going to church over new road-metal should
certainly bring down any rain there is in the busi-
ness—especially if they finish up by publicly
scrubbing the front steps of the tabernacle in their
oldest clothes and with the family Bridget’s apron
on, and then take turns at confessing their sins on
the pavement in front of the building.
An Anarchist-Socialists' Celebra-
tion.
London Holborn Town Hall (writes “Minyarah"
to The Bulletin from London) filled up into the
gallery and then overflowed through side-rooms
and down staircases into the greasy, misty streets.
A weird, warped gathering. Two out of five
were women, tidily though “ East - endily ”
dressed, and silently in earnest. The men were
undersized, with sunken cheeks, and lantern jaws
overrun with scrub. Dull and sulky eyes ; matted,
uncut hair; and a felt-hat slouched over on one
side. They had met to commemorate the anni-
versary of the “Chicago Martyrs’” legal demise
in ’B7, and hear those who could put in words a
quota of the hearers’ fierce thoughts. Some nota-
bilities in the World of Bombs and Barricades
were expected to harangue : Louise Michel, Tou-
zeau Parris, Prince Kropotkin, Enrico Malatesta,
James Tochatti, and more phlegmatic lights—
Edward Carpenter, Tom Mann, John Turner,
Lothrop Withington.
* * * *
The “ Laborers’ Battle Hymn,” melodized to the
“Marseillaise,” was sung by the “choir ”of solidly-
built young women ; apologies read from allegori-
cal-artist Walter Crane and Keir Hardie. The
former said his views were unchanged ; and the
latter that he was explaining his views to the
Bradford electors—who refused them and him.
Telegrams fluttered in from the different
anarchist - communists’ “Groups”: “Hurrah for
Anarchy ! ” “ Long Live Anarchy ! ” &e. Sounds
enthusiastically soothing to the lay auditor, whose
belief is usually that if anarchy prevailed no one
would “long-live.”
The dock-laborers’ idol, Tom Mann, followed
less - known “preliminary canterers.” A big-
boned, fair-sized man with a harsh, stone-smash-
ing-machine voice intended for open-air orating,
muscularly-vigorous delivery, and savage intensity
of utterance. Frock-coat no relation to the vest,
an absolute stranger to the trousers. Loose
black hair straggles down the prominent forehead,
from under the shady verandah of which the eyes
gleam with restless fierceness. A dangerous man
under direction and restraint. Much spasmodic
gesticulation and jerky gesture. “Trades-union-
ism that doesn’t develop a rebellious spirit is use-
less”; “I am a determined opponent of all
existing institutions ” ; “Rebel I am and rebel I
intend to remain.”
* * * *
Peter Kropotkin, after five years’ imprison-
ment, shed his title of “ Prince,” and looks a most
respectable, spectacled old gentleman, who
wouldn’t kill a March fly, but would drop a
melinite bomb on the pet corn of Nicholas 11.
with pleasure. He is a very fiery fiz-gig. The
hair has fled completely from the hot exterior of
his frizzling brain-pan, and, clinging to his cease-
lessly wagged chin, spreads itself out in a bushy
fan o’er his lucifer-box chest. His head bobs
like a China mandarin’s as he jerks out with a
thick foreign accent “hire-rid ass-sass'ins ” and
“ mass-sackers. ” He abruptly spun on one leg
and disappeared, letting on the Oxford man,
Edward Carpenter.
The educated anarchist or socialist wore a cheap
grey tweed sac, flannel-collared shirt, and the first
bit of jewellery visible on the platform—a steel
guard. A “gentlemanly,” halting, partly-stutter-
ing speaker, he told the crowd in a semi-conversa
tional way that the cheapening of food, progress
to and fro, education, &c., made wealth of lessen-
ing value daily, and in time it would have none.
This startingly-logical argument was too fine for
the audience, wh > listened in non-committal, com-
plimentary si leu v. Though a “swell,” he was
one of them ; and that was enough.
(Types. A bizarre object under an immense tam-
o’-shanter, like a plum-pudding cloth flattened out
after the contents were partly boiled away. Long
hair curling up in horror from his greasy-glazed
coat-collar. A pugnacious, stubbly moustache, and
a tussocky goatee that began at the under lip and
ended on the apple of his throat. There was a set
purpose of soulful desire in his eye which the blood
of a crowned head or the froth on a “ pint of four-
’arf ” might equally have appeased. No. 2
a ponderous, frock-coated, fat-faced German,
with thick matted hair suggesting Stanley’s
fallen African forest with its strange life crawling
and living in the interlaced undergrowth of for-
midable chaotic darkness. A “ plendy lager;
plendy pipe-smoke ” stamp of contentment on the
solemnly-listening face.)
* * * *
Louise Michel, an old woman whose like I have
seen selling apples at a street-stall, polishing up
the pippins with a ragged cloth and the moisture
of a warm breath. A black gown lay flat on the
curveless breast where hung a pince-nez. A
handkerchief protruded from the right-hand side-
pocket of her skirt. Thin, claw-like hands ; a
shrunken-skinned, scraggy neck threw forward a
hard, bony-featured, vulture face. On the back
of the dark hair a black, plate-shaped straw hat
was balanced as this matriarchal relic of the
“New Woman’ spoke her words in her own
tongue. Sana fire, sans energy, sans pantomimic
gesture, it might have been Sanscrit for all the audi-
ence knew, but they received her enthusiastically.
The nearest approach to the World’s Federation is
in the anarchist-socialists’ranks. Fourteen years
imprisonment had not broken the tough courage
of Louise Michel, and she was just back from a
leugthy tour for the cause in the United States.
Kropotkin gave an interpretation of her perora-
tion, and the listeners settled down into restful
bewilderment. From this state they were dis-
turbed by the announcement that the collection
total would not pay the meeting’s expenses, and a
second attempt would be made, during which the
“choir” would (appropriately) sing the “Com-
rades’ Song of Hope.”
* * * •
The unbiased observer would sum up the anar-
chist-socialists from this celebration as an ill-
assorted bundle of aimless efforts, resultless hopes,
and confused strivings after anything a,"' 1 no-
thing.

am
Out Back.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We dumped our swags by the river-side when the
sun was getting low ;
To reach the boys on the other side we had four
good miles to go ;
But the winding stream that before us stretched
ran sluggishly and wide—
Twas a hundred yards from where we sat to the
sand on the other side.
The tailings washed from the claims above had
sullied the waters clear,
And thicker and thicker they slowly ran as year
succeeded year ;
My mate and myself wished heartily we had
reached the further shore —
The trip was the first he had made on the track,
but I “ had been there before.”
A raw new-chum of the boasting type, in his
stockings six feet two, „
He never tired while he “held the flute or
telling what he could do—
He had pulled a win with an Oxford eight; had
hunted on Yorkshire side ;
Had played with the Gents on the Oval, too ; and
Lord knows what beside.
“ Had never been taken in, bai Jove ! he was
“ far too smart, you know ”
Was a lot too good to carry a swag, and plainly
told me so.
A fortnight back I had picked him up, stone-broke
on the Sydney side, _
And we swagged it out to fossick a creek 1 knew
by this river wide.
“I think I’ll swim it, bai Jove !” he said, and
glanced at the opposite side ;
“I’ve swum the Rhine by the Drachenfels, and
that is tour times as wide. ”
Then he stacked his togs—they were few, Lord
knows !—on the log beside me there,
And turned to dive in the murky stream with a
proud heroic air.
“ I wouldn’t dive, for fear of the rocks, I
grinned as I said to him,
“ But just walk out till you get your depth, and
then you can safely swim. ’ ’
“ A good idea, old chap,” says he, “ for the water
is beastly thick ”
And then he stepped in the sluggish stream, and
stepped at the double-quick.
The first step covered his toes, I think ; the next
he was ankle deep ; .
While I struggled hard with a laughing-fit m
trying my peace to keep.
Then, bending low, he prepared to swim when the
water reached his knees, ,
With arms drawn up and his unkempt hair adrift
on the evening breeze.
Ten paces out—and the water still just reached to
his ankles bare,
While he went prepared for the sudden drop in
the depths that he knew were there.
Half-way across —and his ankles trim still fathomed
the mighty flood ; .
He threw a suspicious glance behind, and just
for a moment stood.
Then on he went with a cautious stride, while
around his spacious feet
The waters mingled with drifting sand and the
three-inch wavelets beat.
He faced about on the other shore—he found he
was fairly “had,”
And the words that fell from that naked man
were the sublimate of bad.
Then to wade across I tucked my pants they
were getting for wear the worse,
While my robeless mate who had swum the Khine
swore hard at the universe.
He cursed the stream and cursed the sand as he
fiercely paced the shore —
’Twas the first gay time he had crossed that creek,
but I “had been there before.”
Dundas (Tas.).
P. P. QUINN.

H. : ‘“ln Sight of St. Paul’s ’ sets me
roaming in theology. The Cathedrals of Peter
and Paul, Rome and London, signalise the pat-
tern apostles of the Catholics and Protestants.
Observe that in Catholic theology there is a
studied depreciation of St. Paul, as m t rotest-
ant of St. Peter. A Catholic grievance against
the Bible is the ignominious way Peter is dropped
out of the Acts, when Paul takes up the running.
Whether or not Peter ever was in Rome, apparently
Paul was the founder of the Church there. Paul,
in the Catholic theology, is like Christ in the
Mahometan.”
The path of Our Sovereign Lady’s Jubilee
festival is being paved with affecting little anec-
dotes of the most artless character. For instance,
a choir-boy was summoned to the royal presence
to sing a solo, and he went away full of the fact
that Princess Beatrice’s offspring addressed V.R.
as “ Granny.” The choir-boy’s experience is cited
in support of the glorious allegation that Her
Gracious Majesty is fond of children, and does
not seem appalling to those who are too young to
know any better. The Queen is called Granny by
her grand-children. Let us tighten our silken
bonds and whoop for the Hempire !
The man charged at Christchurch (M.L.)
on his own confession with criminally assaulting
and murdering a young lady whom he met by
accident on the road, is thus described :
Quite a young-looking man, and gives his age as 20.
He is about sft. llin. in height, with longish arms and
legs. He stoops slightly when walking, and his chest is
somewhat contracted. He is fair, and has a very slight
moustache and whiskers. His neck is very much swollen
from goitre. His mouth is large and drooping, with
rather thick lips. He has small, blue, furtive-looking
eyes, with which he appeared to watch every movement.
His nose is large, as are also his ears, which stand out
prominently. He appeared to be quite unconcerned, and
apparently did not seem to understand his position.
Coming down in the train he smoked cigarettes and
chatted with his guards, and he did not take the slightest
notice of the crowds which had gathered at the various
stations anxious to get a glimpse of him. When arrested
the prisoner wore a hat which was too large for him, and
inside the lining was found a copy of a newspaper con-
taining an account of the Petone murders.
The long arms and the large, glaring ears and the
slouching mouth and the air of complete unconcern
are part of the description of most notable crimi-
nals. Either they are characteristics, or else the
ears grow larger and the arms longer in the police-
man’s imagination from the moment of the arrest.
The daily paper report of almost every sensational
murderer reads like an account of the arrest of an
ape of the most aggravated order.
Pannikin and Hand-out.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Editor Bulletin, — We whalers must have a
Federal Convention to settle the question of what
is a fair thing to expect at sundown, when on the
The curious fact is that meat is the most difficult
article to obtain, except from the cooks. The
various rations are :
1. Flour alone : one pint, top shaken off. Why
it is called rations instead of ration I do not know.
At a Scotchman’s station, called the Almighty
Fountains, they will not give a traveller more than
the pint even if there is a flood all round and the
swaggy cannot move. Their plea is, We give
tea and sugar, and if one gets rations twice the
others expect it.” But they don t get a £lOOO
shout of rain from great Nature every day.
2. Flour and meat : at about one station in five.
3. Meat alone : very rare unless they are killing
worn-out rams or bulls. ,
4. Flour, tea, sugar, and meat : this is so rare
that the travellers’ huts are always mortgaged
th re ti deep.
5. Nothing on any terms : this is much more
satisfactory than—
6. A pint of flour, if you cut wood for the Oflow
or jap. cook. Fancy a Scotchman, pretending to
the title of “ The,” such as “ The Buck of Kin-
buck,” asking a footsore traveller to chop wood
after a 15-mile journey !
Things being as above, we have to work the
“Hand-out,” a “piece” given at all wayside
farms (where they have it) and at the back-doors
in towns. Beware of the large parcel in news-
paper ! It generally contains crusts and bones.
The best dole is given by Irishwomen from the
fist; one can excuse a little stain or two on the
fingers of “ a cheerful giver.”
By far the most comfortable place of call is at
big cookies, who have sheep. They like to treat
you better than the neighboring squatters, and
the girls are always good. Cookies who go in
for wheat are not so liberal. I don’t know why.
Now, it is uniformity that we want. What, is
the use of starvation to-day, feeding on mulberries
and green plums, and plenty to-morrow ?
I have not mentioned the cook’s poke. Where
a cook is “good,” i.e., a thief, one gets the where-
withal to weather out a day or two. The bosses
must know that the slushies stand to the swaggies.
Why don’t they sanction it ?
I have just met two old chums working on a
station. They say the only chance now for a
decent job is to tramp to Melbourne or Sydney
and get sent up from a registry-office there. This
has always been the Adelaide style, and we now
give fair warning that at the grape-picking
federal convention it will be proposed that a com-
bined attack be made on the cities, just as the
Bureau-men have shunted us on the wallaby.
Something must be done after the fruit and grapes
fail to support us. There is no burr-cutting now-
a-days, and no rabbiters’ camps. Road parties are
always brokers ; sheep-camps have disappeared
improved off by the railways ; surveyors do all
their little work from their homes; tanks have
been finished for years ; the remains of the camps
alone are seen. What shall we do ? Coxeyise ?
Jump the rattler to town ? Give ourselves up as
destitute ?or continue to tap the cook ? This is
the question of the night.
SCOTTY THE WRINKLER.
Apropos bearded women, crowing hens
&c. Mr. R. Gildea kept for five years at Bos-an’
dillon, on the Lachlan (N.S.W.), a ewe with
horns as big and curly as a ram’s, the ewe shane
being otherwise perfect. A big joke on the station
was to watch every new hand, whenever the ewes
were yarded, rush in haste to haul her out as a
stag. Like most female animals, including the
human, which ape the male, the ewe was sterile
“Some ghastly objects are now in the
possession of Major-General Robley, a ’64 Maori
War veteran,” writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent. “They are 16 embalmed Maori
heads, more or less seamed and scrolled with tat-
tooing. Though it is over 60 years since they were
cut from the bodies of the slain warriors, they are
in perfect preservation, and with their stitched-up
eyelids, from which the eyes have been gouged
out and eaten, the regular, white, grinning teeth
and haliotus-shell plugged nostrils, they form a
gruesome spectacle.

hind-legs re the probability of Jap-exelusion from
Australia :
Even should the British Govt, permit such a thin?
Japan can easily avenge herself by shutting her doors in
turn against Australians.
Well, even if Australians cared a tin-tack about
the British Govt., they’re not—with perhaps the
exception of Attorney-General Want particu-
larly anxious to go to Japan ; whereas the Japs
are eager to come to Australia—and to stay I
Which is the pinching-point of the shoe.
Dear Bulletin, —Re relations between
advertisers’ interests and writers’, “Large Adver-
tiser ” has simply chopped out the arc that suits.
him from the following circle of interdependence.
Large circulation, plenty of advts., big income,
good pay, effective writing, large circulation ; and
so round again. Effective writing, of course, isn’t
the only factor in large circulation, but it’s such
an important one that the writer is no more be-
holden for his increased pay to the advertiser than
the advertiser is for his increased publicity (which
is his increased pay) to the— Scribbler.
Dear Bulletin, —Some idea of the present
state of Melbourne can be gained from the fact
that I have, within this month of May, counted
from the dummies of the trams as they sped along
the following lines the number of shops and houses
“ to let ” as attached, suites of offices offered for
rental in the city being excluded:—North Mel-
bourne, 59 ; Brunswick, 33; South Melbourne, 54;
Prahran, 72; St. Kilda, 45; Port Melbourne, 48;
North Carlton, 47. Evidently the laundry busi-
ness is overdone, for I have already seen four or
five shops, on which the word “ laundry 1 ” is still
painted, showing also the “to let ” placard. No
doubt if one left the trams and penetrated into
the other thoroughfares, the tale of empty shops
and houses in each suburb could be marvellously
increased. It would not be a bad idea to illuminate
Melbourne during Jubilee time by burning down
a thousand or so of the unsightly wooden cottages
and villas which abound in its environs.—Yours,
&c., C.R.W.
A Record for a Sovereign.— Hordern’s
in Pitt-street sovereign blankets break a record
for sterling value, and the fact that they are for-
warded without extra cost to any town in New
South Wales should induce residents in the
country districts of New South Wales to send for
a pair.—Hordern Brothers, Government Con-
tractors, 203, 205, 207, 209, 211, Pitt-street (only)
Sydney. Telegraphic address simply : “ Hordern
Brothers. ”
